Agroforestry policy issues and research directions in the 
US and less developed countries: insights and challenges 
from recent experience 
L. E. BUCK 
CIIFADICIFOR, Department of Natural Resources, Fernow Hall, Cornell University, Ithaca, 
NY 14850, USA 
Key words: participatory research, policy research, strategic research 
Abstract. Efforts to improve the performance of agroforestry systems, and to expand the land 
area and number of people able to benefit from this integrative approach to agriculture and 
natural resource management, are constrained throughout the world by non-supportive land use 
policies. A growing sense of urgency that policy change is needed to enable agroforestry to 
flourish has contributed during the past two years to an unprecedented level of agroforestry 
policy assessment and planning activity. 
In the US, agroforestry has emerged from academia, where it has incubated since the 
mid-1980s, into the professional resource management arena. A multi-organizational agroforestry 
evaluation process has driven national policy and program formation to the forefront of the 
agenda of the agroforestry community, as it seeks to influence the 1995 Farm Bill. 
Internationally, the Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research and collabora-
tors fostered a sequence of policy issue identification activities as a basis for setting strategic 
research priorities for forestry and agroforestry. 
Following a brief review of forces driving agroforestry development in industrialized and 
less developed countries, the paper highlights recent policy assessment initiatives in each sphere. 
Observations on the issues driving and the priorities emerging from these processes are offered, 
to lend perspective to the critical challenges facing the agroforestry policy research commu-
nity. An explanation for pervasive constraints and inconsistencies in policy effectiveness is 
then explored, from which a promising approach to research intervention is forwarded. 
It is argued that social scientists might influence agroforestry policy most favorably at this 
critical juncture, as perceptions of inter-dependence increase among different stakeholders in 
the policy system, by employing interventionist, actor-oriented perspectives and participatory 
methods to facilitate policy innovation and evaluation. The approach is consistent with 
participatory technology design processes that earlier helped to establish agroforestry as a 
prototype for sustainable development. 
Introduction 
Agroforestry in tropical and sub-tropical regions has been recognized by the 
international scientific community for almost 20 years, while scientific 
engagement with temperate zone agroforestry became evident about a decade 
later. During this time agroforestry practice in the South has proven its ability 
to address some of the region's pressing land use problems, while the science 
has become a strategic integrating discipline that cuts across the boundaries 
of traditional land use disciplines [Nair, 1993]. The expansion of agroforestry

--- Page 62 ---

58 
activity in industrialized country settings is more recent and the pace of 
development slower. Evidence suggests, however, that the temperate zone 
branch of the field may soon catch up. 
Recently, the agroforestry community in both spheres has noticeably broad-
ened its focus of attention from technology to policy dimensions of agro-
forestry development and research. Reasons differ between the two regions 
as a function of the stage of development of agroforestry and associated 
expectations for its advancement. There is universally growing appreciation, 
however, that agroforestry as a prototype of sustainable development is 
constrained by policies and institutions that were codified to support more 
conventional, industrial models of agriculture, forestry and rural development. 
To realize the potential which bio-technical and socio-economic research on 
agroforestry suggests is possible for enhancing agricultural productivity, while 
conserving natural resources and protecting the environment, policy con-
straints must be removed and replaced by a consistent constellation of incen-
tives [Kidd and Pimentel, 1992]. 
The impetus for this growing sentiment is explored in this paper, together 
with the recent assessment and planning activity that it has spawned; and 
implications for agroforestry policy research. First, however, the driving forces 
behind agroforestry development in less developed and industrialized coun-
tries are briefly reviewed to help differentiate agroforestry in the two domains, 
and to appreciate how these factors affect perceived policy needs. 
Driving forces in agroforestry development 
Several authors have compared the emergence and application of agroforestry 
in developing and developed country contexts [Ehrenreich et aI., 1994; Long 
and Nair, 1991; Lassoie and Buck, 1991]. Key issues that motivate the 
agroforestry communities in these overlapping spheres to seek the benefits 
of integrating trees, annual crops and livestock in complex, perennial-based 
production systems are summarized below. The US is used as an example of 
an industrialized country. 
Less developed countries 
Poverty alleviation and rural welfare was the leading justification for invest-
ment in agroforestry by the international aid community throughout most of 
the 1980s. By integrating selected trees back into rural systems, burdens faced 
by the poor in meeting their requirements for forest products from marginal 
and degraded areas could be reduced, often while improving soil conditions 
and plant productivity. 
Throughout much of the South, agriculture remains the primary economic 
sector in terms of GDP, the main source of employment and often the 
principal source of foreign exchange. Thus, maintaining the quality of the

--- Page 63 ---

59 
natural resource base upon which commercial agricultural productivity 
depends is a critical priority for many countries. Agroforestry can play an 
important resource conserving role for this purpose. 
As land is cleared by expanding populations for agriculture, environmen-
tally unique or sensitive areas come under increasing threat. International 
organizations invest in agroforestry to help buffer threatened areas from the 
impact of agricultural encroachment. The influence of the environmental 
agenda on key development sectors has increased appreciably in recent years. 
While in the past this influence was discernable almost exclusively through 
NGOs, bi-lateral and international aid programs are becoming more visibly 
focused on the agricultural- environmental nexus [World Bank, 1994; World 
Resources Institute, 1992]. 
While the foregoing distinctions are of some consequence in assessing 
policy impact and planning interventions, agroforestry is widely appreciated 
for its capacity to perform any or all of these functions, depending on how 
technologies are designed and applied. Similarly, it is becoming less fruitful 
to differentiate among agroforestry research, extension and development 
activities. The participatory, systems approach commonly employed in 
technology development, utilizing the 'Diagnosis and Design' methodology, 
has essential features of each, which can be heightened or diminished 
depending on the objectives and capabilities of the implementing organiza-
tion [Raintree, 1990; Buck, 1990].1 In sum, investment in one domain of 
agroforestry is likely to bear fruit in others. 
United States 
In the United States the rediscovery of traditional agroforestry, and efforts to 
develop new practices, stem from concern about the persistence and high 
cost of natural resource degradation associated with industrialized agriculture. 
Public pressure is growing to limit soil erosion and water contamination and 
to restore wildlife habitat. This, together with changes in rural economics 
and demographics associated with the on-going restructuring of agriculture, 
as well as growing national and international markets for high quality forest 
products, are combining to influence land use decisions in new ways [Garrett 
and Buck, 1994]. Notably in these countries, the influence of agriculture on 
the national economy continues to shrink in terms of GOP, employment and 
export earnings. 
Recent moves to make greater use of agroforestry practices in temperate 
agriculture have been driven by the proven or perceived ability of agroforestry 
to meet the following needs: i) product diversification, ii) environmental 
impact mitigation, iii) land rehabilitation, iv) land use conversion (from annual 
to timber crops), v) increased or decreased food production, vi) sustainable 
use or retirement of marginal or fragile land, vii) habitat enhancement, and 
viii) aesthetic appreciation. 
Less urgency is associated with the agroforestry movement in industrial-

--- Page 64 ---

60 
ized countries than in less developed ones, as problems amenable to this 
approach are not viewed to be as threatening to lives or livelihoods. 
Appreciation of climatic differences and associated growing conditions also 
limits expectations for the immediacy of problem relief in temperate envi-
ronments. It is often argued that agroforestry is unlikely to make as signifi-
cant and immediate a difference to the land and lives of the North as it can 
in the South. 
Nonetheless, the benefits appear real and important to growing numbers 
concerned with rural vitality, environmental protection and resource conser-
vation. From a global perspective, agroforestry is seen to have a role in the 
North's transition to sustainable development as it strives to meet its 
environmental and social responsibilities to the South. As such justifications 
mount, the 'relevance distinction' between temperate and tropical agroforestry 
may be less 'relevant' in the future than it has been in the past. 
Agroforestry policy 
Policy has been defined as any governing principle, plan or course of action 
[Webster, 1988]. Examining policy from this broad perspective, it is not 
surprising that the domain of agroforestry policy seems unwieldy and subject 
to a variety of interpretations. Indeed, scholars of government and political 
science attest to the numerous schemes and perspectives that have emerged 
to study and understand the complex concept of public policy (Uphoff, pers. 
comm.). 
To address the question of how policy affects agroforestry through a par-
ticular theoretical construct, thus, is unlikely to be productive. Instead, atten-
tion is focused on issues arising from the community of researchers, agency 
specialists, program and project managers, local organizations and donors 
concerned with advancing effective agroforestry practice. What do they 
perceive as key policy issues affecting the development and spread of 
agroforestry land use? I shall draw from recent organized efforts to elicit 
such views. 
Policy assessment initiatives 
During the past two years there has been a noticeable surge of activity within 
the overlapping agroforestry communities, North and South, to assess the role 
of policy in realizing the potential agroforestry has for sustainable develop-
ment. A host of interrelated meetings, workshops and conferences in each 
sphere reveal strong convictions about the significant unmet potential of 
agroforestry to address mounting ecological and economic crises worldwide, 
and the important role of policy support in unleashing it. Some of the most 
visible and potentially influential of these initiatives are identified below.2

--- Page 65 ---

61 
Less developed countries 
Between 1992-1994, the Consultative Group on International Agricultural 
Research (CGIAR), in collaboration with the International Union of Forestry 
Research Organizations (lUFRO) and the Food and Agriculture Organization 
(FAO) sponsored a series of policy research workshops throughout the 
developing world. The aim was to better understand forestry and agroforestry 
problem areas that could benefit from strategic policy research. Views from 
several hundred policy leaders and scientists were elicited. Background 
materials for the workshops identified agroforestry policy issues from a variety 
of different perspectives [Gregersen et aI., 1992]. A synthesis report was 
developed to help focus and coordinate the efforts of key centers and their 
partners, especially the International Center for Research in Agroforestry 
(ICRAF), the Center for International Forestry Research (CIFOR) and the 
International Food Policy Research Institute (IFPRI), to conduct strategic 
policy research in the closely interrelated forestry and agroforestry domains 
[CIFOR, 1994]. 
Among the initiatives this activity spawned was a research methodology 
workshop in 1994 on Policies for Local Organizations in Natural Resources 
Management [lFPRI et aI., 1994]. Social scientists from policy research 
organizations throughout the world met to consider research priorities and 
methodologies for improving policy support to local organizations concerned 
with agroforestry and related land use systems. 
A complementary workshop series was held in 1993-1994, bringing 
together members of the CGIAR Centers and the North American university 
research system, with selected National Agriculture Research Systems (NARS) 
and foundations to formulate the Global Research on the Environmental and 
Agriculture Nexus (GREAN) initiative [Cornell University and University of 
Florida, 1994]. The GREAN initiative aims to generate a second Green 
Revolution, to create livelihood and food security for the poorest people in 
the developing world on an environmentally sustainable basis. It calls for 
political will in developed and developing countries alike, to ensure that 
sufficient resources are applied to global research on reconciling sustainability 
with productivity growth. Predictably, there is a role for agroforestry in the 
proposed $ 100 million strategy for research collaboration. 
United States 
In 1993-1994 the USDA Soil Conservation Service (SCS), recently named 
the Natural Resources Conservation Service (NRCS), commissioned a com-
prehensive assessment of US agroforestry as a component of the Resource 
Conservation Act (RCA) appraisal [Garrett et aI., 1994]. It was the first 
organized effort in the US to systematically review the scope of agroforestry 
activity and to assess its potential for further development. The report was 
researched and prepared by a select group of comparatively long-standing 
members of the agroforestry research community. It presents a vision that

--- Page 66 ---

62 
links land use policy and practice in the US to global concerns for meeting 
basic human needs and containing ecological disaster. 
Following a positive reception of the RCA report by the United States 
Forest Service (USFS), Cooperative Extension Service (CES) and other 
concerned agencies, a multi-organizational initiative was launched to develop 
a framework for agroforestry development in the US [AFfA, 1994]. A con-
sultation and workshop process involving numerous new and known actors 
in the agroforestry community resulted in a widely distributed 'white paper' 
for use in formulating national level agroforestry policy proposals. The 
circulation of this report throughout land grant universities, and to numerous 
government agencies, societies and membership groups has noticeably height-
ened awareness and interest in agroforestry activity (AFfA Steering 
Committee, pers. comm.). 
In association with the foregoing activities, the visibility of the Association 
for Temperate Agroforestry (AFfA) has increased, as has the membership of 
this growing network organization.3 Late in 1994 the organization facilitated 
the development of an agroforestry action team, an ad hoc committee charged 
with formulating an agroforestry policy, strategy and action plan for the 1995 
Farm Bill. The action team is comprised of eleven politically astute members 
with diverse backgrounds and a common interest in agroforestry. It met in 
December, 1994 to distill information and priorities from the foregoing 
sources, and draft a succinct agroforestry policy statement to help influence 
members of Congress to introduce or support a package of measures aimed 
at advancing agroforestry. 
Driving forces in agroforestry policy evaluation 
It is not justified to compare agroforestry policy issues and priorities arising 
from the foregoing sets of activities on a uniform set of criteria. Aside from 
a common purpose to elicit resources for agroforestry research and develop-
ment, their foci on agroforestry policy stem from different needs and concerns. 
Instead, the author's observations from participation in the international and 
the US activities described, offer different perspectives to draw from in 
evaluating the issue of agroforestry policy needs and research priorities. 
Less developed countries 
Three key issues appear to be driving the current international focus on 
agroforestry policy. First, limitations of project approaches to agroforestry 
development have been widely encountered throughout the South. Within the 
comparatively artificial system of incentives that it is possible to create in a 
project environment, efforts to influence institutional and farmer behavior in 
favor of agroforestry have often been rewarded. Attempts to ensure the 
sustainability of project activities and impacts following the withdrawal of 
external support, however, have repeatedly failed [Annis, 1988]. Similarly,

--- Page 67 ---

63 
strategies to 'scale-up' agroforestry project successes in order to benefit wider 
regions from experience gained, have often been unsuccessful [Josiah and 
Gregersen, 1994]. 
Second, once local organizations became widely appreciated as effective 
intermediaries for agroforestry development [Buck, 1990; Korten, 1990; 
Esman and Uphoff, 1984], obstacles began to surface in efforts to reproduce 
the innovative learning processes that made external interaction with certain 
local groups initially so rewarding. It was discovered that non-supportive 
regulations, administrative leader opinion, competing socio-cultural norms 
and financial disincentives often combined to limit tendencies for local group 
formation, action and networking. 
Third, the scope and persistence of policy constraints to agroforestry land 
use are pervasive. They include insufficient institutional support, non-con-
ducive systems of land and resource tenure, gender biases in development 
priorities, and the absence of sufficient economic incentives for tree growing. 
The latter are often due to policy distortions in the form of subsidies for 
substitutes. 
Mounting frustration with these structural constraints has reinforced the 
need to address them at their roots. The agroforestry policy environment, 
however, is complex, subject to various perspectives and interpretations, and 
uneven with respect to power relations. Thus, expanded investment is war-
ranted in strategically designed research to generate appropriate knowledge 
needed to penetrate these barriers and help induce the necessary change. 
United States 
The RCA assessment of US agroforestry was commissioned as part of a 
broader effort to evaluate the effectiveness of provisions in the Conservation 
Title of the 1985 and 1990 Farm Bills [Food Security Act of 1985; Food, 
Agriculture, Conservation and Trade Act of 1990]. With the benefit of the 
study, the construction of the 1995 Farm Bill provided a critical legislative 
opportunity to design a comprehensive constellation of measures aimed at 
integrating the research, technical assistance and training activities needed to 
advance agroforestry efficiently. 
A widely recognized need to reform the agriculture sector's costly 
Conservation Reserve Program (CRP) [Soil and Water Conservation Society, 
1994] provided an immediate impetus to invoke agroforestry. Groups of 
professional resource managers, politicians, farmers and environmentalists in 
the State of Missouri, for example, have become uniquely united through a 
common view that agroforestry holds promise for eliciting greater farmer 
enrollment, on a more 'voluntary' basis, to establish more permanent con-
servation practices. This is mainly a function of agroforestry's comparative 
economic advantages over more commonly employed farmland conservation 
practices (H.E. Garrett, pers. comm.). In Missouri, the capacity of black walnut 
(Jug/ans nigra) intercropping systems to produce marketable forage, nuts

--- Page 68 ---

64 
and high value timber in the short, medium and long term respectively, limits 
an operator's reliance on public subsidies in choosing a conservation-oriented 
land use practice. 
At a broader level, the US agroforestry policy initiative has been fueled 
by sporadic and unevenly distributed, but noticeable changes in the country's 
dominant system of resource management values. These foster the separa-
tion of land and resources among uses, and pose rigid distinctions between 
public and private resource ownership [Jacobs and Harper, 1993]. While the 
tradition of segregation prevails upon our rural and urban landscapes, and 
the specialized institutions that support it, public sentiment is growing for 
more integrated, ecologically sound approaches. 
International policy research priorities 
Much of the recent focus on agroforestry policy research in developing 
countries is interrelated with forest policy issues. Of the following five main 
problem areas identified through the CGIAR-sponsored workshop series, the 
first four provide scope for priority-level, agtoforestry policy research: i) the 
negative effects of tropical deforestation; ii) undervaluation of forest benefits; 
iii) insufficient knowledge and understanding of the effects of institutional 
reforms aimed at ensuring greater local participation in the policy dialogue 
and in the conservation and management of forest resources; iv) insufficient 
knowledge and understanding about the effect of policies aimed at improving 
the role of trees in rural welfare; and v) inefficiency and unprofitability of 
forest industries, and insufficient intensity and productivity of forest man-
agement. 
Of 21 priority research topics falling within the four problem areas of 
interest, 15 concern agroforestry directly, as indicated by their assignment 
through this process to ICRAF's research domain. The majority of these fall 
within the mandates, also, of CIFOR or IFPRI, while certain topics associ-
ated more directly with these two centers are linked, in turn, with agroforestry. 
Problems iii and iv, above, are most centrally concerned with agroforestry. 
Distinct from the others, these statements indicate that policy reform efforts 
are evident, but the effects are not well understood or appreciated. Eight of 
the 15 agroforestry research priority areas are associated with institutional 
reform, and roles-of-trees-in-rural-welfare. 
Space precludes an elaboration of the numerous statements of relationship 
that bear examination within the four agroforestry-relevant problem areas.4 It 
is instructive, however, to develop an impression of the phenomena and 
categories of variables that are encompassed in the high priority policy 
research profile: 
macroeconomic and sectoral policy linkages and interactions . . . incen-
tives for deforestation and tree/forest management ... agricultural policies 
... population migration ... forest department practices ... slash and burn 
farming . . . forest buffer zones ... land tenureltree ownership . . . costs

--- Page 69 ---

65 
and benefits of tree/forest goods and services ... externality benefits of 
trees ... value of non-traditional forest products ... tree product markets 
· .. carbon emissions/carbon sequestration value of trees ... North/South 
concessional transfers ... household decisions about tree management 
· .. local organizations, agroforestry adoption ... local participation . . . 
human welfare ... institutional change ... tree and forest productivity 
· .. soil and water protection ... risk reduction ... rural income genera-
tion ... nutrition ... food security ... traditional agroforestry systems 
· .. household agroforestry needs ... degraded land reclamation ... agro-
nomic zoning. 
A similar constellation of factors, bearing urgent research attention, 
emerged from the Workshop on Policies for Local Organizations in Natural 
Resources Management, albeit with reference to this more specific domain 
of action. Finally, the GREAN initiative stresses that policy and institutional 
analysis should be incorporated into each of its four priority research 
programs, e.g., i) enhanced productivity, food security and nutrition, ii) soils, 
water, forests and fisheries, iii) conservation of biological diversity, and iv) 
coping with an uncertain and fluctuating climate. While GREAN's scope is 
much broader than that of agroforestry alone, the roots of the two concepts 
in conservation and sustainable development are consistent and overlapping. 
In sum, while an emerging agroforestry research focus on policy is laudable, 
the magnitude of the need represented by these statements of priority presents 
a daunting challenge to an international policy research community which 
itself professes to be woefully underdeveloped.5 Indeed, an overriding theme 
of each workshop concerned the need to overcome severe weaknesses in 
current forest and agroforestry policy research methodology, as well as 
national institutional capacity for policy analysis. 
United States policy development priorities 
In the US experience presented, policy research was employed to design a 
set of coordinated policy recommendations for advancing the development 
of agroforestry. While the SCS-commissioned study was exploratory and 
descriptive in its approach, it was an important first step in mapping out the 
programmatic and institutional domain for agroforestry in order to locate 
strategic intervention opportunities. An underlying objective of the policy 
evaluation team, and the broader-based agroforestry planning group, was to 
prevent perceptions from developing that agroforestry is the domain of a 
particular sector or activity, thus undermining its value as an integrating 
concept. 
The strategy focusses on the need to get agroforestry 'on the ground', 
so the feasibility and benefits of respective systems become more widely 
recognized and appreciated [AFTA, 1994]. The action team's policy recom-
mendations identify specific institutional niches where agroforestry is likely 
to gain a foothold at an early stage. These include land retirement programs,

--- Page 70 ---

66 
specifically the Conservation Reserve Program (CRP), the Wetlands Reserve 
Program (WRP) and others, as well as private forest improvement programs, 
including the Stewardship Incentive Program (SIP), the Forest Improvement 
Program (PIP) and others. More than ten federally supported program areas 
are identified as currently or potentially supportive of agroforestry activity. 
The strategy emphasizes the need for technology development research, to 
hasten the emergence and spread of innovative agroforestry practices. 
The land grant system of agricultural colleges and universities is consid-
ered well suited to assume scientific leadership for agroforestry technology 
development, despite the widely discussed need for operational reforms 
[Lassoie and Buck, 1991]. A needs-driven, competitive, open grants program 
that encourages multi-disciplinary teamwork and partnership (among scien-
tists, resource management agencies, membership groups, landowner associ-
ations, and farmers) constitutes the major operational vehicle and requirement 
for funds within the overall strategy. Furthermore, the program would be 
most effective if region-specific, and responsive to diverse socio-ecological 
conditions throughout the country. The success of each region's efforts and 
their contributions to agroforestry development nationally, thus will depend 
on effective coordination within and between the consortia of stakeholders 
that are emergent in several regions already. 
Predictably, a key issue facing the agroforestry policy action team concerns 
where, specifically, within the array of candidate institutions and programs 
to center agroforestry. Each possibility must be considered in terms of its 
political implications for passage of agroforestry support measures within 
Congress, as well as that organization's legacy and future prospects for 
assuming the networking and power-sharing roles that will be vital to the long 
term success of agroforestry. The focus of the current debate concerns whether 
to broaden the mandate and geographic scope of the USFS's region-specific 
agroforestry center, to play a national coordinating role, or to lodge this 
function with the more agriculturally-oriented NRCS. 
Policy research can play an important role in evaluating the potential 
effectiveness of respective choices. It can also be used to evaluate and help 
minimize the inevitable transaction costs of coordination and collaboration. 
Once agroforestry begins to develop the momentum that is hoped for, further 
challenges will arise to guide the emergence of innovative, institutional con-
figurations that can effectively link the relevant public and private spheres of 
endeavor. 
As in the arena of agroforestry technology development, there is much to 
be learned from nearly two decades of experience in less developed countries. 
In order to draw relevant lessons from this experience, however, and to con-
structively tackle the priority policy research lists that are emerging within 
this sphere, it is instructive to develop a common perspective concerning key 
causes of agroforestry policy failure.

--- Page 71 ---

67 
Why is policy failing agroforestry? 
Ultimately, most agroforestry practice stems from household decisions to 
maintain or incorporate, and manage trees within a farming enterprise. Simple 
as this sounds, from a policy perspective critical household decisions about 
trees constitute elements of a highly complex process of guided, or misguided 
action. 
Factors affecting household land use decisions can be attributed to three 
broad domains and objectives: i) individual - personal welfare maximiza-
tion; ii) community - social obligation and commitment; and iii) government 
- legal conformity. Corresponding roughly with these decisio~l domains are 
the following three instruments of policy influence and action: i) market 
modifiers; ii) institutions; and iii) laws. Incentives or disincentives for certain 
decisions and behavior emanate from these instruments of policy with various 
degrees of force. This is a function, partly, of the comparative influence of 
three interacting development objectives: i) economic - national growth and 
development; ii) social - human rights and welfare; and iii) environmental -
conservation, preservation, restoration. Key features of this simplified scheme 
are depicted in Fig. 1. 
Poliey Domains 
and Objedives 
Publie Poliey 
Instruments

Fig. 1. Land use decision domains and influences. Broken and solid lines suggest the strength 
of conventional relationships among respective domains. Market modifiers such as taxes, sub-
sidies and direct public investment are employed principally to serve economic development 
and personal welfare maximization objectives, for example. These affect social welfare and 
environmental protection goals, but to a lesser extent.

--- Page 72 ---

68 
Drawing from this construct, for land use policy to foster a predictable 
course of acceptable action, signals from respective policy instruments must 
adequately convey development objectives from corresponding policy 
domains. These signals, in tum, must be consistent and mutually reinforcing 
in their effect on household decision-making. For the resulting action to 
positively affect the condition of land and resources, the farm enterprise, and 
the vitality of the region as a whole, it is important to calibrate policy signals 
to household decision-makers on the basis of sound knowledge of these 
relationships over time. 
In this scheme, agroforestry policy problems may be viewed as a function 
of insufficient correspondence between signals emanating from the instru-
ments of policy (market modifiers, institutions and laws) and respective house-
hold decision-making and action domains. Policy-makers may anticipate for 
example, that granting legal title to land will induce tree management as a 
function of the prospective owners' welfare maximization objective. In reality, 
however, customary tenure institutions based on norms of reciprocity and 
sanction might have an overriding influence. Local norms may not only 
mitigate against intensive tree management therefore, but leadership may incite 
social resistance to land titling procedures! 
Indeed, examples of 'signal dysfunction' in the record of agroforestry 
development efforts abound. These can be attributed either to 'gaps' or to 
'conflicts' between signals and decision domains. Thrupp [1994a] refers to 
gaps as delinkages or divergences, while conflicts entail explicit contradic-
tions. 
'Gaps' and 'conflicts' in tum, are partially attributable to inconsistent or 
competing policy objectives. Simply explained, countries everywhere are 
undergoing dramatic economic, social and environmental reorientations as a 
function of i) pressures to reform national economies through structural 
adjustment and market liberalization measures, ii) initiatives to respond to 
sustainable development mandates for reconciling economic growth with 
environmental and social equity objectives, and iii) associated political move-
ments for democratization and decentralization. As new actors enter the policy 
domain to meet these challenges, and market mechanisms, institutional con-
figurations and legal frameworks take shape to operationalize their respec-
tive objectives, competition for conceptual and operational space within which 
to influence land users' decisions intensifies. 
By this argument, land and resource tenure emerge as persistent constraints 
to agroforestry development as a function of competing claims on property 
rights as an instrument of both economic development and social welfare. 
When these two objectives conflict, defenders of either domain are reluctant 
to cede control over this critical resource. Similarly, direct public investment 
in infrastructure, afforestation schemes, processing capacity, extension edu-
cation, etc., may be conceived and deployed as instruments of economic, social 
or environmental policy. Because they are not adequately tailored to meet 
corresponding household security objectives however, they fall short of their

--- Page 73 ---

69 
intended purpose. The effects of such contradictions and delinkages can range 
from inefficiencies in policy or program implementation, to development 
malpractice as a function of misguided intervention. 
Challenges: constructing agroforestry policy pathways 
From the perspective of land use policy systems as a whole, that is, the 
configurations of actors engaged in various policy processes to influence the 
outcome of land use decisions, it has been shown that they are vastly too 
complex to predict the influence of a particular intervention with meaningful 
precision, or to control it. This is particularly so in many developing coun-
tries where colonialism, and its subsequent, erratic demise permanently dis-
rupted historical patterns of socio-political management. 
Thus, it is useful to disaggregate the system into its constituent stake-
holders. To further focus this effort prominence is given to strategic clusters 
of stakeholder groups, recognizing that these fluctuate, depending on the 
context and issues at hand. Enough has been learned about agroforestry, and 
sustainable resource management in general, to appreciate the central role of 
the resource user and manager in technology innovation. It is also known 
that this capacity is multiplied when the individual's efforts are facilitated 
within motivated groups [Engel, 1995]. It is assumed, therefore, that these 
forces of self-interest and innovative capacity warrant the focus of our 
attention at this level. 
It is appreciated that policy frameworks, or systems of rules and incen-
tives imposed from higher levels of authority, can be essential for creating a 
supportive context for agroforestry. There is concern that uniformly con-
structed and applied measures may be inconsistent with local conditions, and 
thus disabling. Systems of rules and incentives should, therefore, be tailored 
to characteristic socio-ecological regions. 
It is recognized that moving from the domain of agroforestry technology 
to policy involves confronting issues of inter-dependence. These arise when 
a number of actors recognize that they hold stakes in the same scarce resource, 
and can anticipate a collective impact from their actions [Roling, 1994]. 
Furthermore, that collective perceptions of inter-dependence are an essential 
condition for managing 'conflicts' and 'gaps' among stakeholders in the 
development of a land use policy system. 
Experience with agroforestry technology and rural development suggests 
that participatory research mobilizes, empowers and induces technical 
innovation [Rocheleau, 1991; Buck, 1993; Scherr, 1991]. It is reasonable to 
surmise, therefore, that as agroforestry advances more emphatically into the 
realm of policy, it will be appropriate to draw from prior experience to fashion 
participatory methods and collaborative research strategies, suitable for 
building bridges and negotiating conflict among inter-dependent stakeholders 
within the system.

--- Page 74 ---

70 
Indeed, during the period of the agroforestry policy assessment initiatives 
described herein, a profusion of effort has been witnessed to bring these 
methods to bear on linking local stakeholders with wider planning and policy-
making processes, through collaborative research and development activities 
[Thrupp, 1994b]. Recent syntheses of this experience convey important 
successes and an expanding trend toward multiple, convergent research and 
development processes that combine the complementary strengths of organi-
zations with different sectoral expertise and mandates, as well as distinct types 
of institution [Rocheleau, 1994; Thrupp et aI., 1994]. The facilitation of 
multiple knowledge networks is at the heart of this process. 
In the US, the theory and practice of participatory research in sustainable 
agriculture is just coming to light [01ott et aI., 1994]. By recent accounts the 
approach will be instrumental in advancing the science and application of 
agroforestry. As in developing countries, social scientists can help to serve 
the development effort by expanding their repertoire of participatory research 
methods to anticipate and meet the new policy challenges. These will surely 
arise for example, as agroforestry is applied to address watershed or landscape 
level conservation and development issues. 
Conclusions 
A recent surge of interest and activity in policy dimensions of agroforestry 
development throughout the world suggests growing recognition that further 
innovation may be advanced most effectively by tackling the structural barriers 
that appear to constrain this good idea from taking off! This perception serves, 
for a while, to put social scientists in the limelight. It also challenges them 
to provide a focus through which to penetrate the complex policy environ-
ment that characterizes land use decision-making and action. The conventional 
use of 'global', predictive models must begin to give way to decentralized 
policy analysis and design. 
The coupling of agroforestry with forestry policy issues in less developed 
countries provides insight into the important phenomena of perceived inter-
dependence among actors with a common stake in the management of natural 
resources. The condition generates opportunities for joint action, and suggests 
the critical roles of bridge-building and conflict management to facilitate the 
development of this perception among stakeholders. In the domain of agro-
forestry this insight can be usefully applied to foster joint action among 
economic, social and environmentally-oriented interests and objectives for 
land use. It serves also to link practitioners, technical advisors and scientific 
researchers in various configurations, to critically reflect on the policy envi-
ronment in which they operate, and how they might act upon it. 
The strategy places the researcher in the role of facilitator. He or she 
becomes concerned with generating knowledge and sharing information that 
will inform and motivate actors in respective knowledge networks to appre-

--- Page 75 ---

71 
ciate their interdependence. The researcher focuses also on linking networks 
to enhance their effectiveness. Participatory research is instrumental in this 
process. 
Strategic research can contribute importantly through its use to evaluate 
the conditionality and the effectiveness of dispersed policy reform efforts. It 
should aim to deepen understanding of processes involved in the construc-
tion of alternative policy frameworks and mechanisms. It should be employed 
also to identify key attributes of the socio-ecological environments which give 
rise to such initiatives. Ultimately, analysts would seek to draw generalizable 
conclusions concerning conditions and opportunities for strategic policy 
interventions, designed to reduce gaps and manage conflicts within complex 
land use decision-making environments. 
Methodologically, strategic agroforestry policy research should be con-
cerned with understanding the mechanisms involved in facilitative networking. 
Improved knowledge about this form of social organization for innovation 
will help to multiply the efforts of those public and private organizations, 
variously configured, that are combining strengths to improve agroforestry 
and sustainable land use policy. This genre of collaborative action policy 
researchers may be wise, also, to link their efforts with more conventional, 
sector or policy-specific research activities. The latter can serve, when data 
are available, to test the feasibility and impact of specific propositions for 
policy reform that emerge from facilitated networks. Such unions, in turn, 
should help to develop data bases from which increasingly robust models of 
land use decision factors and processes eventually can be constructed. 
Notes 
1. Diagnosis and Design, or 'D&D', was developed at the International Center for Research 
in Agroforestry (ICRAF) in the mid-1980s as agroforestry's analogue to farming systems 
analysis. A key distinguishing feature of D&D is its focus on meeting the broad array of 
basic human needs from the agricultural landscape and farming enterprise. The methodology 
germinated from researchers' observations of rural development project experience with 
agroforestry. It was brought to fruitition as an interdisciplinary framework for scientific 
research, then re-interpreted as a participatory methodology for rural development planning 
and intervention. 
2. In addition, the literature reports numerous case studies of field-based encounters with policy 
constraints, and efforts to overcome them. See for example, Follis and Nair [19941 and Josiah 
[19941. 
3. AFTA has grown from about 100 members in 1993 when its first newsletter was published, 
to approximately 400 in January, 1995. Membership is open to scientists, professionals and 
practitioners of any nationality who have an interest in temperate zone agroforestry. It is 
coordinated by an elected president. based at Michigan State University. and a seven person 
steering committee. 
4. The list of topics associated with each research problem is specified in CIFOR [19941. pp. 
7-9. 
5. The issue of institutional capacity for policy research is discussed in CIFOR [19941. pp. 7-9.

Economic evaluation of financial and non-financial costs 
and benefits in agroforestry development and the value of 
sustain ability 
C. PRICE 
School of Agricultural and Forest Sciences, University of Wales, Bangor, Gwynedd LL57 2UW, 
UK 
Key words: cost-benefit analysis, environmental values, futures, non-market effects 
Abstract. Financial appraisal of agroforestry is not different in kind from appraisals applied to 
pure forestry. Existing techniques for valuing non-market effects may also be applied. Effects 
on hydrological and carbon fluxes might be less favourable than those of pure forests. In the 
confusing field of biodiversity valuation too, only limited and specific claims can be made for 
agroforestry. Amenity valuation techniques developed for trees and woodlands encounter 
additional problems of interpretation in an agroforestry context. Agroforestry may offer a 
quantifiable value in short-term sustainability, and might meet recent economic definitions of 
long-term sustainable development, without encountering the full dangers to future generations 
embodied in conversion of natural to human capital. 
Introduction 
By referring to financial and non-financial effects, to costs and to benefits, 
and (through sustainability) to the long term as well as the short, the title of 
this paper excludes nothing of relevance to agroforestry. Hence it must be 
extremely selective, or extremely superficial in the material treated. On the 
other hand, the number of economic studies of agroforestry which stray 
beyond discounted cash flow (DCF) appraisal of financial costs and benefits 
is pitifully small. Even those which evaluate environmental effects such as 
soil conservation [Ehui et aI., 1990] or nitrogen fixing [Stone et aI., 1993] 
address on-site financial benefits, rather than classical economic externalities. 
Some papers about non-financial benefits quote results from pure forestry 
valuations. However, it is in the interactive nature of agroforestry, that its 
effects cannot be compiled as a weighted mean of agricultural and forestry 
components. 
Hence this paper takes a broad, superficial view, summarizing economists' 
current thinking about valuation of non-financial effects, and about sustain-
ability. It presents no new evidence on biophysical effects, but exemplifies 
how valuation techniques might be applied to the known and expected effects 
of agroforestry. Where appropriate, comparison with the effects of gross scale 
land use mixtures is made. 
Most reference is to temperate conditions, but where relevant - and par-
ticularly in relation to sustainability - tropical conditions are mentioned too.

--- Page 79 ---

76 
Financial values 
Financial evaluations of agroforestry based on projected or actual yields are 
becoming more abundant (many recent articles in Agroforestry Systems). Such 
DCF calculations require no innovations of economic technique, but merely 
a more elaborate application of protocols which have been central to forest 
economics for 150 years [Stone et aI., 1993]. Nor are the results they deliver 
surprising. For example, in comparisons with pure forestry or pure agricul-
ture on similar sites, a low discount rate favours pure forestry, and a high 
rate pure agriculture. But agroforestry may give greater net benefit than either 
pure land use in the following circumstances. 
• There are supplementary or complementary productivity effects between 
crop components [Wannawong et al., 1991]. 
• Agricultural prices are falling relative to forestry prices: thus the agricul-
tural component is sold when its price is relatively high, and the forestry 
component when its price is relatively high [Willis et aI., 1993]. 
• Forestry revenues are higher per unit of photosynthetic potential utilized, 
but the discount rate is high enough to outweigh this for food output at 
the beginning of the production cycle (C. Price, unpub.). (This effect does 
not seem to have been widely noted.) 
• Some non-market benefit arises from mixing of land uses, either physically 
within the site (for example, provision of edge habitats for wildlife), or 
within the economic enterprise (for example, stabilization of cash flow). 
Cases of the last kind are mentioned below. Opposite cases, where the mixing 
of crops creates negative interactions, also receive speculative evaluation. 
Non-financial values 
Recent interest in environmental economics has brought numerous new clas-
sifications of techniques for monetizing extra-market effects. An exhaustive 
and clear-cut taxonomy recognises eight approaches to valuation [Price, 1989, 
Chapter 24]. 
Approach 1 - Market benefits are created or destroyed for other agents in 
the economy. 
Approach 2 - Financial costs are imposed on or undertaken by other agents, 
in order to protect existing benefits or prevent future damage. 
Approach 3 - Similar commodities have a price in other markets. 
Approach 4 - Causes and campaigns produce voluntary contributions. 
Approach 5 - Respondents to questionnaires state a willingness to pay for 
benefits or to accept compensation for costs. 
Approach 6 - Expert judgements of value are formulated and subjected to 
discussion and refinement.

--- Page 80 ---

77 
Approach 7 - Costs have previously been willingly incurred in order to favour 
non-market benefits. 
Approach 8 - Financial costs are incurred to gain access to non-market 
benefits. 
No systematic review of the merits and problems of these approaches is 
offered here: the interested reader is referred elsewhere [Hanley and Spash, 
1993]. But at least one relevant example of each approach is given in the 
following sections. 
Biophysical impacts and production function approaches 
Approaches 1 and 2 (which include variants known severally as production 
function, replacement cost, defensive expenditure, environmentally compen-
sating expenditure) recognise that particular land uses have effects which, 
though not themselves marketed, nonetheless hold financial implications for 
other land users, locally, regionally and even globally. The economic elements 
of these approaches are, in principle, straightforward: it is in obtaining reliable 
biophysical relationships that most of the problems arise. 
Hydrological impact 
It has long been known that conifer crops, under temperate oceanic condi-
tions, evaporate significantly more water than grassland on equivalent sites 
[Calder and Newson, 1979]. They are also more successful in 'scavenging' 
particulates (by dry deposition from aerosols) and acidic solutions (from 
clouds) [Gee and Stoner, 1988]. 
Both ionic and sediment loads are increased by cultivation associated with 
afforestation, and by the gross intervention of forest harvesting [Leeks and 
Roberts, 1987; White, 1990]. 
Effects on water quality and differential evaporation bring various finan-
cial implications. Unexpected extra costs may be incurred, such as liming of 
catchments to counteract the acidification due to the scavenging of acid mists 
by conifers [Whitehead et aI., 1990]. In one celebrated incident the sediment 
load running off a 90-ha plantation into Crai Reservoir in Wales led to £45,000 
being spent on temporary alternative sources of water. 
Discounted costs may increase because engineering works are brought 
forward in time. Advanced investment in new water storage capacity, due to 
increased evaporation from forest canopies, could incur a cost of the same 
order of magnitude as the cost of planting the forest [Collett, 1970]. The cost 
of advancing water treatment investment as a result of the Crai Reservoir 
incident was put at £317,000, giving a total imposed cost of about £4000 per 
hectare of forest. 
Economically quantifiable loss of benefits may be incurred too, including

--- Page 81 ---

78 
reduced revenues from fisheries suffering acidification [Milner and Varallo, 
1990], and reduced electricity sales where hydroelectric catchments are 
affected [Barrow et aI., 1986]. In both cases, the effects may determine 
whether forestry is worthwhile or not. 
The above effects are due partly to high moisture retention on large leaf 
surface areas, and partly to the aerodynamic roughness of tree canopies. In 
both respects, open-grown tree crops would be expected to exert an effect 
which is more-than-proportional to the volume of timber grown. Recent 
research [Teklehaimanot et aI., 1991] has indeed shown greater rainfall inter-
ception and evaporation rates for widely spaced trees. In small woods greater 
losses were found at woodland edges than in interiors [Harding et aI., 1992], 
while edges also scavenge mists and atmospheric particulates more efficiently, 
potentially contributing more to watercourse pollution. Thus greater costs may 
be ;ncurred by arranging a given size and number of trees in agroforestry 
configurations, ratber than as conventional woodlands. 
Cultivation effects depend on the specific agroforestry system employed 
[White, 1990]. Where there is an arable component, and where terrain varies 
in erodibility, a separation of crop components may minimize the cultivation 
of susceptible sites, and thus soil losses. 
Climate change 
At least eight approaches to costing carbon fluxes are found in the literature 
[Cline, 1992]. They generally refer to losses of production consequent on 
global warming and sea-level rise, or to responsive/compensating expendi-
tures undertaken to avoid or mitigate damage. Cost estimates have ranged 
widely, from 20p to £200 per tonne of carbon [Price and Willis, 1993], with 
typical estimates for loss and damage around £10, depending on the discount 
rate used and on whether some arbitrary time horizon is imposed. 
Inserting the £10 value in a model for poplar grown at a wide, agroforestry 
spacing gives the estimated value of carbon fixing as about £470 per ha, or 
30% of combined timber/carbon benefit. For a conventional forestry spacing, 
carbon is worth £960 per ha, or 43%. Assuming no differential carbon storage 
by ground vegetation and soil, the difference in favour of conventional spacing 
seems significant. It is attributable to the smaller volume per ha under agro-
forestry. Growing an equal volume of timber would give similar carbon values 
under either regime. 
Willingness to pay for biodiversity 
Biodiversity has become a fashionable field for economic research, full of 
unresolved debates about terminology and concepts, and much intertwined 
with philosophical issues [Randall, 1991]. An intensive and high-productivity 
cultural system as often used in agroforestry is based on numerously repli-

--- Page 82 ---

79 
cated genes. Expansion of the system thus has marginal direct benefit to the 
gene pool. On the other hand, compared with intensive conifer forest (low 
levels of light reaching the forest floor) and intensive agriculture (chemical 
application regimes designed to promote monoculture) agroforestry does offer 
an improved spectrum of niches for colonization. 
To evaluate biodiversity benefits requires a deep enquiry into why diver-
sity should be considered valuable. Biodiversity values are conveniently 
treated under the headings of instrumental, interest and intrinsic values [Price, 
1993a], these referring respectively to: 
• substances produced or functions served which promote the material cause 
of humanity; 
• pleasure given to humanity by the existence of 'nature'; 
• the good supposed to reside in the existence of 'nature' in itself. 
Instrumental values 
Intimate mixing of crops promotes system function in numerous ways: fixing 
of nitrogen; soil stabilization; provision of nesting and foraging sites for crop-
pollinating species [Saville, 1993]. These benefits of agroforestry are, 
however, mostly captured by financial analysis based on the enhanced pro-
ductivity of individual enterprises: whether the long term implications are 
adequately addressed is touched on later in relation to sustainability. 
Interest values 
On-site enjoyment of wildlife has traditionally been valued by analysis of 
travel costs (approach 8). Using this method, the annual wildlife benefits of 
Britain's state forests have been estimated at about £15 million [Benson, 
1994]. The method, however, fails to distinguish the extra value attributable 
to the particular land use, and is unlikely to allow separate valuation for 
particular species [Price, 1987]. The emphasis has now shifted to question-
naire research (approach 5), where, despite a plethora of problems associated 
with sample and questionnaire design, the contingent valuation method (CVM) 
has become a fashionable tool [Mitchell and Carson, 1989]. 
A particular recent focus of CVM studies has been on what are called non-
use, or more properly passive use values, which are experienced without actual 
visits to site. While these values are susceptible to shameless exploitation as 
a means of boosting the value ascribed to any land use, they are normally 
important only for primeval ecosystems or ancient cultural landscapes, rather 
than for new land use systems. Thus agroforestry - and particularly agro-
forestry development - is unlikely to show any measurable passive use value 
in an unbiased survey of habitat. Colonizing species in an open-structured 
and rather dynamic agroforestry system are likely to be opportunists, which 
are not of the greatest public interest (S. Hinsley, pers. comm.): current con-

--- Page 83 ---

80 
servation priorities in Britain are with species of open moorland, undrained 
wetland, and the deep interior of ancient semi-natural woodland. 
This rather unenthusiastic assessment is less applicable to conditions, 
especially tropical ones, where agroforestry represents a richer successor to 
a multilayered forest ecosystem than any pragmatic alternative would do. 
CVM studies of passive use should on the whole be treated with caution, 
for several reasons in addition to those normally associated with hypothet-
ical questionnaires. It is, firstly, suggested [Minter, 1994] that willingness to 
pay is meaningful only after respondents have received adequate information 
about the nature and functions of species or habitats. However, such infor-
mation both makes the respondent highly unrepresentative of the population 
generally, and risks double-counting instrumental values, which are appro-
priately addressed through other valuation approaches. 
Secondly, the very fact of a questionnaire's being administered raises the 
profile of a particular species or habitat. It suggests that something important 
is at stake, and at the same time (temporarily) displaces other environmental 
concerns. The value derived is an artefact of the questionnaire, and over-
states the long-term impact of a particular species or habitat on the interest 
value derived from nature conservation taken as a whole [Price, 1995]. 
Thirdly, it has been shown that CVM studies tend to elicit a 'symbolic 
value' [Blamey and Common, 1994] or a value for the 'moral satisfaction' 
[Kahneman and Knetsch, 1992] associated with a general support for the 
environment. Such values could be reallocated to other convenient symbols, 
without significant loss. 
Money subscribed to wildlife campaigns (approach 5) is potentially even 
more likely to misrepresent values. Subscriptions can scarcely be interpreted 
in terms of rational consumers buying a conservation good, since the proba-
bility that an individual contribution will affect the outcome of the campaign 
is very small. Instead, contributions represent the purchase of image and self-
image, and possibly altruistic motives which are likely to entail multiple 
counting of benefits. 
Intrinsic values 
Multiple definition of intrinsic value has led to a deep confusion of interpre-
tation. It has been equated with some aspects of passive use value [Pearce and 
Turner, 1990]. It has also been suggested that 'the value we place may include 
our valuation of an animal's own existence'. Truly, however, intrinsic value 
is not a part of human satisfaction, and the only means to interpret it is via 
judgements about what sacrifices of profit ought to be made, to improve 
conditions for other species. Past 'ethical' conservation decisions might be 
thought to reflect such valuations (approach 7). However, past decisions may 
have been made in different circumstances. The initiator of this approach, 
Robinson Gregory [1955], mentions open forage areas in forests ambiguously, 
in terms both of financical optimization, and as a means of judging broader

--- Page 84 ---

81 
mUltiple use. Even if an ethical conservation motive can confidently be 
attributed for past sacrifices, there are further possibilities of confusion: human 
judgement is influenced by human interest, and a quite unwarranted bias 
towards 'cuddly' and towards rare species may have prompted such decisions. 
Seen from the 'viewpoint' of wildlife itself, existence as an individual in a 
population of individuals, rather than existence as a member of a rare or 
attractive species, must be considered the principal source of value. 
The relevance of such metaphysical considerations for agroforestry devel-
opment is this: ecosystems whose structural diversity encourages a high 
biomass per hectare should be deemed to contribute a high intrinsic value, 
even if the represented species are neither rare nor of particular human interest. 
Attributes of amenity 
Hedonic pricing 
While contingent valuation has become the 'lead methodology' for valuing 
wildlife, recent attention in valuing landscape has concentrated on the hedonic 
pricing method. This interprets expenditure, typically on house purchase or 
travel to countryside sites, as a valuation of attributes to which the expendi-
ture gives access (approach 8): these attributes include environmental as well 
as social, locational and structural attributes of houses, or the environment 
and activities available on countryside visits. 
One study [Garrod and Willis, 1992] found that the woodland attribute 
which best accounted for variation in house price was whether tree cover in 
proximity to the house exceeded 20% or not. Preference for 20-30% tree cover 
also appeared in a survey of designated community forestry areas [Whiteman, 
1994]. These studies, however, make no reference to the arrangement of 
woodland and open ground - whether, for example, the components were in 
large blocks, or intimately mixed as in classical agroforestry systems. More-
over, they do not consider changing appearance through time. In any event, 
results given for conventional, closed canopy forestry are unlikely to trans-
late into an agroforestry context. 
Of more relevance are survey results revealing greater willingness to pay 
for access to forests which included open areas and diversity of height [Hanley 
and Ruffell, 1993]. However, in terms of statistical significance and agree-
ment between techniques, the results of comparison of several statistical 
approaches to forest attributes were described as 'disappointing'. Given the 
theoretical objections to hedonic pricing [Price, 1991], this should cause no 
surprise. 
Expert judgement 
Approach 6 is also based on attributes. Here, however, money values are 
derived from subjective estimates of what is 'reasonable' and what commands

--- Page 85 ---

82 
consensus among other experts. Helliwell's system [Helliwell, 1990] requires 
a score on a scale 1-4 to be given for each of seven attributes, some relevant 
and readily quantified for an agroforestry system. For example, the scoring 
for a hectare of poplar, in a flood plain, spaced at 8 x 8 m, halfway through 
a rotation of 30 years would be 
Size of tree 
Useful life expectancy 
Importance of position in landscape 
Presence of other trees

provided the trees were regarded as individuals. Scores are multiplied together, 
and further multiplied by £10 to give a value per tree - in this case £360. 
The value for the whole hectare would be £56,250. However, if the trees are 
considered collectively as part of a wood, the aggregate score would be 
derived as follows:

3 
The aggregate score, 96, is to be multiplied by a 'points' value of £25, giving 
a value of £2400. 
Clearly, whether an agroforestry system is seen as individual trees or as 
an integral wood has an important bearing on its ascribed value. There are 
also problems in interpreting the descriptive categories. Notably, 'appropri-
ateness to setting' depends on cultural background. The English Countryside 
Commission has been negative about poplar agroforestry in the traditional 
English landscape (D. Gear, pers. comm.). 
Recreational agroforestry 
The open structure and variety of agroforestry systems makes them in one 
sense amenable to the kind of recreational development which has been widely 
canvassed as a justification for conventional and community forestry. On the 
other hand, regularity of structure and intensive management of the agricul-
tural component are less favourable. One proposal for introducing agroforestry 
to a recreation park [Broder and Odronic, 1990] saw it simply as a means of 
raising funds for recreation management.

--- Page 86 ---

83 
The value of sustainability 
Sustainability of land use systems may be seen not only from physical and 
economic viewpoints, but also in short- and long-term perspectives. In the 
short-term economic sense, sustainability implies an ability to survive fluc-
tuations in the conditions of production and sale. Several authors [Blandon, 
1985; Lilieholm and Reeves, 1991] have pointed out that agroforestry, by 
delivering several products, is less vulnerable to fluctuations in markets. In 
a subsistence setting, many reasons have been identified for expecting tree 
crops to enhance the stability of consumption [Falconer, 1990]. 
However, the systems which are most diversified may not have the highest 
value measured in DCF terms [Reeves and Lilieholm, 1993]. A means of 
trading off profitability and stability of income is offered by examining behav-
iour in the market for a similar product (approach 3), that is, a diversified 
portfolio of shares on the stock market. An elaborate body of theory (the 
capital assets pricing model) 'prices' the advantage of a stabilized cash flow 
in terms of the lower rate of return habitually accepted for products whose 
price varies little, or even inversely, with fluctuations in prices generally. 
But many economists have argued that using such variation in rates of 
return as a basis for adjusting discount rates has no adequate theoretical basis, 
and gives misleading and perverse results when the production cycle is not 
an annual one [Price, 1993b]. In particular, it favours the forestry component 
over the agricultural component, when favour should be given to the very 
jointness of the products. Thus a price justified by the diversification that 
agroforestry offers may actually work to reduce the system's diversity. The 
value of stable income is more reliably interpreted as a percentage reduction 
in revenue accepted as the price of stability. 
Note that the advantages of diversification in the face of market risks may 
be achieved by mixing enterprises on a coarse scale as well as by adopting a 
single enterprise which embodies intimately mixed components: once again, 
the case for agroforestry per se must hang on the benefits noted in the 'finan-
cial values' section above. Such particular benefits include robustness to 
biophysical threats, but not to market fluctuations [Price, 1989, Chapter 18]. 
In relation to long-term economic sustainability of land uses, economists 
have recently advanced some striking, counter-intuitive, and dangerous claims. 
Sustainability as most widely understood entails that resource use should avoid 
'compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs' 
[World Commission on the Environment and Development, 1987]. Conven-
tional DCF, on the other hand, automatically gives negligible weight to 
benefits and costs accruing beyond a couple of generations into the future, 
and so seems a very efficient way of compromising sustainability. Yet econ-
omists argue discounting is justified precisely to protect the interests of future 
generations: firstly because discounting reduces the profitability of invest-
ments, and thus the probability that environmentally damaging investments 
will achieve an acceptable economic performance [Pearce and Turner, 1990];

--- Page 87 ---

84 
secondly, because it ensures allocation of resources to investments in human-
made capital, which offer greater returns to future generations than keeping 
resources in low-yielding natural assets [Kay and Mirrlees, 1975]. 
These ideas can be traced back to development theory in the 1950s and 
1960s [Westoby, 1962]. Yet the actual outcome in that era was that natural 
resource destruction financed, not real development, but immediate con-
sumption and unproductive investment [Price, 1990]. There is little reason to 
suppose that the current round of forest destruction will be accompanied by 
a more benign use of the funds made available. 
As for the role of discounting in barring environmentally destructive invest-
ments, it is more aptly achieved by focusing on the sustained and undiscounted 
environmental losses in themselves, in so far as these effects are deemed to 
have undiminishing importance in the future. 
But, as already emphasized, it should not be supposed that low or zero 
discount rates would favour agroforestry rather than pure forestry. The justi-
fication of agroforestry may be seen as a compromise technology: human-
made capital that, while offering higher returns than unmodified ecosystems, 
may nonetheless preserve some environmental advantages of the original 
multilayer system. Such a realignment of productive assets is physically 
accountable within the ambit of the land manager, by contrast with the 
remission of exploitation revenues into a nebulous programme of 'develop-
ment investment'. 
Conclusion 
Judging by relevant insights and results from environmental and forestry 
economics, agroforestry does not offer, consistently and without need for 
quantification, extraordinary benefits of the financial or non-financial kind. 
There may, indeed, be particular costs. The argument for agroforestry devel-
opment continues to rely largely on biophysical and economic complemen-
tarity effects, and, in the tropical situation, on the ecological advantage over 
monocultures. No mystique, no propagandizing attached to a newly-topical 
production system will impress hard-nosed economists: a systematic and 
quantitative investigation of the claimed benefits and admitted disadvantages 
is what they require, as a precursor to fuller economic evaluation.

I am grateful to colleagues in the BioEconomic Agroforestry Modelling group 
in Bangor for useful discussions, and especially to Rob Willis for develop-
ment of the carbon modelling spreadsheet.

Farmer costs and benetits* from agroforestry and farm 
forestry projects in Central America and the Caribbean: 
implications for policy 
D. CURRENT' and S. J. SCHERR2 
I University of MinnesotalEPAT Project, St. Paul, MN, USA; 2IFPRI, 1200 17th Street N.W., 
Washington, DC 20036, USA 
Key words: economic analysis, extension, financial analysis 
Abstract. This paper presents results of an evaluation of the benefits provided by agroforestry 
and farm forestry projects in Central America and the Caribbean, and the policy implications. 
Tree planting provided financial benefits to farmers, as well as social, economic and environ-
mental benefits. These justify policy interventions to explicitly recognize the potential of 
on-farm tree-planting efforts and provide a policy environment favorable to such efforts. New 
or revised policies are needed, particularly in a period of declining public budgets, to limit 
external incentives to in-kind inputs, rather than financial subsidies; promote market develop-
ment for tree products; adopt low-cost, community-based extension strategies; focus research 
support; and reduce regulatory disincentives. 
Introduction 
The technical potential of agroforestty and farm forestry to address a range 
of development and environmental challenges is now widely recognized. 
Efforts to integrate more trees into farming systems on a large scale have 
generated new interest in creating a supportive policy environment for these 
types of land use. A set of international workshops held to identify priority 
policy research issues for forestry and agroforestry noted, among the five pre-
dominant themes, the policy challenges of use and management of open wood-
lands, woody fallows and the role of agroforestry in supplying tree products 
and improved environment [CIFOR-ICRAF-IFPRI, 1994]. Referring to Latin 
America, in particular, De Camino [in press] highlighted several areas where 
policy change was necessary: public support, institutional reform, land tenure, 
access to resources, markets, and incentives. 
Central America and the Caribbean have, for the last 10-15 years, been the 
scene of development projects and programs which have included the 
promotion of tree-growing on small and medium-sized farms. These projects 
have been a response to increasing deforestation and the subsequent envi-
ronmental degradation affecting soil productivity and the general welfare of 
the large rural population in the region. The rural population depends on these 
diminishing tree resources for fuel, construction material, posts and poles, and 
protection for crops and homesteads from winds and excessive heat. Many 
initiatives are underway to modify public policy to be more favorable to

--- Page 91 ---

88 
afforestation and improved forest resource management. However, the design 
and implementation of these policies is hindered by lack of empirical infor-
mation on the response of farm and forest users to policy action. Much of 
existing policy research on agroforestry and farm forestry in Central America 
is informal and not necessarily linked to policy review and formulation 
[Current, 1994]. 
This paper summarizes the results of the first major comparative study of 
financial and economic benefits to farmers from agroforestry practices 
promoted by these development projects. It briefly describes forestry policy 
challenges in Central America and the Caribbean and the status of agroforestry 
and farm forestry development and policy in the region. It describes the field 
studies used to collect data from 21 projects in the region and summarizes 
the results of the evaluation of financial and economic benefits provided by 
agroforestry and farm forestry systems. 
Findings suggest some policy changes which will lower the costs of pro-
moting widespread agroforestry adoption, by better matching agroforestry 
systems to local economic conditions and encouraging the development of 
latent markets, reducing the role of subsidies and subsidized credit, improving 
institutional support to extension and research, and modifying tenure and land 
use regulations currently constraining adoption. 
Background 
Forestry situation in Central America, Haiti and the Dominican Republic 
Central America has a population of about 30 million, with approximately 
50% of that population living in rural areas. This contrasts with South America 
and the Caribbean, where about 28% of the population lives in rural areas. 
This rural population depends heavily on wood for fuel, as well as for 
construction material and a variety of other tree products. For energy use 
alone, vegetable fuels (primarily fuel wood and charcoal) account for 56% of 
the total energy consumption in the region, compared to 23% for the 
Caribbean, and less than 20% in South America (Trossero, 1994]. There is 
also a relatively high commercial demand for tree products in Central America 
(see Table 1). 
At the same time, there has been tremendous pressure put on the forest 
resources of Central America to provide land for agriculture and livestock 
production and sustenance for the large rural population. This high demand 
for tree products and forested land has led to high rates of deforestation. While 
all of Latin America and the Caribbean lost approximately 7% of their forest 
cover between 1981 and 1990, Central America, Haiti and the Dominican 
Republic lost approximately 18%. In 1991, Haiti and El Salvador had about 
1 % and 6% forest cover respectively. In these countries, only about 2.3% of 
the area deforested annually is replaced by reforestation efforts [FAO, 1993a].
