Agroforestry Systems 30: 125-143, 1995. 
© 1995 Kluwer Academic Publishers. 
Tree root characteristics as criteria for species selection and 
systems design in agroforestry 
G.SCHROTH 
Institute of Soil Science and Soil Geography, University of Bayreuth. D-95440 Bayreuth. 
Germany 
Key words: nutrient leaching. nutrient recycling, root competition, root distribution, root 
management, tree-crop interface 
Abstract. This literature review presents information about the role of tree root systems for the 
functioning of agroforestry associations and rotations and attempts to identify root-related criteria 
for the selection of agroforestry tree species and the design of agroforestry systems. Tree roots 
are expected to enrich soil with organic matter. feed soil biomass. reduce nutrient leaching. 
recycle nutrients from the subsoil below the crop rooting zone and improve soil physical prop-
erties. among other functions. On the other hand. they can depress crop yields in tree-crop 
associations through root competition. After a brief review of favourable tree root effects in 
agroforestry. four strategies are discussed as potential solutions to the dilemma of the simulta-
neous occurrence of desirable and undesirable tree root functions: 1) the selection of tree species 
with low root competitiveness. eventually supplemented by shoot pruning; 2) the identification 
of trees with a root distribution complementary to that of the crops; 3) the reduction of tree 
root length density by trenching or tillage; and 4) the use of agroforestry rotations instead of 
tree-crop associations. The potential and limitations of these strategies are discussed. and deficits 
in current understanding of tree root ecology in agroforestry are identified. In addition to the 
selection of tree species and provenances according to root-related criteria, the development of 
management techniques that allow the manipulation of tree root systems to maximize benefit 
and minimize competition are proposed as important tasks for future agroforestry research. 
Introduction 
A principal aim of agroforestry is to create interactions between woody peren-
nials, herbaceous crops or pastures, and their biotic and abiotic environments 
which increase the overall productivity of the land use system and/or its 
sustainability. To design new agroforestry systems or to optimize existing 
ones, these component interactions need to be understood. The prediction of 
the behaviour of agroforestry systems when components are changed or 
interactions modified by management options, such as pruning, tillage and 
fertilization, is a major aim of agroforestry research and modelling. 
Interactions in agroforestry systems can be divided into above-ground and 
below-ground processes, and it is evident that much more is currently known 
about the former than the latter [Anderson and Sinclair, 1993]. The aim of 
this paper is to review some aspects of below-ground interactions in agro-
forestry and to give at least tentative answers to the following questions: 1) 
Are root characteristics important for the agronomic success and sustainability

--- Page 128 ---

126 
of agroforestry systems? 2) What root system attributes are relevant in the 
selection of agroforestry tree species? 3) In what ways should root charac-
teristics be taken into consideration when designing agroforestry systems? 
In this paper, the term agroforestry includes both spatial and sequential 
combinations of trees and crops, which will be named agroforestry associa-
tions and agroforestry rotations, respectively. Although the discussion should 
be relevant to most types of agroforestry system, emphasis is mainly on com-
binations of annual crops with service trees. Priority has been given to the 
presentation of concepts, rather than to a complete coverage of the available 
literature. 
Benefits expected from the presence of tree roots in agroforestry 
A common feature of agroforestry is that several benefits for soil fertility 
and nutrient cycling which partly or entirely depend on the activity of root 
systems are expected from the integration of trees into land-use systems. These 
processes are only briefly reviewed here; additional information is provided 
by Young [1989] and Anderson and Sinclair [1993]. 
Soil enrichment with organic matter, maintenance of the soil biomass and 
enhanced nutrient cycling through root production and turnover 
Although numerous studies have been conducted on root mass and its distri-
bution in the soil in tropical agroforestry systems (see below), very little is 
known about the turnover of the below-ground biomass and thus the input of 
C into the soil. According to different authors cited by Young [1989], root 
production in natural and plantation forests is 30 to 70% of total biomass 
production. In a coffee plantation shaded by Erythrina spp. and Inga spp. on 
acid soils in the Venezuelan highlands, root production was estimated to be 
6600 kg ha-1 yr-1 in the top 7.S cm of soil [Cuenca et aI., 1983], whereas values 
of only about 1100 kg ha-1 yr-1 in O--SO cm soil depth were calculated for an 
alley cropping system with Gliricidia sepium on a Plinthic Lixisol1 in the West 
African rainforest zone (compared with 13,600 kg ha-1 yr-1 of above-ground 
biomass production) [Schroth and Zech, 1995b]. Nye and Greenland [1960] 
suggested that root litter may be more efficiently converted into soil humus 
than above-ground litter. However, living roots may also increase organic 
matter mineralization by supplying easily degradable C sources [Helal and 
Sauerbeck, 1986], and the net effect of tree roots on the soil C balance may 
thus be reduced. Decomposing roots are a nutrient source for other plants, 
especially when there is little retranslocation of nutrients from the roots during 
senescence [Ferrier and Alexander, 1991; Nambiar, 1987], or when non-
senescent roots are cut off during tillage. Bowen [1984] summarized data from 
forest stands outside the tropics suggesting that the amounts of nutrients which 
are cycled through tree roots and their mycorrhizas can be far higher than

--- Page 129 ---

127 
corresponding values for litterfall, but similar information from tropical agro-
forestry systems is lacking. The role of roots in carbon and nutrient cycling 
in agroforestry is particularly important when the above-ground biomass is 
removed from the system, for example as fodder. Also, because of their con-
siderable lateral extension (see below), tree roots may influence the soil at 
distances from trees far beyond the area which is affected by natural litter-
fall. 
Uptake of water and mobile nutrients from the soil, thus reducing leaching 
losses 
Nutrient-conserving processes are particularly relevant when crops are absent 
or insufficiently developed but mineralization rates of soil organic matter are 
high, as at the onset of the rains after a dry season. Trees have been shown 
to dry out the soil of adjacent fields, thus not only competing with associated 
crops [Malik and Sharma, 1990; Schroth et aI., 1995b], but presumably also 
reducing percolation rates. Kuhne [1993] found considerably lower leaching 
of N, Mg and K in alley cropping with Leucaena leucocephala and maize 
compared to a maize mono crop on a Nitosol1 in subhumid southern Benin. 
Seyfried and Rao [1991] measured much lower nutrient concentrations in the 
soil solution under a perennial cropping system than under an annual 
mono crop on a Typic Dystropepe in Costa Rica and calculated higher leaching 
losses from the latter system, but the systems also differed in fertilization rates 
and presumably in soil tillage. 
Pumping up nutrients from subsoil layers below the main rooting zone of 
the crops 
The recycling of nutrients which have been leached from topsoil or released 
by rock weathering in subsoil is considered an important mechanism of soil 
regeneration during forest fallows in shifting cultivation. The extension of tree 
roots into deep subsoil layers and the ability of these roots to take up nutri-
ents which are incorporated into the above-ground biomass and eventually 
released by litterfall have been demonstrated [Comerford et aI., 1984; Stone 
and Kalisz, 1991; Van Rees and Comerford, 1986]. However, the actual con-
tribution of this process to nutrient cycling in agroforestry systems and nutrient 
enrichment of topsoils is not known. Woody perennials usually have their root 
maximum in the topsoil (see below) where they also take up most of 
their nutrients [IAEA, 1975], although the contribution of the subsoil to total 
nutrient and water uptake increases when the topsoil dries out [Comerford 
et aI., 1984; Eastham et aI., 1990). It would thus be expected that nutrient 
pumping by tree roots is least efficient in perhumid climates, where soils are 
usually most weathered and leaching rates highest so that the recycling of 
subsoil nutrients would be most needed.

--- Page 130 ---

128 
Improvement of soil penetrability for crop roots and of other soil physical 
properties 
Woody perennials can improve the root development of associated or subse-
quent annual crops [Hulugalle and Lal, 1986; Schroth and Zech, 1995b; 
Yamoah et aI., 1986]. Possible mechanisms include the creation of root 
channels [Schroth and Zech, 1995b; Stone and Kalisz, 1991; Van Noordwijk 
et aI., 1991b], an improved soil structure through increased earthworm activity 
[Hulugalle and Lal, 1986] and organic matter enrichment, or a general 
improvement of crop growth through favourable effects on soil fertility and 
microclimate. Tree roots may also increase the rooting volume of subsequent 
crops by penetrating hardened subsoil layers. On a shallow Ferric Acrisol1 in 
the savanna of central Togo, roots of three to four years old Senna siamea 
and Acacia auriculiformis trees penetrated a subsoil horizon which could only 
be dug with hammer and chisel, whereas the weaker roots of Cajanus cajan 
in the first season were seldom observed in this horizon (Schroth et aI., 
unpub.). The organic debris in old tree root channels may protect the roots of 
sensitive crop species from toxic aluminium in acid subsoil horizons [Van 
Noordwijk et aI., 1991b]. The 'bypass flow' of surplus water which rapidly 
infiltrates through such channels may reduce nutrient leaching from the soil 
matrix, although the nutrient loss from fertilizers or mulch applied to the 
soil surface may be increased by reducing their contact with the soil and the 
plant roots [Cameron and Haynes, 1986]. The overall effect of enhanced 
macroporosity thus depends on the site concerned and its management, and 
the difficulty of quantifying macropore flow on a per-area basis is a major 
problem in the study of tree root effects on nutrient leaching. Improved water 
infiltration and soil structure also reduce erosion hazards [Young, 1989], 
although the risk of soil shear may be increased. 
Fixation of atmospheric N 
With the exception of stem-nodulated tree species such as Sesbania rostrata, 
symbiotic N-fixation in trees is related to tree root systems and the condi-
tions in which they develop. Symbiotic N-fixation reduces the dependence 
of trees on soil N reserves and may increase N-availability in an ecosystem 
if the fixed N is returned to the soil via litterfall, root turnover or prunings 
[Young, 1989]. A high N-fixation potential is thus an important criterion for 
tree species selection. This aspect of tree root ecology has recently been 
reviewed [Dan so et aI., 1992; Giller and Wilson, 1991] and will not be further 
considered here. 
The afore-mentioned root-related benefits are supplemented by above-ground 
effects, such as improvement of microclimate (although shading may be detri-
mental), covering of soil by leaf litter and, of course, tree products. The listed 
processes seem to suggest that the presence of tree root systems with high

--- Page 131 ---

129 
biomass, rapid turnover and efficient nutrient uptake in the soil is necessary 
for soil fertility maintenance in agroforestry. Unfortunately, this situation is 
likely to provoke problems of root competition between trees and crops when 
grown together. Root competition occurs when one root system reduces the 
access of another root system to soil water and nutrients, either by taking them 
up itself or by impeding the development of the associated root system, for 
example through allelopathic effects [May and Ash, 1990] or the promotion 
of root diseases [Mchowa and Ngugi, 1994]. Avoiding the inherent conflict 
between fertility maintenance through tree roots and competition from tree 
roots is a key problem in agroforestry. Four possible strategies for alleviating 
this dilemma are discussed in the following section. 
1) Selection of tree species with low root competitiveness 
Root competition can be avoided if trees and crops are planted at sufficient 
distance so that the root systems do not intermingle, but this would neces-
sarily also negate the favourable effects of tree roots. In addition, the roots 
of tropical tree species, such as Adansonia digitata, Bombax costatum, 
Eucalyptus camaldulensis and Senna siamea, may extend several tens of 
meters from the trunk [Kessler and Breman, 1991; Stone and Kalisz, 1991; 
Schroth et aI., 1995b], and even in agroforestry systems where the trees are 
widely spaced, much of the area between them may be influenced by tree roots 
to some extent. An extensive root system is particularly useful where soils are 
infertile and often dry, especially when nutrient distribution is irregular [Stone 
and Kalisz, 1991] as in many savanna areas. In alley cropping, where 
hedgerow spacing is normally only a few meters, the whole alleys are usually 
permeated by tree roots [Hauser, 1993; Rao et aI., 1993; Schroth and Lehmann, 
1995; Schroth and Zech, 1995a]. However, the presence of tree roots in 
adjacent fields does not necessarily indicate root competition, nor does it 
indicate favourable root effects on soil fertility. On shallow Acrisols l in central 
Togo, root mass and length density decreased logarithmically with increasing 
distance from S. siamea trees [Schroth et aI., 1995b], and in much of the area 
within which roots were found their quantity may have been too low to affect 
soil conditions significantly. On a Haplic Lixisol l in semiarid Kenya, Huxley 
et aI. [1994] observed a patchy root distribution of Grevillea robusta trees and 
associated maize within the shared volume of soil and suggested that this may 
have reduced interspecific root competition. 
Several authors have compared different tree species according to their 
competitiveness. On sandy soils in the Sudan/Sahel zone of Senegal, Cazet 
[1989] found that line plantings of Acacia tortilis subsp. raddiana, Acacia 
nilotica subsp. adstringens, and Acacia senegal were more competitive with 
associated crops than Prosopis juliflora and F aidherbia alb ida, but this could 
only partly be explained by the particularly dense root systems of the Acacia 
species in the topsoil because of their limited lateral root extension. In humid

--- Page 132 ---

130 
to subhumid India, Eucalyptus sp. and L. leucocephala were found to be more 
competitive for soil moisture than banana (Musa x paradisiaca) and coconut 
(Cocos nucifera) when intercropped with cassava (Manihot esculenta) 
[Ghosh et at, 1987]. Budelman [1990] explained higher yam (Dioscorea alata) 
yields in association with Gliricidia sepium compared with L. leucocephala 
on a sandy ferralitic soil in the rainforest area of the Cote d'Ivoire partly by 
the higher root mass in the topsoil of the latter species. The denser root system 
of L. leucocephala compared with G. sepium was confirmed on a Lixisoll 
in semiarid Kenya, with S. siamea being intermediate [Rao et at, 1993]. 
Accordingly, experiments with root barriers on Alfisols2 in semiarid India 
demonstrated the intensive root competition between L. leucocephala and 
associated crops in alley cropping [Ong et at, 1991; Rao et at, 1991; Singh 
et at, 1989], whereas no yield increases of the crops were found with barriers 
in a similar experiment with G. sepium on a Ferralic CambisollPlinthic Lixisol1 
in the subhumid forest area of the Cote d'Ivoire despite water-limited condi-
tions [Schroth and Zech, 1995a]. On a shallow Plinthic/Ferric Acrisol1 in 
central Togo, maize yield gradients as a function of the distance from two 
year old S. siamea, G. sepium and Calliandra calothyrsus hedgerows were 
measured with and without root barriers [Schroth and Lehmann, 1995]. Only 
the maize in the C. calothyrsus plots showed a yield depression at the tree-
crop interface, which was significantly reduced by the installation of root 
barriers, indicating that this tree species had a more competitive root system 
than G. sepium and S. siamea. Unfortunately, the root characteristics of the 
tree species could not be related to their differing competitiveness, because 
the roots of C. calothyrsus and G. sepium could not be quantitatively distin-
guished from weed and crop roots. Rapid root growth, leading to a high root 
length density, is considered a particularly important parameter for nutrient 
uptake [Caldwell and Richards, 1986; Smethurst and Comerford, 1993], but 
efficient physiological uptake mechanisms, mycorrhizal infection or allelo-
pathic effects may also contribute to high competitiveness. 
The selection of tree species with low root competitiveness is a reason-
able strategy when 'above-ground' functions of the trees, such as the pro-
duction of wood, fodder or mulch, are to be achieved with minimum 
competition. However, where major benefits for the cropping system are 
expected from favourable root functions, it becomes critical to know if root 
systems of low competitiveness are also less efficient in the reduction of 
leaching losses and in soil enrichment with organic matter. There seems to 
be no direct evidence which shows this, but a comparison of the competitive 
L. leucocephala with the less competitive G. sepium may be instructive. As 
mentioned above, L. leucocephala was able to reduce nutrient leaching in an 
experiment in southern Benin, but reduction in soil water content and nitrate 
concentration in the soil solution was held responsible for negative effects 
on associated crop yields [Horst, 1991; Kiihne, 1993]. In the aforementioned 
alley cropping experiment with G. sepium in the Cote d'Ivoire, on the other 
hand, a tendency for increased root length densities in the subsoil (30-

--- Page 133 ---

131 
50 cm) of the agroforestry plots compared with sole cropping was observed 
during phases of high leaching risk; in general, however, the root length 
density of the trees was much less than that of the crops they replaced, and 
it decreased during the cropping season, presumably as an effect of frequent 
shoot pruning [Schroth and Zech, 1995a]. It also appeared as though total root 
production and thus C inputs into the soil through root turnover were not 
increased by tree integration in this system [Schroth and Zech, 1995b]. It 
was concluded that a tree species with a more intensive root system would 
be preferable in associations where the creation of a closed nutrient cycle 
and soil enrichment with organic matter are important objectives. 
The latter example also shows that root competitiveness is not a fixed char-
acteristic of a tree species, but may be influenced by tree management. Tree 
roots in forests and plantations follow soil moisture conditions in their seasonal 
growth rates [Cuenca et aI., 1983; Kummerow et al., 1990; Srivastava et aI., 
1986], and their competitiveness would thus be expected to be highest during 
the cropping season. Intensive shoot pruning of the trees during the rainy 
season, on the other hand, can induce an 'anti-cyclic' tree root development 
with decreasing root mass and length density during crop development and a 
regeneration of the root systems during the dry season, thereby leading to a 
certain complementarity in the time of tree and crop root development 
[Schroth and Zech, 1995a]. The reason is probably the depletion of energy 
and possibly nutrient reserves of the trees by frequent removal of the above-
ground biomass [Kandiah et aI., 1984], although Erdmann et aI. [1993] found 
that stem starch reserves were sufficient for initial regrowth of pruned G. 
sepium. Mycorrhizal infection may also be negatively affected by defoliation 
[Gehring and Whitham, 1994]. Increased root mortality and reduced forma-
tion of new roots after tree pruning should temporarily reduce root competi-
tion with associated crops, but increased nutrient losses after crop harvest may 
be a consequence of low tree root length densities during the rainy season 
when the risk of nutrient leaching is highest [Schroth and Zech, 1995a]. 
For maximizing C inputs into the soil through tree root turnover at 
minimum competition cost, tree species that take up low amounts of nutri-
ents per unit C investment in roots should be advantageous. Also, the trees 
should have a lower flexibility in terms of root formation to react to local 
nutrient enrichment, for example from fertilizer application or mulch decom-
position, than the crops. Efficient infection of tree roots with mycorrhiza can 
increase both nutrient uptake per unit root mass and flexibility of a root system 
[Atayese et aI., 1993; Bowen, 1985] and would thus be a disadvantage in 
this case. There seems to be a tendency that plants with thick roots have less 
'opportunistic' root systems than those with fine roots; that is, they reallo-
cate their roots less readily in favourable volumes of soil, shed them less 
readily in unfavourable soil and have a lower capacity for water and nutrient 
uptake [Eissenstat, 1992]. If this tendency can be substantiated, tree species 
with thick roots would be advantageous in agroforestry systems where the C 
input through root turnover is a primary aim of the association and root com-

--- Page 134 ---

132 
petition needs to be reduced. However, there is also a tendency for thicker 
roots to have lower turnover rates [Caldwell and Richards, 1986], and this 
would reduce their value for soil C enrichment. Trees with dense, flexible and 
mycorrhiza-infected root systems, on the other hand, would be most efficient 
in the interception of leached nutrients, but would be more competitive in 
tree-crop associations. Thus, the criteria for species selection depend on the 
priorities given to different tree root functions and the relative importance of 
tree and crop productivity. 
2) Selection of tree species with a root distribution complementary to 
that of the crops 
An ideal tree from a nutrient-cycling perspective should possess a deep root 
system with limited lateral extension in the topsoil, thus recycling nutrients 
from the subsoil and forming a 'safety net' against nutrient leaching below 
the crop rooting zone without interfering much with the crop root systems in 
the topsoil [Van Noordwijk et aI., 1991a]. This theoretical tree root distribu-
tion is in contradiction to the observation that most trees, like crops, normally 
have the maximum root length density in the topsoil [Dhyani et aI., 1990; 
Jonsson et aI., 1988; Kessler and Breman, 1991; Ruhigwa et al., 1992; Schroth 
et aI., 1995a; Toky and Bisht, 1992], although some species with a relatively 
uniform root distribution in the soil profile have been identified, such as 
Eucalyptus spp. [Jonsson et aI., 1988] and Dactyladenia (Acioa) barteri 
[Ruhigwa et aI., 1992]. Many tropical soils are too shallow or too acidic and 
nutrient-deficient in the subsoil to allow a spatial separation of the rooting 
zones of crops and trees [Szott et aI., 1991]. Furthermore, several factors are 
likely to reduce rooting depth of trees in agroforestry associations: fertiliza-
tion and biomass application make topsoil more attractive for tree roots and 
may favour the formation of shallow root systems. Accordingly, the percentage 
of roots near the soil surface increased with increasing age of forest succes-
sions in Costa Rica, corresponding to the increasing importance of litterfall 
for nutrient cycling [Berish, 1982]. Mulching has been shown to reduce the 
rooting depth of fruit trees in temperate climates, and to increase their lateral 
root extension [Kutschera, 1983; Yocum, 1937]. Shoot pruning of trees, a 
common procedure in many associations, seems to increase root branching 
in the topsoil and to restrict tree roots to shallower soil depths compared with 
roots of unpruned trees [Rao et aI., 1991; Van Noordwijk et aI., 1991a]. It 
should also be stressed that, although the 'inverse' tree root distribution may 
be ideal from a nutrient cycling perspective, it is not desirable for C cycling 
as it would negate C inputs from tree root turnover in the topsoil. 
Although the presence of tree roots in the topsoil is thus desirable, agro-
forestry trees should also be deep-rooted and take up a maximum of water 
and nutrients from subsoil horizons beneath the main rooting zone of the crops. 
The presence of competing root systems in the topsoil, either from neigh-

--- Page 135 ---

133 
boring trees planted at narrow spacing or from associated pastures or spon-
taneous undergrowth, has been shown to increase the rooting depth of trees 
and the use of subsoil resources [Comerford et al., 1984; Eastham et aI., 1990]. 
This may improve resource sharing in tree-pasture associations [Anderson and 
Sinclair, 1993] and the recycling of subsoil nutrients. In associations of trees 
with annual crops, tree spacing has to be wide to leave room for the crops, 
and competing grasses are normally removed by weeding. Although the root 
systems of annual crops are less dense than those of perennial grasses and 
are not present permanently, competitive crops such as maize are able to 
restrict the lateral spread of tree roots and to force them into the subsoil. 
Yocum [1937] described how apple tree (Malus sylvestris) roots turned 
downward when they came into competition with the root systems of asso-
ciated maize and continued to spread laterally below the crop rooting depth 
on a deep loess soil in Nebraska. Huxley et aI. [1994] observed on a Haplic 
Lixiso11 in semiarid Kenya that the lateral root development of Grevillea 
robusta hedgerows was less (and the trees were also smaller) when maize was 
grown adjacent to the hedgerows than when the adjacent area was uncropped 
and weeded, and the tree root density under the hedgerows seemed to 
be higher in the association than it was without competing root systems 
(Fig. 9 in Huxley et aI., 1994). These observations suggest that strips of crops 
or grasses with dense root systems may be used to protect less competitive 
crop species from tree root competition and may help to promote desirable 
tree root systems that take up a major proportion of their water and nutrient 
requirements from the subsoil. Evidently, significant nutrient uptake from 
the subsoil is largely limited to sites where at least some weatherable minerals 
have been retained within the reach of tree root systems during soil forma-
tion. 
3) Reduction of tree root density by root trenching or tillage 
In arid regions, where trees are planted as shelterbelts at relatively wide dis-
tances, the digging of open trenches between the trees and the fields reduces 
root competition and increases crop yields near the tree-crop interface 
[Onyewotu et aI., 1994; Singh, 1987]. The effort required to dig trenches, 
however, is not commensurate with unmechanized farming, and the exclu-
sion of tree roots from fields would also negate any benefits of roots in C 
and nutrient cycling. An interesting aspect of root trenching might be that it 
forces tree roots into deeper soil [Kang, 1993], where they may spread later-
ally and take up nutrients below the main rooting zone of the crops. However, 
the efficiency of this strategy depends on the ability of the tree root systems 
to colonize the topsoil of neighbouring fields by roots growing upward [Singh 
et aI., 1989] and may only be of short duration for some species. 
Soil tillage may be used to destroy tree roots in the topsoil before sowing 
crops, thereby protecting the crops for some time from tree root competition,

--- Page 136 ---

134 
although the tree roots remain active in the subsoil and may serve as a 'safety 
net' against nutrient leaching. Zero-tillage provoked yield-reductions of maize 
and cowpea (Vigna unguiculata) in different alley cropping experiments with 
L. leucocephala which were explained by root competition [Ssekabembe, 
1985], whereas deep hoeing to destroy the superficial tree roots reduced bean 
(Phaseolus vulgaris) yield depressions around G. robusta trees on an Ultisoe 
in the highlands of Rwanda [Neumann, 1984]. On a Typic Chromustere in 
semiarid India, 30 cm deep ploughing ('root pruning') along L. leucocephala 
hedgerows before sowing increased grain and stover yields of alley cropped 
sorghum, and higher soil water contents in the alleys with tree roots pruned 
indicated that root competition had been reduced [Korwar and Radder, 1994]. 
In addition, nutrients may have been released from the decomposing tree roots 
and taken up by the crop. Interestingly, a significant interaction of root pruning 
and the frequency of shoot pruning of the trees was only exceptionally 
observed in this study, despite the above-mentioned effect of shoot pruning 
on root mass and distribution. In some agroforestry associations, like boundary 
planting, it may be possible to repeat deep ploughing between trees and crops 
during the cropping season, thus further reducing the competition from 
regrowing tree roots and increasing the input of dead roots into the cropped 
soil. No effect of two different tillage methods, traditional hoe-ridging and 
level ploughing with an ox-plough, was found on root competition from S. 
siamea trees bordering agricultural fields on a Ferric Acrisol1 in central Togo, 
although ploughing led to generally higher crop yields and accelerated soil 
degradation [Schroth et aI., 1995b]. 
Besides the reduction of tree root competition, a desirable aspect of soil 
tillage in agroforestry is that it probably increases root turnover, and thus C 
inputs into the soil, by the periodic destruction of the tree roots. A consider-
ably higher fine root mass of S. siamea was found in ploughed than in untilled 
soil [Schroth et aI., 1995b]; however, as live and dead roots were not distin-
guished, part of the increase may have been caused by a stimulation of root 
growth by increased mineralization of soil organic matter. By increasing both 
root turnover and mineralization rates of soil organic matter, tillage is likely 
to have contrasting effects on the soil C balance even when a possible increase 
of erosion losses is not considered, and the net effect requires investigation. 
A further desirable effect of soil tillage is that nutrients are released from 
sloughed-off tree roots and may become available for the crops. In a study 
with nine leguminous tree species on a Ferralic Cambisol1 in the Cote 
d'Ivoire, N-fixing species had higher N-concentrations particularly in the 
coarser roots (1-5 mm) than non-N-fixing species, but the latter had the higher 
total root mass in most cases [Schroth et al., 1995a]. According to the 'syn-
chrony' hypothesis [SWift, 1984], nutrients released from decomposing organic 
matter should be used most efficiently by the crops if the release is matched 
with crop demand; hence the need to predict nutrient release dynamics. The 
leaf biomass of tropical legumes is not always a source of readily available 
N [Palm and Sanchez, 1991], and the decomposition oflegume roots and even

--- Page 137 ---

135 
root nodules does also not necessarily lead to immediate net N mineraliza-
tion [Nnadi and Balasubramanian, 1978; Sandhu et aI., 1990; Wardle and 
Greenfield, 1991]. C/N ratios are often unreliable as predictors for N release 
from legume biomass, and more sensitive predictors, such as the polyphenollN 
ratio [Palm and Sanchez, 1991] or the (polyphenol + lignin)/N ratio [Fox et 
aI., 1990] are currently being developed. Lehmann et aI. [1995] compared 
the N release from decomposing leaves, twigs and two diameter classes of 
roots of three agroforestry species in a field experiment in central Togo and 
found a curvilinear relationship between initial N release and the (polyphenol 
+ lignin)/N ratios of these substrates (Fig. 1). At ratios greater than about 10, 
the N release from the substrates, if any, was quite low and was not further 
reduced by increasing ratios. Nutrient release from Gliricidia sepium fine roots 
was considerably faster than from C. calothyrsus and S. siamea roots, but was 
slower than from leaves. The slow nutrient release from roots is a disadvan-
tage when these are immediately required by annual crops, but may be an 
advantage in tree-production systems with more continuous nutrient demand 
[Palm and Sanchez, 1990]. The temporary immobilization of mineral N in 
decomposing roots with low N or high lignin and polyphenol contents may 
be desirable under conditions of high leaching risk. A possibly interesting 
aspect is that excised roots from temperate-zone tree species, including their 
mycorrhiza, may remain alive for prolonged time intervals [Ferrier and

Alexander, 1985; Publicover and Vogt, 1993], so that nutrient release from 
roots that were cut off during tillage could be retarded. 
Despite these uncertainties, deep tillage could be an ideal management tool 
as it reduces root competition and increases at the same time the input of C 
and nutrients from the tree roots into the topsoil while retaining the 'safety 
net' of tree roots in the subsoil. Ways should be sought by which the effi-
ciency and frequency of tree root destruction in the fields by tillage can be 
increased, but the simultaneous increase of organic matter mineralization and 
eventually erosion losses deserves careful attention. Also, information about 
the speed with which roots of different tree species recolonize the topsoil after 
tillage is lacking. 
4) Agroforestry rotations 
A complete solution to the dilemma of the simultaneous occurrence of desir-
able and undesirable tree root functions is probably not possible in agroforestry 
associations, but requires the separation of trees and crops in time. In rota-
tional systems, the problem of root competition between trees and crops does 
not exist, and the tree species may be selected exclusively according to 
maximum fertility regeneration and economic output. However, improved tree 
fallows are not always an alternative to permanent tree-crop associations, but 
rather a supplement. In high rainfall areas, a disadvantage of agroforestry 
rotations is that during the cropping phase leached nutrients may infiltrate 
into the soil too deeply to be recycled by fallow trees, so that permanent 
asso-ciations would be necessary for creating closed nutrient cycles. Fallow 
rotations would only be suitable in subhumid areas where infiltration rates are 
lower [Van Noordwijk, 1989], although the efficiency of the recycling of 
leached nutrients during the fallow phase needs to be quantified experimen-
tally. As in humid climates, the restriction of trees to fallows may also not be 
possible in semi-arid areas because of low growth rates of trees which require 
long fallow periods for significant wood production. On sloping or wind-
exposed terrain, the permanent presence of trees in the fields may be neces-
sary for reasons of soil and microclimatic protection. 
In view of the regeneration of soil fertility, one would expect deep-rooted 
trees with, in addition, a high root mass and rapid root turnover in the topsoil 
to be particularly efficient fallow species, but the relationships between tree 
root characteristics and soil regeneration in fallows have rarely been investi-
gated experimentally. In the afore-mentioned study in the central Cote d'Ivoire, 
the soil regenerating effect of nine leguminous tree species, of which six 
were N-fixing, and a spontaneous control were compared [Schroth et aI., 
1995a]. The trees were grown in replicated plots for five years on a Ferralic 
Cambisoll , where the original topsoil had been partly removed during earlier 
land preparation. Litterfall and root mass in 0-60 cm soil depth were measured 
and were related to chemical and biological properties of the soil in the plots

--- Page 139 ---

137 
in order to identify characteristics of efficient fallow species. Despite consider-
able differences between tree species in above- and below-ground biomass 
and litterfall, total C and N in the soil differed little between species. However, 
N-mineralization in topsoils (0-10 cm) which were incubated in the labora-
tory showed a significant species effect. For the N-fixing species, tree root 
mass was significantly correlated with N-mineralization of the soil (Fig. 2). 
N-mineralization was also correlated with litterfall for seven of the tree 
species. High N-mineralization rates resulted in better N-nutrition as well as 
higher biomass and grain yield of rice (Oryza sativa), which was grown in 
15 I bags filled with topsoil from all plots as a bioassay of soil fertility. 
Apparently, tree species with high root mass and litterfall were most effi-
cient in increasing the available N-pool in the soil, in agreement with a 
suggestion by Haggar et al. [1993], that agroforestry maintained soil N avail-
ability at a higher level than sole cropping mainly by providing high organic 
matter inputs from the trees. Root intensive tree species also suppressed weeds 
efficiently. A potential problem was however that the fallow species that 
promoted high N-mineralization rates tended to accumulate less N in the soil 
and it could, therefore, be expected that there would be a more rapid deple-
tion of labile soil N-pools after the fallow phase when compared with species 
that promoted lower N-mineralization rates. There may thus be a trade-off 
between short-term yield improvement and sustainability in the selection of

and spontaneous fallow vegetation (Chromolaena odorata) in a field experiment in the central

control (redrawn from Schroth et aI., 1995a).

--- Page 140 ---

138 
fallow species, and long-term studies are needed to investigate this [Szott et 
aI., 1991]. 
Fallow trees should be deep-rooted for an efficient recycling of subsoil 
nutrients, and in addition to an appropriate selection of tree species and prove-
nances [Vandenbeldt, 1991], the design and management of the fallow should 
be planned with the aim of expressing this characteristic as much as possible. 
Close spacing of trees leading to early intraspecific root competition may be 
an efficient way of achieving this [Atkinson et al., 1976; Comerford et aI., 
1984; Eastham et aI., 1990]. In addition, a root-intensive understorey vege-
tation that dries out the topsoil and forces the tree roots into the subsoil may 
be desirable for nutrient recycling [Comerford et aI., 1984], but this advan-
tage has to be balanced against the danger of increased weed infestation during 
the subsequent cropping phase. 
Conclusions 
Root characteristics and root processes are clearly important factors for the 
success of agroforestry systems. Tree roots contribute to the maintenance and 
regeneration of soil fertility, but they are also important in competition with 
crops. Tree species and even provenances differ markedly in their root char-
acteristics, with respect to both their competitiveness and their effects on the 
soil. For this reason, root characteristics must become criteria in the selec-
tion of tree species for agroforestry, but more research is necessary before 
these can be defined on a sound basis for different agroforestry systems and 
pedoclimatic situations in the tropics. The undisputable deficits in our knowl-
edge about tree root ecology should not be taken as a justification for selecting 
agroforestry trees merely according to easily measurable, above-ground pro-
duction criteria, as this could implicate the partial sacrifice of desirable tree 
root functions [Sinclair et aI., 1994]. 
The temporal sequence of trees and crops would be an ideal solution 
to the conflict between desirable and undesirable tree root effects in agro-
forestry. However, in many situations fallow improvement techniques cannot 
replace tree-crop associations, although they may supplement them. In fallow 
improvement, root intensive tree species are probably preferable to species 
with smaller root systems, although some evidence suggests that this might 
not necessarily be so. In tree-crop associations, the situation is even more 
complicated, as a balance needs to be found between the contrasting aims of 
low competitiveness, high carbon inputs into the topsoil through root turnover 
and efficient nutrient recycling from the subsoil. Depending on the relative 
weight given to these aims, desirable tree root characteristics vary from one 
system to another: limited lateral root extension and low root length density 
in the topsoil for low competitiveness; high root mass and turnover rates in 
the topsoil for high carbon inputs; and high root length density in the subsoil 
and deep root systems for efficient nutrient recycling.

--- Page 141 ---

139 
Tree root systems are dynamic and flexible and react to environmental 
and management conditions. Thus, the genetic potential of a tree, for example 
for deep rooting, may not be fully expressed under the conditions of a given 
agroforestry system unless this is facilitated (or at least not impeded) by the 
growth conditions of the tree in this system. For this reason, the develop-
ment of 'root management' [Bowen, 1984] packages, such as suitable com-
binations of tree spacing, tillage methods, fertilization, pruning regime and 
association with competitive herbaceous plants, which allow the manipulation 
of tree root systems according to the requirements of a specific agroforestry 
situation, should be given similar priority in agroforestry research to the 
screening of species and provenances for root characteristics. 
A consequence of the contradictory nature of tree roots in agroforestry for 
research projects is that whenever possible, different aspects of tree roots 
should be studied simultaneously in order to avoid the selection of tree species 
or management options according to a single objective, because this may be 
unsuitable for meeting the requirements of another objective. For example, 
when management options for reducing the root length density of trees in 
neighboring fields are investigated, the effects on carbon cycling and nutrient 
leaching in the fields should also be quantified. Only when this is done can 
the optimum balance between the contrasting aims of reducing root compe-
tition and maximizing the soil improving effects of tree roots be found. Tree 
selection for agroforestry according to multiple objectives has been discussed 
in more detail by Sinclair et ai. [1994]. 
This review has concentrated on the direct effects of tree roots and how 
these can be manipulated. However, trees may also have important indirect 
effects on the root ecology of a cropping system. Not only may the crop root 
development be affected by associated trees, but the composition of the weed 
flora may also be changed [Siaw et aI., 1991], and this may have significant 
consequences for the quantity and distribution of the weed root systems 
[Schroth and Zech, 1995a]. These indirect effects increase further the con-
siderable complexity of the root ecology of agroforestry systems.

Soil amelioration and root symbioses of Parkia biglobosa 
(Jacq.) Benth. in West Africa 
H. TOMLINSONi , z. TEKLEHAIMANOTi , A. TRAORE2 and 
E.OLAPADE3 
I School of Agricultural and Forest Sciences, University of Wales, Bangor, Gwynedd, LL57 2UW, 
UK; 
2 Centre National de Semences Forestieres, B.P. 2682, Ouagadougou, Burkina Faso; 
3 Forest Research Institute of Nigeria, Jericho, Ibadan, Nigeria 
Key words: endomycorrhiza, mUltipurpose tree, rhizobium, soil fertility 
Abstract. Parkia biglobosa is an important mUltipurpose tree from the savanna zone of West 
Africa. It has been reported to increase soil fertility and crop yields beneath its crown. However, 
no work has been conducted to determine the role of root symbioses in soil amelioration by 
this species. The existing reports of nodulation in Parkia biglobosa are contradictory and the 
presence of mycorrhizae is not documented in the literature. 
Fieldwork was conducted at six sites in Burkina Faso and Nigeria, to investigate the 
amelioratory effect of Parkia biglobosa on soil fertility and to ascertain the occurence of root 
symbioses. Soil samples were collected from points close to the trunk, the mid-crown, crown 
edge and the open areas away from individual trees. The samples were analysed chemically 
to assess pH, organic matter, nitrogen, phosphorus and potassium content. Root samples 
were also collected from the field and examined for nodules and for evidence of mycorrhizal 
infection. 
The results of the soil analysis showed a significant amelioration for total nitrogen and 
available potassium with proximity to the tree, but organic matter, available phosphorus and soil 
pH showed no significant trend. This pattern was similar for all sites. The results also indicated 
that amelioration of nitrogen and potassium with proximity to the tree increased with tree size. 
Root nodules were absent from the roots of trees examined in the field, both in Burkina 
Faso and Nigeria. A pot experiment was set up using the soil collected from beneath the trees 
and a laboratory grown cowpea miscellany culture as rhizobial inoculum for Parkia biglobosa 
seedlings. The roots of these seedlings also showed no evidence of nodulation. Ectomycorrhizae 
were not present in roots collected from the field, but endomycorrhizal infection was prolific. 
A study of root distribution in Burkina Faso suggested that one mechanism for increased 
soil fertility beneath Parkia biglobosa is the widespread lateral root system, which redistributes 
nutrients from an extensive radius to a concentrated area beneath the crown. The root system 
is aided in the efficient uptake of nutrients by endomycorrhizae. Other possible mechanisms 
include interception of dust by the crown and deposition by throughfall and stemflow, defaca-
tion by animals beneath the tree and decomposition of leaf litter. 
Introduction 
Parkia biglobosa is a leguminous tree (sub-family Mimosoideae) indigenous 
to the savanna zones of West Africa, with a natural range extending from 
5° N to 15° N and 16° W to 32° E [Hopkins and White, 1984]. The tree is 
normally protected around homesteads [Okigbo, 1983], but is rarely actively 
regenerated and maintained [von Maydell, 1987]. It is highly valued by the

--- Page 147 ---

146 
rural populations, and is one of the commonest tree species found in farmed 
parklands in West Africa [Gijsbers et aI., 1994]. The seeds are fermented to 
produce a condiment used extensively in the preparation of local dishes. In 
Nigeria, the daily consumption of fermented seeds has been estimated at 1-17 
g per head [Campbell-Platt, 1980]. It is sometimes described as a meat or 
cheese substitute because of its savoury taste and high protein content [Booth 
and Wickens, 1988]. In addition to human nutrition, the leaves and pods are 
used for fodder; the bark, leaves and roots for medicine and the timber for 
fuel wood and light construction. The crude protein content of the leaves is 
low compared with Leucaena leucocephala [Ie Houerou, 1980], but the leaf 
biomass yield is similar to that of Gliricidia sepium at a site in humid Nigeria 
[Sabiitti and Cobbina, 1992a]. 
The mUltipurpose nature of this tree has attracted attention from agro-
foresters. However, little scientific information exists regarding the possibil-
ities for its incorporation into agroforestry systems other than the traditional 
farmed parklands, and in particular its potential for soil amelioration. 
Published reports are conflicting regarding the tree's suitability for inter-
cropping. Some researchers in Nigeria [Sabiitti and Cobbina, 1992b; Young, 
1989] have observed that crops grow better under Parkia biglobosa compared 
with the open field. In contrast, in Burkina Faso it has been reported that 
sorghum yields were only 30% of those in the open field, as light intensity 
was reduced under the large spreading crown by as much as 80% [Kessler, 
1992]. In a study in south Mali, yields of cotton, sorghum and millet under 
P. biglobosa trees decreased by as much as 60-65% compared with the open 
field. This was attributed to shading, competition from the trees' root systems 
and increased humidity under the tree resulting in susceptibility of the crop 
to fungal attack [Kater et aI., 1992]. However, the potential negative effect 
on cereal yields can be more than compensated by the income received from 
the trees' seeds. A study in Burkina Faso in 1992 showed that the average 
benefit accrued from P. biglobosa seeds was estimated to be approximately 
5,000 FCFA (US$20) per hectare, based on an average density of two trees 
per hectare [Kessler, 1994]. In addition, pruning has been shown to reduce 
the negative impacts on crop production and is commonly practised in many 
parts of Nigeria and Burkina Faso. Where trees have recently been cut or have 
died, crop yields are often higher than in the open field, apparently as a result 
of the combination of relatively high soil fertility and an absence of shading 
[Kessler, 1992]. 
The aim of this research was to investigate the potential for soil amelio-
ration under isolated P. biglobosa trees growing on farmland in Burkina Faso 
and Nigeria in three savanna ecozones; Derived (Forest-Savanna Mosaic), 
Guinea and Sudan savanna [Keay, 1959]. The methodology chosen was a 
transect, whereby a series of soil samples were taken from the base of a tree 
to open land beyond the crown [Young, 1986]. This technique has been used 
successfully in other savanna ecosystems [Belsky et aI., 1989; Bernhardt-
Reversat, 1982; Kater et aI., 1992; Kellman, 1979]. In addition to soil

--- Page 148 ---

147 
sampling, the root systems of the trees were examined for the presence of root 
symbioses to determine their contribution to soil amelioration. The genus 
Parkia has been listed as having potential for nitrogen fixation [Brewbaker 
et aI., 1984], and early workers [Allen and Allen, 1936] reported nodulation 
in Parkia africana, previously synonymous with Parkia biglobosa [Hopkins, 
1983]. However, more recent investigations have found no evidence of 
nodulation in nursery-grown P. biglobosa seedlings [Workman, 1986] or 
mature trees (J. Sprent, pers. comm.). There are no reports in the existing 
literature regarding the mycorrhizal status of the tree. 
Materials and methods 
Soil and root sampling 
In July to October 1993, fifty-four Parkia biglobosa trees were selected; nine 
in each of the six locations listed in Table 1. The trees at each location were 
selected to represent three size classes; 10-25 cm diameter at breast height 
(d.b.h.), 35-70 cm d.b.h. and> 70 cm d.b.h., with three replicates of each size 
class at each location. The trees were well isolated from other trees and located 
mainly in cultivated and fallow areas. However, it was not always possible 
to find trees of each size class growing under similar conditions at each 
location. Table 2 characterises the conditions at each location for each of the 
three size classes. 
Soil samples were taken at the four cardinal points (north, east, south and 
west) from the base of the trunk, mid-crown, the crown edge and from the 
open area outside the crown. The sample from the open was taken at 5 m from 
the crown edge for the two larger size classes, and at 2 m from the crown 
edge for the smallest size class. This was because the smaller trees were less 
well isolated from surrounding vegetation as they were generally found in 
the fallow fields as a result of natural regeneration. 
Each soil sample was taken to a depth of 30 cm and mixed thoroughly. 
The four samples for each direction were bulked according to distance from 
the tree; making a total of 216 samples. The bulked samples were combined

Secondary forest 
and a sub-sample of approximately 300 g of soil taken. 200 g of this sub-
sample was air dried for soil chemical analyses, assessments of mycorrhizal 
spores and for use as mycorrhizal inoculum in greenhouse experiments. The 
remaining 100 g was kept moist to be used as rhizobial inoculum in green-
house experiments. 
A main lateral root was selected for each tree and traced up to a distance 
of 4 m. The ability to trace a root depended on the size of the tree, the soil 
type and depth of the root. The fine roots (diameter < 2 mm) were examined 
for any evidence of nodulation. Samples of fine roots were taken along the 
length of the exposed root, washed and stored in acidic glycerol, prior to 
staining for mycorrhizal infection. 
Soil chemical analysis 
The air dried soil was analysed for soil organic matter by loss on ignition 
(LOI) at 450°C for 16 h. Soil pH was determined using a 1:2.5 (w/v) 
suspension of soil in distilled water. Total nitrogen was analysed using a 
Kjeldahl digestion; available phosphorus and potassium were extracted using 
aiM ammonium acetate solution and determined by the Olsen method and 
flame photometry respectively.

--- Page 150 ---

149 
Rhizobial inoculation 
Each 100 g sample of moist soil was mixed with sufficient perlite to fill a 
7 em diameter plastic plant pot. The perlite had been sterilised in an autoclave 
at 121°C for 15 min at 1 atm. Seeds collected from three provenances in 
Nigeria were pre-germinated on blotting paper in petri dishes. The prove-
nances were selected to represent the three ecozones under investigation; 
Derived, Guinea and Sudan savanna, to account for any possibility of 
specificity between rhizobial strains and the host plant. Once the radicle had 
emerged, the seeds were transferred into the pots containing the relevant soil 
type. Emergence of the cotyledons occurred within a week, and any seedlings 
which became susceptible to fungal disease were replaced within the first 
week. Ten seedlings from each provenance were also grown in perlite inoc-
ulated with a cowpea miscellany culture and a further ten were placed in 
uninoculated perlite as a control. The seedlings were grown in a heated green-
house with supplementary lighting. The day length was 16 hours, with a day 
time temperature of 25°C and a night time temperature of 20 °C. After four 
weeks, when all cotyledon reserves had been utilised, a modified Long Ashton 
solution, without nitrogen, was applied to the plants twice weekly. After twelve 
weeks the seedlings were harvested and the roots gently washed and examined 
for nodules. 
Assessment of mycorrhizal infection 
The roots collected from trees in the field were examined for both ectomyc-
orrhizal and endomycorrhizal infection. The root tips were stained with 0.1 % 
toludine blue and examined for any evidence of a mantle or fungal sheath. 
Further samples were stained with 0.05% Trypan blue [Phillips and Hayman, 
1970] with some recent modifications to the original methodology [Koske and 
Gemma, 1989]. The percentage of mycorrhizal infection was assessed by 
counting fields of observation of infected root length under a light microscope 
at a magnification of xlOO. 
Statistical analysis 
The data from the chemical analyses was analysed by a three-way analysis 
of variance using 'location', 'tree size' and 'distance from tree' as factors. 
The percentage mycorrhizal infection was analysed using a two-way analysis 
of variance with 'location' and 'tree size' as factors.

--- Page 151 ---

150 
Results and discussion 
Soil analyses 
Figure 1 shows soil properties at different distances from the tree at each 
location. There were significant differences between locations for all soil 
characteristics (p < 0.001), probably as a consequence of the underlying parent 
rock, and climatic and vegetation effects, but no significant interactions were 
observed between location and distance for any of the sites surveyed. Soil 
pH was lowest at Toussiana, possibly because of the combined effect of high 
leaching rates and low soil fertility, and highest in Saki and Pama. The organic 
matter content at each site can be directly correlated with percentage vegeta-
tion cover and biomass production at each of the locations [Ahn, 1970], with 
the highest percentage LOI at Saki in the loamy Derived savanna soil and 
lowest at Kano in the sandy Sudan savanna soil. The high organic matter in 
the soils at Gonse can be attributed to the samples taken from the secondary 
forest. There were no significant differences between pH and organic matter 
between samples taken at different distances from the trees within a site. At 
all sites except Kano there was an amelioration of total nitrogen with prox-
imity to the tree (p < 0.001). The lack of amelioration under the trees at 
Kano may be a consequence of high leaching rates in the sandy soil. Available 
potassium showed an significant amelioration with proximity to the tree for 
all sites (p < 0.00l). 
The results for phosphorus were extremely variable between sites. A 
correlation between the percentage LOI and phosphorus concentrations for 
each sample gave an r value of 0.05, suggesting that some of the available 
phosphorus in the soils was derived from sources other than organic matter. 
At Saki, Afaka, Pam a and Gonse there were no significant differences in 
phosphorous concentrations with distance from the tree. However, at 
Toussiana, the phosphorus concentrations were approximately double beyond 
the canopy compared with close to the trunk. Toussiana had the most impov-
erished soils of the locations analysed and it is known that fertiliser is applied 
to the cultivated fields beyond the tree crowns at this site. At Pama the reverse 
was observed, with almost twice as much phosphorus found close to the tree 
trunk compared with the open field. Cattle are known to shelter around the 
trees at this location and the elevated phosphorus concentrations may be 
attributable to deposition of faeces and urine. However, similarly high con-
centrations of nitrogen and potassium were not found at either Toussiana or 
Pama. As the ions of these elements are more mobile in the soil than phos-
phate, it is possible they may have been leached, or in the case of ammonium, 
volatilised. 
The concentrations of available phosphorus found in this survey were 
extremely low, on average 0.05-0.25 Jlg g-l soil. However, a previous soil 
survey at Gonse gave results of a similar order of magnitude [Anon, 1990]. 
These low concentrations can partly be attributed to the traditional methods

--- Page 152 ---

151 
a) pH 
b) Organic Matter 
c) Total Nitrogen 
d) Available Phosphorus 
e) Available Potassium 
Fig. 1. Soil properties according to location and distance from Parkia biglobosa trees (n = 9) 
Touss. = Toussiana.

--- Page 153 ---

152 
of cultivation used in West Africa, whereby the topsoil is overturned, intro-
ducing the less fertile subsoil into the upper soil horizons. Loss of fertile 
topsoil during the Harmattan, and the lateritic nature of the parent rock at 
some of the sites such as Toussiana, are other possible explanations. When 
concentrations of nutrients are limiting, small changes in actual nutrient 
concentrations, such as those observed for phosphorus in this survey, can have 
significant effects on plant growth. 
The results of the soil analyses according to tree size class and distance 
from the tree are shown in Fig. 2. There were significant differences for all 
soil characteristics between size classes, with more organic matter, total 
nitrogen, available phosphorus and available potassium beneath the larger trees 
(p = 0.019). Soil pH, organic matter and phosphorus showed no significant 
differences with increasing distance from the tree bole for any of the size 
classes. Only nitrogen and potassium were significantly higher with increased 
proximity to the tree (p = 0.001 and p < 0.001, respectively) and this 
amelioration was more pronounced for the larger trees. The phosphorus 
concentrations were significantly higher for all soil samples taken from the 
larger trees. This can be explained by fertilisation at Toussiana and defeca-
tion by animals at Pama, as elevated concentrations of phosphorus were found 
mostly around the larger trees at these locations. The interaction between 
location and size class was significant for all soil characteristics. For nitrogen 
(p = 0.009), phosphorus (p = 0.001) and potassium (p = 0.012) there were 
higher nutrient concentrations under the bigger trees at all locations, but the 
magnitude of the amelioration varied between sites. For pH (p < 0.001) and 
organic matter (p = 0.006), no consistent pattern was observed and the 
variation appeared to be site specific. 
Soil amelioration by savanna trees has been reported by many researchers 
[Belsky et aI., 1989; Bernhardt-Reversat, 1982; Dancette and Poulain, 1969; 
Kater et aI., 1992; Keay, 1959; Mazzarino et aI., 1991]. In each case organic 
matter, nitrogen, potassium and magnesium decreased with radial distance 
from the tree bole or centre of crown, although in one case [Kater et aI., 1992] 
available phosphorus increased away from the influence of the tree. 
Phosphorus uptake may decrease in the area outside the crown because of 
the lower tree root densities, resulting in higher phosphorus availability in 
the soil. Yet in this study, a genuine amelioration of soil fertility beneath 
Parkia biglobosa was observed only for nitrogen and potassium. 
The results also suggest that soil amelioration is augmented with increasing 
tree size, particularly in the case of nitrogen and potassium. A study of soil 
amelioration under individual savanna trees in Senegal [Bernhardt-Revers at, 
1982] showed that there was a positive correlation between tree size and 
nitrogen concentration beneath the crown, implying that soil enrichment by 
a tree continues throughout its life. Larger trees also have the ability to 
establish a larger plant-litter-soil nutrient cycle by prolonged persistence at 
one site [lsichei and Muoghalu, 1992]. However, the form of soil survey used 
in this study is limited because the vegetation varied between tree size classes

--- Page 154 ---

153 
a) pH 
b) Organic Matter 
c) Total Nitrogen 
d) Available Phosphorus 
e) Available Potassium 
Fig. 2. Soil properties according to tree size class and distance from Parkia biglobosa trees 
(n = 18); values followed by different letters are significantly different at p = 0.01.

--- Page 155 ---

154 
at each location and detailed information on the management and history at 
the different sites is lacking. Therefore, it is only possible to make general 
assumptions regarding the potential for soil amelioration under P. biglobosa 
over time. In order to make conclusive statements, it would be better to 
monitor changes in soil properties with time on the same site [Sanchez, 1987]. 
Rhizobial inoculation 
There were no nodules present on the roots of any of the P. biglobosa trees 
examined in the field, although other plants in the vicinity e.g., Erythrina 
senegalensis and groundnut (Arachis hypogaea) had active nodules. In the 
greenhouse experiment, none of the seedlings grown in soil collected from 
beneath mature P. biglobosa trees, or the seedlings inoculated with the cowpea 
miscellany rhizobial culture, showed any indication of nodulation. 
These results would indicate that either there is no suitable inoculum 
available in the soils studied, or that P. biglobosa is not capable of nodula-
tion. Parkia species in Asia are known to nodulate but no nodules have been 
found on South American species in Brazil [de Faria et aI., 1984]. Recent 
attempts to find nodules on Parkia biglobosa in West Africa have also been 
unsuccessful [Workman, 1986] (J. Sprent, pers. comm.). The trees were 
growing in soils with a very low phosphorus content, which may have been 
insufficient for nodulation [Hogberg, 1986]. In the greenhouse experiment, 
phosphorus levels in the soils were augmented with nutrient solutions but still 
nodulation did not occur. The initial low phosphorus concentrations may have 
been a critical factor in the survival of rhizobia in the soil [Virginia, 1986], 
as suitable inoculum is unlikely to persist where conditions are unfavourable. 
The inoculation with cowpea miscellany was also unsuccessful, but this may 
have been related to host-strain specificity. 
It is possible that the nodules may be ephemeral and only persist for a few 
weeks before senescing. Nodules on Acacia species grown in Sudanese soil 
were in an advanced state of decay nine weeks after inoculation [Deans et 
aI., 1993]. To determine if this were the case with Parkia biglobosa it would 
be necessary to conduct destructive harvests over a very short time course. 
In another study, nodules were found on seedlings of Faidherbia (Acacia) 
albida but not mature trees. It has been proposed that amelioration of nitrogen 
under mature trees is likely to be a result of some mechanism other than N-
fixation, possibly through redistribution by the root system [Dunham, 1992]. 
Nodules were also reported to be absent from the roots of mature Acacia 
senegal trees [Bernhardt-Reversat, 1982]. 
The indication that P. biglobosa may not nodulate, and therefore may not 
fix atmospheric nitrogen, does not imply that the tree is of little of value in 
agroforestry systems. It may be more worthwhile for a farmer to plant a tree 
with mUltipurpose uses rather than one whose sole benefits are N-fixation, 
because of the economic benefits generated. On many sites, investment in an 
extensive root system may increase tree growth and survival more efficiently

--- Page 156 ---

155 
than investment in nodules which can only alleviate nitrogen deficiency 
[Sprent, 1985]. 
Mycorrhizal infection 
There was no evidence of ectomycorrhizal infection in the roots of Parkia 
biglobosa. Approximately 85% of the roots were infected with endomycor-
rhizae, but there were no significant variations with location and tree size 
class. These results are in keeping with reports from other Mimosoideae 
[Hogberg, 1986; Hogberg, 1992]. Non-nodulated endomycorrhizal species are 
highly competitive where the availability of phosphorus is low, which at the 
same time is a severe restriction for N-fixing species [Hogberg, 1986]. 
Mycorrhizae increase the tree's absorptive capacity by enlarging the exchange 
surface available for uptake of mineral elements such as phosphorus and 
potassium [Berthelin et aI., 1991], thereby increasing the efficiency of the root 
system. They promote tolerance to drought and salinity and bind soil parti-
cles together, thus forming and maintaining soil structure [Young, 1986]. They 
also play an important role in the conversion of nutrients within the nutrient 
cycle through their ability to take up nitrogen and phosphorus directly from 
litter. It is possible that in the case of P. biglobosa, endomycorrhizae enhance 
nitrogen uptake to a sufficient level to compensate for the lack of nodula-
tion. 
Root distribution 
It has been suggested that the main mechanism by which legumes alter the 
fertility of the superficial soil layers is through root activity [Virginia, 1986]. 
Deep rooting is one possible mechanism by which this is achieved. However, 
rooting patterns in arid lands are likely to be predominantly shallow because 
of limited soil depth and soil wetting. Most data on West African root systems 
indicate that the roots are concentrated in the upper 20-50 cm of soil [Kessler 
and Breman, 1991]. Thus, redistribution of soil nutrients as a consequence of 
the lateral spread of roots is likely to be more important in concentrating 
nutrients under the tree crown compared with uptake from deep soil layers. 
Results from a study of root distribution of Parkia biglobosa in Burkina Faso 
(H. Tomlinson, unpub.), where roots were excavated to a depth of 50 cm using 
a logarithmic spiral trench [Huguet, 1973], showed that the majority of roots 
(approximately 60%) were concentrated in the upper 20 cm of soil but no 
attempt was made to assess the depth of the tap root. The study also suggested 
that the roots of P. biglobosa trees with an average crown radius of 7 m could 
extend up to 20 m from the trunk. Thus, the potential area for resource capture 
by the tree's roots is eight times that of the crown area. This is similar to 
data from other researchers. The root systems of Faidherbia (Acacia) albida 
in Zambezi riverine woodlands extended to two and a half times the crown 
radius, with a potential area for root exploitation of six times the crown area

--- Page 157 ---

156 
[Dunham, 1992]. Burkea africana roots exceeded crown spread by seven times 
and consequently an accumulation of nutrients in the sub-crown habitat was 
predicted [Rutherford, 1982]. An extensive root system such as that of P. 
biglobosa, aided by mycorrhizae, could effectively mine nutrients over a large 
area which would then accumulate beneath the tree crown. 
Other mechanisms of amelioration 
Other external inputs to the soil beneath the crown could be derived from 
atmospheric deposition or animals. Dust deposition in Kano has been reported 
at a rate of 1.2 kg ha-I phosphorus annually from the Sahara, 1000-1200 km 
away [McTamish and Walker, 1982]. Nutrients in aerially transported dust 
could reach the area below the crown via throughfall and stemflow. Nitrogen 
input in rain was recorded as 0.04 g m-2, compared with 0.57 g m-2 in through-
fall beneath Acacia senegal [Bernhardt-Revers at, 1982]. However, it was not 
stated whether this was as a result of leaching (recycling) or atmospheric 
deposition (genuine input). It is possible that the large umbrella-shaped crown 
of Parkia biglobosa would be efficient in trapping dust particles, but no studies 
have been conducted to evaluate this. The quantity of gross rainfall accounted 
for by stemflow may also vary considerably according to tree architecture. 
Stemflow in Acacia aneura accounts for 40% of gross rainfall compared with 
5-10% for most tree species [Ley ton, 1983]. It is likely that stemflow will 
be important for P. biglobosa as a result of the spreading nature of the tree's 
crown, and this may account for the increase in potassium close to the trunk. 
Inputs from animals may be derived from defecation by cattle resting in 
shade beneath the trees or from birds roosting in the crown [Belsky et aI., 
1989]. There is some evidence of this at Pama, where increased phosphorus 
concentrations are likely to be a consequence of animals sheltering by the 
trees. However this would need to be explored further, and the role of nitrogen 
and potassium present in the faeces and urine determined more clearly. 
Other possible mechanisms for soil amelioration include input of leaf litter 
and modification of the microclimate by the tree. Trees provide a range of 
carbon substances whose nutrients are released over different periods of time 
[Young, 1986]. Accumulation of carbon beneath the tree canopy may enhance 
the availability of soil nutrients as suitable carbon substrates can provide 
energy for soil micro-organisms to carry out mineralisation. The tree canopy 
also provides shade and moderates surface temperatures thus making condi-
tions more favourable for microbial activity. 
Implications for intercropping 
Traditional practices (G. Ky, pers. comm.) and information from other studies 
[Kater et aI., 1992; Kessler, 1992] suggest that planting crops beneath the 
crown of P. biglobosa trees is not a viable farming application. On the other 
hand, under conditions of marginal soil fertility it may be important to

--- Page 158 ---

157 
maximise the potential of these' islands' of enhanced nitrogen and potassium. 
Advantage could be taken of these by growing secondary crops, e.g., veg-
etables and cowpea for fodder, or dwarf sorghum, as has been suggested by 
a survey of farming practices in Nigeria (H. Tomlinson, unpub.). Information 
would then be required on the root distribution of the tree and crops in order 
to obtain maximum benefits from such intercropping systems. Methods of 
pruning could also be established to reduce the negative effects of shading. 
This could mean a compromise between arable production and P. biglobosa 
seed production, although pruning may stimulate flower production. Similar 
conclusions to these were drawn from a study of P. biglobosa in South Mali 
[Kater et aI., 1992]. 
Conclusions 
The results from the tree-open land soil transects indicate that soil nitrogen 
and potassium increase with proximity to Parkia biglobosa trees. However, 
it is likely that the majority of this amelioration takes place as a result of the 
redistribution of nutrients by an efficient laterally spreading root system. 
Therefore, the increased nutrients are present under the tree crown at the 
expense of treeless areas [Kessler and Breman, 1991]. N-fixation through 
rhizobial symbiosis would be a genuine contribution to soil enrichment, but 
there is no evidence of this. The presence of endomycorrhizal symbioses 
would, however, enable the root system to exploit the existing soil nutrients 
more effectively. Aerial and animal inputs are also likely to provide a 
contribution to soil fertility beneath the crown but it would require further 
experimentation to confirm this. 
In areas of marginal soil fertility it may be worth exploiting the area beneath 
the crowns of P. biglobosa trees, but appropriate farming practices would need 
to be adopted. 
Acknowledgements 
We wish to acknowledge staff at the Centre National de Semences Forestieres 
(CNSF), Burkina Faso, and at the Forest Research Institute of Nigeria (FRIN), 
Nigeria, for assistance in collecting the soil and root samples. The technical 
assistance of Andy Owen is gratefully acknowledged for all chemical analyses. 
This research was financed by the European Commission Directorate General 
XII, under the 'Life Sciences and Technologies for Developing Countries' 
programme.

Agroforestry Systems 30: 161-173,1995. 
© 1995 Kluwer Academic Publishers. 
Root architecture in relation to tree-soil-crop interactions 
and shoot pruning in agroforestry 
M. VAN NOORDWIJK and P. PURNOMOSIDHI 
ICRAF-S.E. Asia, P.O. Box 161, Bogor 16001, Indonesia 
Key words: competition, fractal, mUltipurpose trees, root methods, root pattern 
Abstract. Desirable root architecture for trees differs between sequential and simultaneous 
agroforestry systems. In sequential systems extensive tree root development may enhance nutrient 
capture and transfer to subsequent crops via organic pools. In simultaneous systems tree root 
development in the crop root zone leads to competition for resources. 
Fractal branching models provide relationships between proximal root diameter, close to 
the tree stem, and total root length or surface area. The main assumption is that a root branching 
proportionality factor is independent of root diameter. This was tested in a survey of 18 multi-
purpose trees growing on an acid soil in Lampung (Indonesia). The assumption appeared valid 
for all trees tested, for stems as well as roots. The proportionality factor showed a larger 
variability in roots than in stems and the effects of this variability should be further investigated. 
A simple index of tree root shallowness is proposed as indicator of tree root competitiveness, 
based on superficial roots and stem diameter. 
Pruning trees is a major way to benefit from tree products and at the same time reduce 
above-ground competition between trees and crops. It may have negative effects, however, on 
root distribution and enhance below-ground competition. In an experiment with five tree species, 
a lower height of stem pruning led to a larger number of superficial roots of smaller diameter, 
but had no effect on shoot:root ratios or the relative importance of the tap root. 
Introduction 
Competition for below-ground resources between trees and food crops may 
mask or surpass many of the advantages trees may provide for the long term 
sustainability of agroforestry systems. Competition depends on above-ground 
demand for water and nutrients, as well as possibilities for uptake. Here initial 
tests of a new method to characterize the competitive strength of trees, as far 
as it is based on root distribution, are described. 
Agroforestry systems can be biologically more productive than either pure 
crop or pure tree systems provided that trees and crops are at least partly 
complementary in the use of resources (above- and/or below-ground). If the 
tree and crop component represent approximately equal direct value to the 
farmer relative to the area occupied, a weak complementarity is sufficient to 
justify agroforestry; if trees have less or no direct value, the complementarity 
has to be pronounced [Van Noordwijk, in press]. 
Tree-(food) crop interactions in agroforestry can be spatial, temporal or 
both (Fig. 1). The lower left corner of Fig. 1 does not classify as agroforestry 
as the interactions are too weak, the upper right corner is only viable for shade-
