Sub-canopy resistance (sm") 
Fig. 7. Variation in the ratio of transpiration calculated using the Penman-Monteith formula 
and Eq. (10), (ETp.M/ETERIN) with sub-canopy surface resistance. The three lines are for a 
dominant over-storey with leaf area indices of 0.2, 1.0 and 3.0 under typical mid-day 
conditions. 
the Penman-Monteith Equation under-estimates the over-storey transpiration 
by nearly 20%. Clearly, the net effect of flux interactions from the two 
canopies over a season will depend on the constituent species surface resis-
tances and how these are affected by rainfall and soil moisture stress. 
The mechanism by which the over-storey canopy transpiration is altered 
is via the modification of the within-canopy vapour pressure deficit (Do) 
because of fluxes of heat and water vapour from the sub-canopy. When the 
sub-canopy resistance is low, transpiration from the sub-canopy is large 
enough to humidify the air around the over-storey and hence reduces its 
transpiration. When the sub-canopy resistance is high, the sensible heat flux 
from the sub-canopy is sufficient to increase the vapour pressure deficit around 
the over-storey, thereby increasing its transpiration rate. The magnitude of this 
effect is greatest when the over-storey leaf area is low, ,because the amount 
of energy reaching the sub-canopy, and hence the degree of modification of 
Do is largest.

--- Page 216 ---

218 
Discussion 
Present problems 
The resQlts presented here are sensible, with competition for light and water 
represented at broad (Hybrid/PARCH) and detailed (MAESTRO/PARCH) 
levels. The effect of fluxes of heat and water on tree-canopy transpiration have 
also been explored (ERIN). However the four models described here are being 
continuously developed and integrated. This paper records an initial stage of 
integration where several deficiencies exist. 
1. Hybrid and PARCH are linked by the exchange of input and output files, 
and are executed in different platforms and in different languages. Trees 
are assumed to take all available soil-water, rather than reducing their 
uptake as a result of competition for water from crop roots. 
2. The Hybrid/PARCH link assumes horizontally-uniform light absorption 
and water uptake. This is not realistic for widely-spaced hedges or indi-
vidual trees, and does not allow management strategies such as tree 
spacing or pruning to be investigated. 
3. Hybrid requires 74 input parameters (31 tree, 23 soil and 20 site or run 
specific). There are few, if any, tropical agroforestry species for which 
sufficient physiological information is available. This is even more the 
case for MAESTRO, which requires 112 parameters (55 tree, 4 soil and 
53 site or run specific). 
4. Both Hybrid and PARCH contain rudimentary routines to allow soil and 
tissue nitrogen levels to control growth, but the routines are not compat-
ible. 
5. The MAESTRO/PARCH link represents tree-root distribution in three-
dimensions, but tree-roots were assumed to be fixed through the growing 
season, and do not respond to areas of high water-content or permeability. 
The drying front around a tree will expand horizontally as well as verti-
cally, and by ignoring dynamic root growth the competitive effect of trees 
is overestimated close to the stem, and underestimated further out. 
6. MAESTRO is one of the most sophisticated three-dimensional models of 
a tree canopy. It has high spatial and temporal resolution, and provides a 
sophisticated approach to light interception and photosynthesis. However, 
it does not yet partition photosynthate or grow the tree. This is not a 
problem in current simulations, where leaf area has been held constant, 
but will be a limitation in the future when it is desired to test the effect 
of a developing tree canopy on crop yield. 
7. PARCH has so far only been fully validated for sorghum [Bradley et al., 
1994], although data exists for both millet and maize. 
8. No effects of the fluxes of water vapour, heat or CO2 from the crop on 
tree photosynthesis or transpiration (some of which are included in ERIN) 
are yet included in MAESTRO or Hybrid.

--- Page 217 ---

219 
9. ERIN has yet to include the effects of spatially heterogeneous canopies 
and needs to be linked to a complete agroforestry water balance model. 
Future development: HyPAR 
The main aim is to develop the Hybrid/PARCH model to become a compre-
hensive physical agroforestry model, incorporating all the essential processes 
required to design optimum tree-crop systems which maximise crop and timber 
yields in particular locations. A new fully-coupled model is already in 
development, and will be called HyPAR. 
Hybrid already couples the soil-plant-atmosphere system, and will be 
developed within HyPAR to: 
(i) 
enable the canopy to be disaggregated to simulate individual trees and 
hedgerows (simplifying MAESTRO by using approaches suggested in 
Jackson and Palmer [1979] and Thomley and Johnson [1990]); 
(ii) include more than one soil stratum, enabling different patterns of water 
and nitrogen abstraction to be examined in response to different vertical 
and horizontal rooting patterns, (using techniques in PARCH and 
FUSSIM2 [Heinen and De Willigen 1992]); and 
(iii) allow essential micro-climatic feedbacks to occur between the crop and 
tree canopies (using techniques in ERIN). 
PARCH has been translated into FORTRAN and partially integrated with 
Hybrid to form an initial version of HyPAR which allows feedbacks between 
tree water uptake, crop water uptake and soil water pools on a daily time-step. 
MAESTRO is being used in conjunction with PARCH to examine the effect 
of canopy pruning on tree photosynthesis, crop-growth and overall water 
consumption. Simple techniques to deal with canopy clumping are being 
developed within ERIN. 
Once HyPAR is complete, its predictions will be compared with observa-
tions of biomass and crop yield, both regionally and at specific sites. Different 
tree-crop combinations will be parameterised, and management systems 
explored in conjunction with bio-economic models. 
Acknowledgements 
The ODA Forestry Research Programme (FRP) Grant R5651 has sponsored 
integration of Hybrid, MAESTRO, PARCH and ERIN. Development of 
PARCH has been funded by the Agronomy & Cropping Systems and the 
Resource Assessment Programmes of ODA. Work on ERIN has been sup-
ported by the ODA-FRP Project R4853.

Agroforestry Systems 30: 223-234, 1995. 
© 1995 Kluwer Academic Publishers. 
A framework for a modular modelling approach for 
agroforestry 
R. I. MUETZELFELDT 
The University of Edinburgh, Institute of Ecology and Resource Management, Darwin Building, 
King's Buildings, Mayfield Road, Edinburgh EH9 3JU, Scotland, UK 
Key words: modelling environment, simulation 
Abstract. There are a number of problems with conventional methods for implementing models 
of agroforestry systems, including the difficulties of building, modifying and understanding 
them. One way of addressing these problems is to adopt a modular modelling approach, in which 
a model is constructed by linking a number of submodels, which in tum may consist of smaller 
computational units. 
This paper presents a basic framework for the design of a modular modelling approach 
suitable for agroforestry modelling. This approach is based on a close correspondence between 
the notion of 'module' in modelling and of 'subroutine' in a programming implementation of 
a model, so that selecting and linking modules is analogous to choosing and issuing calls to 
subroutines. This approach has the potential for enabling the complex modelling structures 
that are required for agroforestry modelling, such as spatial aspects and the representation of 
individual trees, to be handled in a consistent and uniform manner. 
The approach requires that subroutines should be held as symbolically-represented struc-
tures, rather than just source code in a programming language, so that they can be referenced 
as discrete units, and internally modified by symbol-processing operations. It is shown that the 
logic-programming language Prolog is well-suited to the task of representing subroutines in 
template form, ready to be fleshed out and combined into a single program in response to the 
selections made by a user. Prolog's suitability for the development of a user-friendly model 
design interface is also discussed, enabling models to be constructed simply by selecting options 
from a menu of design choices. 
Introduction 
The value of computer simulation modelling in agroforestry research and 
management is well recognised [Muetzelfeldt and Sinclair, 1993]. Models 
make it possible to test one's understanding of how agroforestry systems 
function; to predict the outcome of particular agroforestry systems; and to 
explore alternative management regimes. 
Such models are usually implemented in a conventional programming 
language (such as Fortran), and their design is communicated to the rest of 
the research community in the 'Model Description' part of a report or journal 
paper. The use of a programming language for implementation brings with it 
a number of problems, including the length of time it takes to implement and 
debug the program, the difficulty of exploring alternative but equally plau-
sible model formulations, and the difficulty of elucidating the logic of model 
design from the program listing. The use of English text for communicating

--- Page 221 ---

224 
model design is also unsatisfactory: such descriptions are frequently incom-
plete or ambiguous, and there are no agreed standards for describing model 
structure. 
It would seem that there is a need for a computer-based environment to 
support the modelling process. Such environments are common-place in other 
domains, such as engineering and architecture, where they provide the 
modeller with a toolkit of model-building elements that correspond to the sorts 
of real-world elements appropriate to the domain, such as pumps and valves 
in chemical engineering. In contrast, the only alternatives to programming 
available to the agroforestry modeller are the use of simulation languages or 
diagram-based modelling software. None of these enables the modeller to 
design a model using agroforestry concepts, nor do they allow for all the 
model-design features required for agroforestry modelling. 
The aim of this paper is to present a framework for the development of a 
modular modelling system for agroforestry modelling. The ideas in this frame-
work underpin an ongoing project to implement such a system. This project 
uses the Prolog programming language both for representation of modules and 
models, and also for implementation of the various procedures involved in 
constructing and working with models. For those readers unfamiliar with 
Prolog, a number of introductory textbooks are available, for example [Malpas, 
1987; Sterling and Shapiro, 1985], while an introduction to Prolog in the 
context of ecological modelling is given in [Muetzelfeldt et aI., 1988]. 
Modelling in an agroforestry context 
For present purposes, it is assumed that agroforestry is concerned with growing 
two types of plant on a given area of land. The larger and more permanent 
plant type is referred to as 'tree', recognising that in some contexts this plant 
might be more appropriately labelled as 'shrub'; and the smaller and less 
permanent plant type is labelled 'crop', recognising that in some contexts 
this might be (for example) grass which is grazed by sheep or cattle. Tree 
and crop might be separated temporally, in a rotational system, or might 
occupy the same area at the same time in a number of ways, such as in blocks, 
in rows, or with the crop growing amongst dispersed trees. 
The management objective is, in general, to optimise (in some sense) the 
return from such a system, where the return is characterised in terms of the 
yield and service roles from the crop and the tree components, set against 
inputs of labour and money. Management choices include the choice of tree 
and crop species and varieties, temporal and spatial planting patterns, and 
the type, timing, location and intensity of management actions such as 
weeding, pruning and mulching. 
It is implicit in the term 'agroforestry' that there will be interactions 
between the tree and crop components of the system. Individual interactions 
may be negative, such as shading in a light-limited environment, or positive,

--- Page 222 ---

225 
such as nitrogen fixation by the tree benefiting the crop. The adoption of an 
agroforestry system is based on the assumption that the positive effects will 
outweigh the negative ones, such that the net return to the farmer will be greater 
when the two components are grown together and these interactions occur. 
The aim of modelling agroforestry systems is generally to explore alter-
native management options through simulation, in order to permit a reasoned 
assessment of which options are likely to be preferable. It follows that the 
model must produce, as output, estimates of those factors that are important 
to the farmer (such as yield); that it must permit the setting of management 
inputs corresponding to those that the farmer would be able to vary; and that 
the outputs of the model for given management inputs should faithfully mimic 
those that would be obtained for the corresponding real-life system managed 
in the same way. In order to achieve these objectives, agroforestry modelling 
must be capable of taking into account the key features of agroforestry systems 
identified above: that is, the spatial and temporal interactions between dif-
ferent types of component. 
Reasons for adopting a modular modelling approach 
There are a number of reasons for preferring a modular modelling approach 
over a conventional programming approach for agroforestry modelling. These 
are outlined below in terms of what can be achieved through the adoption of 
such an approach. 
• Simplification of the task of designing and implementing models - Model 
construction becomes a design activity, achieved by selecting components 
and linking them together, rather than a programming activity. 
• Simplification of the task of modifying model structure - The structure 
(design decisions) in models implemented in a programming language 
readily become ossified in the program used to implement the model. The 
need for sensitivity analysis on model parameters is widely recognised. A 
modular approach makes it much easier to undertake structural sensitivity 
analysis and to explore alternative but equally plausible model designs. 
• Increased co-operation between modellers and researchers - An improved 
modelling environment will encourage modellers and researchers to interact 
to a greater extent than at present in the design of appropriate models. 
• Increased reusability - Internationally, a huge amount of effort goes into 
determining modelling relationships, and in constructing models and sub-
models. The adoption of a common modular modelling approach would 
greatly facilitate the re-use of these modelling components by others, 
leading to a great reduction in overlap of effort. This is particularly perti-
nent in an interdisciplinary subject area such as agroforestry. 
• Support for the modelling process - A modular approach, combined with 
standard ways for documenting modules, opens up the way for computer-

--- Page 223 ---

226 
based guidance in the model-construction process. For example, the user 
could specify context (country, species) and required outputs, and the 
computer could suggest one or more suitable modules. 
• Increased clarity of communication of model structure - A modular 
approach would make it much easier for others to understand the structure 
of a given model, since it lends itself to diagrammatic presentation, provides 
a disciplined and hierarchical format, and guarantees a direct correspon-
dence between model description and implementation. This in tum is 
necessary if a model is to be considered as part of the scientific process, 
and again aids the appreciation of models amongst scientists across disci-
plines. 
• The ability to investigate model components separately - It is difficult, 
using conventional methods of model implementation, to explore the behav-
iour of individual relationships or submodels; an activity which is crucial 
if one is to have confidence in the overall model behaviour. A modular 
approach means that all the tools provided for exploring model behaviour 
(run control, sensitivity or uncertainty analysis) can as readily be applied 
to individual components of the model. 
Conceptual basis of a modular modelling approach 
There are a number of real-world systems that provide useful analogies for 
thinking about modularity. For example, there are the familiar examples of 
modular electronic systems: a radio consists of a number of circuit boards, 
each of which performs some designated task and which contains lower-level 
electronic units, such as transistors and integrated-circuit chips. At any level, 
modules are connected though wires or copper track. 
Computer-aided design (CAD) software has been developed in a number 
of subject areas, supporting a modular approach. For example, there is 
software that supports the process of designing electronic circuits, in which 
components are selected from 'bins', 'plugged into' a circuit board, and 'wired 
up', operations directly paralleling the real-world operations. A similar 
modular approach is used in CAD software in architecture. In engineering, a 
number of modelling systems based on block diagramming have been 
developed for systems analysis, in which the basic modules correspond to 
mathematical operations, such as multiplication and integration. These 
modules can in tum be combined together to give higher-level modules. These 
systems clearly demonstrate the effectiveness of graphical, mouse-based user 
interfaces for supporting the design process. 
A generic modular modelling environment, SIMPLEX, has been developed 
[Eschenbacher and Schmidt, 1987], and applied in an environmental model-
ling context [Wittman, 1994]. This software represents an explicit commit-
ment to a hierarchical modelling approach. 
For people with programming experience, the concept of a 'subroutine' is

--- Page 224 ---

227 
a useful one for thinking about modularity: a program consists of some calls 
to subroutines, each of which performs some designated task and which may 
itself call lower-level subroutines. At any level, subroutines communicate 
through the passing of data structures, such as variables or arrays. A diagram 
showing the subroutine calling sequence of a model is frequently used to 
illustrate the submodel structure of the model: for example, PARCH [Bradley 
and Crout, 1992] and MAESTRO [Wang and Jarvis, 1990]. 
Indeed, modellers frequently respond to discussions on modular model-
ling by asserting that they work in this manner already, by the extensive use 
of subroutines in implementing a model. Why not, they argue, simply 
encourage good structured programming practices, and the development of a 
common subroutine library, so that models could be constructed simply by 
issuing the appropriate subroutine calls? However, with such an approach, 
model construction would still be a programming activity, and the programmer 
would have responsibility for ensuring correct matching of subroutine 
arguments. Moreover, modifying the functions called within library routines 
would be difficult. In addition, it would be hard to provide support for the 
process of selecting appropriate modules, and there would be no guarantee 
that model documentation would correspond to the design of the implemented 
model. These are, however, problems with current methods for working with 
subroutines, rather than with the subroutine concept itself. 
The modular modelling approach presented here is based on the idea of 
developing a user-friendly interface for manipulating subroutine-like modules. 
Manipulation refers to the processes of selecting subroutines from a sub-
routine library, constructing subroutines in terms of calls to other subroutines, 
linking the input and output arguments of subroutines, and modifying the 
internal structure of previously-programmed subroutines. The development of 
an interface for supporting these tasks requires us to view a subroutine as a 
symbolically-represented structure, which can be referenced as a single unit, 
internally modified by symbol-processing operations, and reasoned with using 
some form of symbolic logic. 
Conventional implementation of an agroforestry model 
In order to provide a basis for discussion, it is useful to consider a model 
which is simple enough for purposes of exposition, but yet which captures a 
number of the significant features of agroforestry systems. This will be 
presented as an outline program, which will serve as a reference example for 
considering various ways in which concepts of modularity can be supported 
by an appropriate modelling environment. 
The following pseudo-program represents a simple model of a tree-crop 
system, in which: 
-
the tree component is represented by the height of a set of individual trees;

--- Page 225 ---

228 
-
the crop component is represented as crop biomass on each of a number 
of grid squares; 
-
the interaction between the two is only in terms of tree shading reducing 
crop growth rate. 
A row of three dots ( ... ) represents parts of the programming (in this case, 
argument lists for subroutines) that are omitted for the sake of clarity. 
1. call init ( ... ) 
2. for year = 1 to 20 
3. call shading ( ... ) 
4. call tree_growth ( ... ) 
5. call crop_growth ( ... ) 
6. call tree_update ( ... ) 
7. call crop_update ( ... ) 
8. call display ( ... ) 
9. next year 
There are several points to note about the programming features shown here. 
First, the program consists of calls to subroutines, plus some control struc-
tures (in this case, a loop over years). The extensive use of subroutines reflects 
a certain conventional view of 'modularity', and may be taken as representing 
a good structured-programming solution. It provides the basis for the approach 
to modular modelling explored in this paper. 
Second, the tree component of the system is handled by not just one but 
two subroutines, one for calculating the growth (height increment) of each of 
the trees, the other for updating height with the new growth. The same is 
true for the crop. This reflects the requirement that, in general, all the rates 
in a system should be calculated before updating the state variables [Supit et 
aI., 1994]. It is not uncommon to find published model flow-charts using a 
single subroutine call for each component with the main time loop, with both 
rate calculations and updating of state variables occurring during a single call, 
but this can lead to significant errors in modelling processes that operate in 
parallel. 
Third, some of the sequencing of the subroutine calls is important, while 
some is not. 'shading' needs to be called before 'crop_growth', and the 
'growth' routines need to be called before the 'update' routines; but the 
ordering of the two 'growth' routines and the two 'update' routines is irrel-
evant and has no effect on model behaviour. 
Fourth, this apparently simple program can encompass a model of consid-
erable complexity. For example, the subroutine 'shading', which takes as input 
data structures representing the size and location information for all the trees 
and returns a shading value for each grid square, could incorporate a sophis-
ticated light interception model. Similarly, the tree growth routine could be 
based on a detailed process-based representation of the mechanisms of photo-
synthesis and partitioning of assimilates. Thus, even a simple main program

--- Page 226 ---

229 
such as this is capable of modelling complex spatial, organisational and 
geometric aspects of agroforestry systems. 
Clearly, this example illustrates a certain notion of 'modularity': the one 
task of initialising and performing the calculations for a complex tree-crop 
model has been broken down into a number of subtasks. However, it might 
also be expected that other aspects of modularity might be indicated: for 
example, the notion of re-useability, with perhaps the 'shading' routine being 
copied from another program; or the notion of choice, with perhaps the 
program writer selecting one of several available tree growth routines. 
Conventionally, working with these notions of modularity is a program-
ming task: the programmer must edit a program to replace a call to one routine 
with a call to another; calls to additional routines must be edited into the 
program; and changes to the argument list in subroutine calls must be made 
as required to agree with the specified argument lists of the routines included. 
Moreover, this requirement applies equally to the subroutines called by the 
main program, to the subroutines that they call, and those called in turn at 
lower levels. These programming tasks require considerable skill, are error-
prone and time-consuming activities, and are a long way removed from any 
notion of model construction through interactive module selection and linkage. 
In order to move towards a subroutine-based modular modelling environ-
ment, it is necessary to view a subroutine as a structured item of informa-
tion, not as text entered by a programmer using a text editor. Viewed in this 
way, it is then possible to develop software tools that can locate, select, insert 
and delete subroutines and - equally importantly - that can modify the internal 
structure of a subroutine to ensure consistency with the rest of the program. 
Conventional programming languages are ill-suited for this task. However, 
languages developed within Artificial Intelligence for reasoning with quali-
tative, symbolically-represented information are ideally suited. The logic-pro-
gramming language Prolog is particularly appropriate, since it can be used for 
representing subroutines themselves as structured items of information, for 
guiding selection of appropriate subroutines given the goals of the model and 
the choices made so far, and for undertaking any re-writing of the subrou-
tines to ensure program consistency. 
Interactive model design 
There are two ways in which the model design process can be supported within 
a subroutine-based modular modelling environment. The first is that the user 
can be given choices corresponding exactly to the modules (subroutines) them-
selves. The second is that the user can be asked to specify features of the 
system that are to be captured, and these can then be used behind-the-scenes 
to select appropriate modules. 
Using the first approach, it is merely necessary to ensure that the user is 
presented at each stage with a choice of modules that are consistent with those

--- Page 227 ---

230 
already selected. There is an obvious hierarchy in the calling structure of 
subroutines, so it is natural to exploit this in interacting with the user. 
Prolog lends itself very naturally to representing a set of choices that are 
contingent on those already made. For example, the first two of the following 
four Prolog 'clauses' (statements) assert that there are two subroutines 
('growthl' and 'growth2') that are compatible with the 'tree_growth' slot in 
the program called 'mainl' (which can be taken as being the program given 
above). The next two clauses assert that there are two subroutines ('photosyn5' 
and 'photosyn7') that are compatible with the photosynthesis call in the 
'growth2' subroutine (a process-based submodel of plant growth). 
possible_sub(mainl ,tree_growth,growthl). 
possible_sub(mainl,tree_growth,growth2). 
possible_sub(growth2,photosyn,photosyn5). 
possible_sub(growth2,photosyn,photosyn7). 
Given such a representation, it is straightforward to write a Prolog program 
that can present the user with a menu of all the outstanding choices. With 
modern Prologs, this can be done in a GUI (graphical user interface) envi-
ronment, enabling the user to build a model simply by selecting options from 
scrolling menus using the mouse. The user can be supported in making this 
choice by the provision of extra information on each module: again, it is a 
straightforward task in Prolog to associate some descriptive text with the 
symbolic name of each module. 
The second approach involves the computer identifying appropriate 
modules in response to the user specifying desired features of the model. 
This task is directly analogous to that of identifying a plant from its attrib-
utes, or a disease from its symptoms, and can be handled by a variety of 
techniques developed for such purposes, such as look-up tables, decision trees 
and expert systems. The relational and rule-based features of Prolog make it 
well-suited for such tasks, and it has been used to develop a large number of 
expert-system-type programs. 
Prolog representation of subroutines as modules 
The argument for basing a modular modelling approach on the handling of 
subroutines as modules depends critically on the ability of software to manip-
ulate the source code of the main program and of subroutine blocks. The aim 
of this section is to demonstrate the feasibility of such manipulation. 
In order to provide a sufficiently concise example for presentation, it has 
been restricted to a model of just the tree component of an agroforestry system. 
The main program therefore does not include the shading and crop aspects of 
the previous example. Tree growth is modelled by a simple logistic growth 
term. Also, some small liberties have been taken with the programming aspects 
(for example, the exclusion of dimensioning information for arrays). These

--- Page 228 ---

231 
restrictions have been done only for purposes of clarity, and in no way detract 
from the central message of this section, that it is indeed possible to manip-
ulate subroutine source code symbolically in ways that result in a consistent 
and coherent simulation program. 
In this approach, each subroutine available for inclusion in a model is 
represented in template form in a Prolog clause headed by the predicate 
'module'. In the Prolog program given below, there are two of these: a module 
called 'mainl' (lines 2-15), and a module called 'growl' (lines 16-24). Each 
'module' clause is a rule, with the general form: 
module(Name,Template) :- Conditions. 
in which the symbol :- is read as the word 'if'. The Template is represented 
as a list (enclosed in [ ... D of terms that are closely related to program 
statements. Thus, the Template for 'mainl' is in lines 3-8, and that for 'growl' 
is in lines 17-21. The task of the Conditions component is (despite its name) 
mainly to flesh out elements of the template with particular variable and array 
names, depending on the suite of modules selected by the user. 
Line 1 is a clause which contains the list of modules selected by the user: 
this list is the result of interaction with the user interface, as described in the 
previous section. The 'compatible_with' predicate (line 25) indicates which 
subroutines can fit into a particular slot of a particular calling routine or main 
program, while the 'argumenclist' predicate (line 26) indicates separately the 
state variable, parameter and rate variable arguments for each subroutine. 
1. usecselected([main 1 ,grow 1 D. 
2. module(mainl, 
3.

4. 
(for year = 1 to 20), 
5. 
(call Sub(Args2)),

7. 
(call display(State_ vars)), 
8. 
(next year))):-

26. argumenUist(grow 1 ,[height(ntree)], [r,maxheight] ,[growth(ntree) D. 
The following pseudo-code program for the selected modules is generated 
from the above set of templates with an extra 10 or so lines of Prolog, their 
main functions being to declare words such as 'subroutine' and 'call' as 
operators, and to step through the modules in the selected list. The corre-
spondence between the program templates and the generated program can be 
seen by comparing the template for 'main 1 ' in lines 3-8 above with the 
generated program in lines 1-6 below, and similarly for' grow I' (lines 17-21 
above with lines 7-11 below). 
Generated program: 
I. call init([height(ntree),r,maxheight]) 
2. for year = 1 to 20 
3. call grow 1 ([height(ntree ),r,maxheight,growth(ntree)]) 
4. call update([height(ntree ),growth(ntree)]) 
5. call display([height(ntree)]) 
6. next year 
7. subroutine grow 1 ([height(ntree),r,maxheight,growth(ntree)]) 
8. for tree = 1 to ntree 
9. growth(tree) = r*height(tree)*(I-height(tree)/maxheight) 
10. next tree 
11. end sub 
As stated above, a number of simplifications have been made for presenta-
tion purposes. These include the convention that 'height(ntree)' is used to 
denote that 'height' is a one-dimensional array with 'ntree' elements; the 
absence of specification statements; and the inclusion of square brackets in 
argument lists. For the same reasons, the example does not demonstrate how 
the programming code for the subroutines 'init', 'update' and 'display' would 
be generated, though in fact that would be done using similar code-genera-
tion methods to those shown here. There are also numerous refinements to 
this system that can be envisaged, such as enabling the system to handle 
subroutines nested to any depth, and undertaking more rigorous type checking 
on the subroutine argument lists.

--- Page 230 ---

233 
Discussion 
In this paper, one possible approach to the problem of designing a modular 
modelling system for agroforestry has been presented. It has two key features. 
First, there is a close correspondence between the notion of 'module' in 
modelling and 'subroutine' in programming. In this approach, a library module 
corresponds to the subroutine itself, while an instance of a module in a model 
corresponds to a call to a subroutine, binding the subroutine arguments to 
particular variables or values. This correspondence enables the generation of 
a conventional program from the model specification, with a one-to-one rela-
tionship between a module and subroutine. This greatly simplifies the task of 
being able to generate a runable version of the model. It should also help in 
persuading practising modellers that the modelling environment works cor-
rectly, since they can check through the generated program. 
Second, arbitrarily complex ways of representing the components in the 
system can be supported, such as grid squares and layers for soil water, or an 
individual-based tree component. This is achieved by allowing modules to 
contain arbitrary data structures (e.g., 2-D arrays), and arbitrary amounts 
of programming in a conventional programming language for performing 
calculations with these data structures. This is necessary in order to be able 
to make use of the large number of agroforestry-relevant submodels that 
already exist as program subroutines, and to capture important spatial inter-
actions in agroforestry systems. 
This approach results in a system that corresponds closely to everyday 
notions of modularity in the context of modelling, such as notions of 
'submodel' and 'choice of light response function'. There is little doubt that 
the implementation and widespread adoption of such a modular modelling 
system would greatly enhance the effectiveness of agroforestry modelling: 
reusability of model components would be much simpler; alternative model 
formulations could be readily compared and evaluated; and it would be much 
easier to understand other people's models. A common objection to this 
approach - that it would constrain the modeller - has some theoretical merit, 
but in practice most modellers would probably have greater flexibility, rather 
than less, since they would have access to pre-programmed representations, 
and so would be less constrained by the limitations of their own programming 
ability or awareness of modelling approaches.

Agroforestry Systems 30: 235-248, 1995. 
© 1995 Kluwer Academic Publishers. 
Incorporation of indigenous knowledge and perspectives in 
agroforestry development 
Part 1: Review of methods and their application 
D. H. WALKER', F. L. SINCLAIR2 and B. THAPA3 
I Division of Tropical Crops and Pastures, CSIRO, P.O. Aitkenvale, Queensland 4814, Australia; 
2 School of Agricultural and Forest Sciences, University of Wales, Bangor, Gwynedd, LL57 2UW, 
UK; 3 Pakhribas Agricultural Centre, clo BTCO, P.O. Box 106, Kathmandu, Nepal 
Key words: knowledge-based systems, participatory rural appraisal, rapid rural appraisal 
Abstract. Calls for the effective integration of indigenous knowledge and perspective into 
agroforestry are increasingly familiar in agroforestry programmes. This is the result of a need 
to better target research, ethical concerns about participation and power and the recognition 
that indigenous knowledge is a potentially powerful source of understanding that may often be 
complementary to scientific knowledge. 
Incorporating indigenous knowledge into development may be achieved through farmer 
participation in research planning and implementation, external survey of local needs as a basis 
for planning research or the active synthesis of indigenous and scientific knowledge (in addition 
to collaboration between the scientist and the farmer) in order to capitalise on their potential 
complementarity. 
It is argued that active synthesis has received inadequate attention. In part this is because it 
demands rigorous analysis of indigenous and scientific knowledge. This is an exacting process 
and requires effective means of explicitly representing the knowledge concerned. An approach 
to this task is briefly introduced. A case study description and evaluation can be found in the 
accompanying paper [Thapa et aI., 1995 (this issue)]. 
Introduction 
Calls for more effective integration of indigenous knowledge and perspec-
tive into development are increasingly familiar to those working in agro-
forestry. Although some past research has placed considerable emphasis on 
local knowledge [see e.g., Richards, 1939], mainstream interest is relatively 
recent. Brokensha et al. [1980] provided a key impetus by bringing together 
a set of case studies and coherently arguing the need for enhanced under-
standing of local knowledge in planning and implementing development 
activities. Growth in the application of indigenous knowledge to rural research 
and development over the succeeding decade and a half is evident from the 
establishment of three international, two regional and nine national networked 
indigenous knowledge resource centres [CIRAN, 1993]. This ground-swell of 
activity can be attributed to: 
• the demonstrable need to target research to client needs more effectively 
if the results of the research are to achieve high rates of adoption;

--- Page 233 ---

236 
• a rise in the importance accorded to ethical issues relating to participation 
and power; and 
• the recognition that indigenous knowledge, particularly in relation to 
agroforestry, may be a powerful resource in its own right and comple-
mentary to knowledge available from scientific sources. 
Targeting research and development activities to client needs 
It is widely acknowledged that many of the technological solutions that have 
been proposed to address problems in rural communities have been compro-
mised by a failure to take into account local knowledge, perspective and 
practise [Chambers, 1983; Warren and Cashman, 1988; Chambers et al., 1989; 
Haverkort et al., 1991]. The role of farmers' perspectives in addressing this 
problem has been recognised by increasing numbers of researchers and 
development professionals, who point out that effective external interventions 
can best be achieved 'once we know what they already know, and what else 
might be most useful to add to their store of knowledge and tools' [Rocheleau, 
1987]. 
A variety of approaches to better targeting research to client needs have 
been espoused. The defining feature of all such approaches is some form of 
interaction with the target communities during project planning and imple-
mentation. 
Ethical concerns 
There is a growing awareness of the extent to which colonisers, administra-
tors and social scientists in the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries 
established negative attitudes towards indigenous peoples and their knowl-
edge [Slikkerveer, 1989; Warren, 1989]. Negative attitudes to indigenous 
knowledge persist and Hatch's assertion [1976] that' even the supposedly 
enlightened development profession suffers from an entrenched superiority 
complex' is still relevant. These negative values are often reinforced by the 
educated elite of developing countries who are strongly influenced by the 
current dominance of Western science [Compton, 1989] which is firmly linked 
to wealth, power and prestige [Chambers, 1983]. 
Development programmes are usually conceived within the framework of 
formal science and Western values. Until very recently the values of the rural 
agricultural practitioner were barely taken into account because societies that 
were perceived as primitive were assumed to have primitive modes of thought. 
The failure of many agricultural projects and technology packages imposed 
by the industrialised world appeared to reinforce these negative attitudes. 
The slow rate of assimilation of new technology and cropping schemes was 
attributed to local inertia rather than the failure to take account of the 
knowledge, experience and needs of local people upon whom they were 
imposed [Brokensha et al., 1980; Chambers, 1983; Richards, 1985; Rocheleau, 
1987; Warren et al., 1989]. From this perspective, research into indigenous

--- Page 234 ---

237 
knowledge aims to validate the expertise of rural practitioners and to 
encourage and inform a problem-solving dialogue between them and the 
scientific community in order to establish a more constructive and equitable 
approach to development. 
Indigenous knowledge as a resource for science 
In agroforestry as a science, there remain many more questions than answers. 
Much scientific investigation of the ecology of agroforestry is intended to 
produce knowledge relevant to farmers in managing agroforestry practices 
[Gholz, 1987]. Indeed, recent reviews of research agendas in agroforestry 
indicate that future activities, particularly where process-based, will be 
increasingly productive of accurate, predictive understanding of interactions 
within agroforestry practices relevant to farmers [Anderson and Sinclair, 
1993]. Nevertheless, research has yet to address many of the issues pertinent 
to the improvement of agroforestry practices [Nair, 1990]. 
Experimental research, although an important means of improving the 
information upon which farmers make decisions, cannot be relied upon to 
address all the pertinent issues over the next decade. With this in mind, it is 
worth considering whether relying on experimental scientific methodology 
alone is, in fact, the most expedient, effective and efficient way to fill gaps 
in current understanding about the functioning of agroforestry systems. In 
particular, it is worth seeking approaches that could complement experimental 
investigation, leading to a more rapid understanding of the functioning of 
agroforestry systems. One approach to filling the gap between what it is 
desirable to know about agroforestry practises in addressing development 
needs and what knowledge is currently held by research and extension staff, 
as a result of their professional training and from the scientific literature, is 
to mobilise and synthesise currently available knowledge from all sources 
more effectively. It is proposed that one of the richest but least utilised sources 
of knowledge available to the development professional is the indigenous 
knowledge of farmers who practice agroforestry. 
For whatever combination of these different motivations, reference to 
indigenous knowledge and local participation is increasingly common in agro-
forestry-based project proposals. A considerable literature justifies this 
emphasis by arguing the case for making more effective use of indigenous 
knowledge in development [Brokensha et aI., 1980; Chambers et aI., 1989; 
Warren, 1991a; Warren et aI., 1994]. This is supported by work that has 
characterised and explored indigenous knowledge in a range of contexts -
including bibliographic reviews of indigenous knowledge in relation to private 
tree management [Mathias-Mundy et aI., 1992], in sustainable agriculture 
[Warren, 1991b] and natural resources more generally [Warren, 1991a]. Much 
of this work remains anecdotal and descriptive but provides a persuasive basis 
for pursuing a more rigorous investigation of the nature and extent of indige-
nous knowledge.

--- Page 235 ---

238 
Consideration of the literature demonstrates that indigenous knowledge and 
perspective can impact on agroforestry development at multiple levels and 
stages. Nevertheless, it is also apparent that making effective practical use 
of indigenous knowledge and perspective in designing and implementing 
development activities is not easily done. 
In this paper, a range of approaches to the integration of indigenous knowl-
edge and perspective into development activities are reviewed. Some strengths 
and weaknesses and appropriate contexts for application are indicated. It is 
argued that the active synthesis of indigenous ecological knowledge with 
scientific knowledge has a potentially important role to play but has received 
inadequate attention. An approach to this demanding process is briefly intro-
duced. This approach is described and evaluated in more detail through case 
study evaluation in an accompanying paper [Thapa et aI., 1995 (this issue)]. 
Approaches to incorporating indigenous knowledge and perspective 
Three broad categories of approach to incorporating indigenous knowledge 
and perspective into development can be distinguished: 
• approaches based on the active participation of local experts in develop-
ment activities in which no understanding, per se, of indigenous knowl-
edge is required to be sought by development professionals; 
• interaction with local people to generate descriptions of actual practice and 
constraints on the system in order to improve development professionals' 
understanding of the target community's needs; and 
• interaction with local people to investigate indigenous explanation of the 
ecological functioning of the system, in order to facilitate the integration 
of local and scientific knowledge and produce a more complete and useful 
resource base for use by professionals or local people in the incremental 
improvement of land-use systems. 
Local participation in research 
The integration of target communities into the research process is actively 
espoused and implemented by growing numbers of development professionals 
[Richards 1985; Chambers et al., 1989; Amanor 1989; Scoones and Thompson, 
1994]. 
Rocheleau [1987] shows that local experts can often adapt new methods 
to local conditions better than outsiders, that local control of change often 
matters more than rapid transformation, and that new technologies are often 
most successful when built upon existing practice and knowledge. Further-
more, it has been argued that local participation can complement scientific 
manpower: local people, as competent observers, can extend the range of data 
channelled to the scientific decision maker [Howes and Chambers, 1980]. 
Indeed, many instances of active indigenous experimentation and consequent

--- Page 236 ---

239 
evolution of farming systems have been recorded [Geertz, 1963; Johnson, 
1972; Richards, 1985; Bentley, 1994; Stolzenbach, 1994; Winarto, 1994]. With 
this in mind, effective integration of internal (local) and external (' scien-
tific') efforts may help to avoid wasteful duplication of effort. 
To a certain extent, farmers have long been participants in the externally 
driven research and development process, albeit at a late stage, in that they 
adapt extension recommendations according to detailed knowledge of 
interactions between crops, pests and soils under local climatic and edaphic 
conditions. Richards [1994] documents a long-established process of discovery 
amongst Mende-speaking communities in Sierra Leone involving screening 
of new rice germplasm, arising both from seed transport into the area and 
genetic variation. The farmers used an explicit methodology capable of 
yielding objective knowledge concerning crop-environment interactions both 
through intentional experimentation, such as observation plots of new rice 
varieties and comparative trials; and from drawing inferences from oppor-
tunistic observations. Richards [1994] asserts that the farmers are generating 
knowledge that is 'in conformity with general scientific principles, but which, 
because it embodies place-specific experience, allows better assessments of 
risk factors in production decisions'. Recently, agricultural development 
initiatives have explicitly tried to incorporate farmer experimentation within 
the research and development cycle. Millar [1994] for example, describes how 
the results of farmer experimentation with cereals and tubers in northern 
Ghana (driven by curiosity, a problem-solving requirement, a need for adapta-
tion and peer pressure) was incorporated into development in NGO projects 
through dialogue between extension staff and farmers. Bentley [1994] reports 
how scientists were able to stimulate farmers to experiment with biological 
pest control in Honduras by explaining ecological relationships, such as the 
existence and behaviour of parasitoids and entomopathogens, that were 
previously unknown to farmers because they are not easily observed. The 
involvement of farmer evaluation in on-farm research is now known to lead 
to the design of more appropriate technical interventions as, for example, in 
Zambia, where adaptive research has been institutionalised at a national level 
for over a decade [Drinkwater, 1994]. 
Effective participation evidently provides not only a means of integrating 
indigenous knowledge and perspective into the development process but also 
an environment that encourages better understanding through dialogue. 
However, without an independent record of the indigenous knowledge system, 
each new interaction with a local community effectively starts with a similar 
level of ignorance on the part of the professionals, and only those profes-
sionals who actively participate may learn what people know and be forced 
to re-evaluate their scientific knowledge accordingly. Much of the process of 
incorporating indigenous knowledge remains implicit, occurring because the 
balance of power shifts from imposing the perspective of the researcher to 
enabling the farmer to realise his or her objectives [Chambers, 1992]. Thus, 
while participation may empower farmers to pursue their own research, and

--- Page 237 ---

240 
make the activity of individual research and extension workers more relevant 
to farmer circumstances, it does not necessarily imply, nor result in, a suffi-
ciently detailed and explicit evaluation of indigenous knowledge for this 
knowledge itself to be used as a basis for planning research and extension 
strategies. 
Fuller understanding of target communities 
Traditionally, communication in the development process has been one-way, 
from the researcher to the farmer. While this approach has had spectacular 
success [see e.g., Khush, 1993], it has frequently resulted in very limited 
adoption. Success may be more likely in circumstances where a relatively 
simple technology or new input can be fitted into relatively simple produc-
tion systems in relatively homogeneous ecological zones to produce a high 
return. In the context of development programmes, these circumstances are 
the exception rather than the rule. Furthermore, it has been argued that while 
a one-way model of extension may be suitable for the environment in which 
it has evolved, the US and Europe, the one-way flow of information is less 
appropriate where there is a more serious element of cross-cultural transla-
tion between researcher and target community [Meehan, 1980]. As a result, 
traditional extension has been increasingly recognised as being of limited 
utility within development programmes. This has led to developments in 
approaches to farming systems research [Byerlee et aI., 1982; Hildebrand, 
1990; Biggs, 1985; Simmonds, 1985] and, separately [Ison, 1993], to the 
development of Rapid Rural Appraisal methodologies [Carruthers and 
Chambers, 1987] and specifically in agroforestry to the Diagnosis and Design 
methodology [Raintree, 1987]. 
Approaches to Rapid Rural Appraisal (RRA) are essentially qualitative 
survey methodologies and are primarily aimed at better understanding the 
needs of farming communities and the constraints under which they operate. 
As with approaches described in the previous section, these techniques' are 
concerned with participation by the target communities in development 
activities. However, participation is essentially passive (i.e., responding to 
requests for information) rather than active. The emergence of Participatory 
Rural Appraisal (PRA) [Mascarenhas et aI., 1991] has gone some way towards 
blurring this distinction. 
The development and adoption of RRA and similar methodologies over the 
last fifteen years has had a profound and positive impact on the development 
process in many instances. However, resulting experience casts doubt on the 
prevailing view that the primary requirement in agroforestry is for adaptive 
research, aimed at the development of technology packages that are then 
extended to farmers [Beer et aI., 1990; Van den Hoek and Bekkering, 1990]. 
The extension of prefabricated technology packages has certainly proved less 
effective in agroforestry development than incremental improvements that 
build on local practice [Bunderson et aI., 1990]. The need to synthesise

--- Page 238 ---

241 
research outputs into technological packages as in the design phase in the 
Diagnosis and Design methodology [Raintree, 1990], has not been justified 
by field experience. In fact, there is evidence that farmers who do not adopt 
an integrated package may nevertheless be interested in elements of that 
package [Buck, 1990]. A need to support farmers in decision making about 
the incorporation of trees within their farming systems based on the results 
of more fundamental ecological research by the scientific community has been 
identified as a result [Anderson et aI., 1993]. 
This recognition of the need to provide support to the farmer in the light 
of the farmer's role as the decision maker, demands means of assessing the 
ecological knowledge that farmers use in their decision-making processes, and 
the knowledge that farmers hold about how to make decisions on the basis 
of that ecological knowledge. Simply describing actual practice and the 
constraints contributing to actual practice is inadequate: an understanding of 
the knowledge used to generate that practice is also necessary. 
Analysis of indigenous knowledge and synthesis with scientific knowledge 
Given that farmers have much more intimate experience of their production 
practices than external professionals, it seems intuitive that they have 
developed an in-depth understanding about the ecology of those practices. 
There is clear evidence to support this suggestion [Conklin, 1957; Barker et 
aI., 1977; Brokensha and Riley, 1980; Posey, 1985; Brosius et aI., 1986; 
Chandler, 1991; Barrow, 1992]. 
In principle, the knowledge held by farmers can provide a resource for 
science irrespective of the participation of the farmers in the use of that knowl-
edge. In other words, the ecological understanding developed by one farming 
community may be relevant to addressing the constraints encountered by other 
communities (with all the attendant issues of intellectual property rights). As 
a result, investigating local knowledge may be a powerful and efficient means 
of rapidly filling gaps in scientific understanding about agroforestry. So, 
amalgamating specific local knowledge and general scientific knowledge may 
be more powerful in designing appropriate land use practices than the use of 
either alone. Indeed, it has been claimed that agroforestry is in a unique 
position to learn from traditional knowledge and practice given the long 
history of traditional agroforestry practice as compared to a relatively recent 
scientific interest [Rocheleau, 1987]. The investigation of local knowledge is 
not reliant on that knowledge being 'accurate' [Mathias-Mundy et aI., 1992]. 
Understanding local knowledge systems can expose inaccurate or technically 
outdated components and allow the identification of complementary external 
knowledge [Rocheleau, 1987], and incomplete areas of understanding [Bentley, 
1994], allowing extension (where appropriate) to be targeted accordingly 
[Gladwin, 1980; Bentley, 1994]. 
It is argued that, by contrast with the two categories of approach already 
discussed, effective use of indigenous ecological knowledge demands rigorous

--- Page 239 ---

242 
analysis of indigenous explanation of system function. Evaluations of the 
current state of indigenous and scientific knowledge that rely on some implicit 
synthesis of the two knowledge systems, through extensive participant 
observation for example, may be effective in some instances but are limited 
in that the process and, therefore, the validity of analysis and synthesis cannot 
be evaluated. Explicit analysis provides an evaluable process. Furthermore, 
the process of explicit representation and analysis of knowledge helps to high-
light assumptions and incongruities that are otherwise easily missed. Rigorous 
analysis essentially comprises an evaluation of what is currently known and 
its implications and an evaluation of gaps in current knowledge. Evaluation 
of what is known might focus on coherence and consistency. Gaps in indige-
nous knowledge might be defined in terms of information needs for 'opti-
mised' management or by comparison with scientific knowledge. Rigorous 
analysis, in turn, depends fundamentally on means of getting farmers to 
articulate their knowledge and means of recording that knowledge in a form 
that is amenable to rigorous analysis and subsequent synthesis with scientific 
knowledge. In effect, a comprehensive and explicit representation or model 
of indigenous knowledge is required. Once this representation has been 
created, a comparative analysis in relation to a similar representation of 
scientific knowledge can be undertaken. This process leads to the possibility 
of a rigorous and effective synthesis of that scientific and indigenous knowl-
edge. 
In summary, the process of explicit representation, analysis and synthesis 
of indigenous ecological knowledge and scientific knowledge is method-
ologically demanding but can have a profound impact on development 
activities - as illustrated in the accompanying paper [Tbapa et aI., 1995 (this 
issue)]. It is argued that, in contrast to local participation in research and the 
use of survey methods to better understand target communities, these oppor-
tunities have not received adequate attention. 
In the next section, one approach to the explicit representation and analysis 
of indigenous ecological knowledge and its synthesis with scientific knowl-
edge is briefly introduced. 
Formal representation and analysis of indigenous ecological knowledge 
Previous work on indigenous knowledge systems has been undertaken largely 
by ethnographers. Although their studies are often rich in detail, the descrip-
tive nature of this work renders it difficult to analyse systematically in terms 
of ecological processes. Simply recording indigenous knowledge collected in 
such research remains a challenging task [McKiernan, 1989]. 
One approach to addressing this problem is through explicit representa-
tion of knowledge as it is collected. This involves storing the knowledge in 
a form that permits repeated and variable analysis and interpretation of the 
knowledge for decision-making purposes. This can be achieved through the 
application of knowledge-based systems techniques for representing knowl-

--- Page 240 ---

243 
edge on computers [Walker et aI., in press]. This allows the processing power 
of desk-top computers to be applied to the representation, analysis and use 
of large and complex sets of knowledge, much of which would be imprac-
tical without computing assistance to handle tedious and repetitive tasks. 
The application of knowledge-based systems techniques also allows the 
application of automated reasoning enabled by the explicit and syntactically 
precise representation of knowledge and concomitant development of appro-
priate inferencing mechanisms. These may be used in: managing complex sets 
of knowledge; evaluating complex sets of knowledge; synthesising knowledge 
from different sources; producing clear justifications for research plans; and 
producing extension materials [Kendon et aI., in press]. In practice, this type 
of approach builds on many of the features of participatory and RRA 
approaches but incorporates a more formal and rigorous procedure than pre-
viously possible, involving the analysis of what is and what is not known 
and the specification, in functional terms, of what knowledge new research 
aims to create. 
The Agroforestry Knowledge Toolkit (AKT) methodology and software 
[Walker et aI., 1994], is based on this knowledge-based systems approach to 
agroforestry research and extension. The methodology provides a means of 
iteratively creating knowledge bases about the ecology of agroforestry from 
mUltiple sources and of applying formal reasoning tools and less formal 
exploration of the content of the knowledge base (through the automated 
creation of, for example, systems diagrams) to the analysis of the content of 
the knowledge base. The knowledge base provides a central point of refer-
ence throughout the research. Knowledge may be formally represented on 
the computer from the first interaction with the community of interest. Formal 
and informal analysis of this prototype knowledge base then provides a basis 
for further knowledge elicitation. In this way a tight (typically daily) cycle 
of knowledge elicitation, knowledge representation and analysis helps target 
interaction with informants to create a 'complete', coherent and accurate 
representation of the knowledge of a target community. This process of 
knowledge base modification and further knowledge elicitation will continue 
throughout the useful life of the knowledge base. 
Although the AKT toolkit and methodology have been designed primarily 
for the representation of knowledge elicited through semi-structured inter-
view, a multi-method approach to knowledge elicitation can be used. Methods 
successfully applied include questionnaire surveys, slip sorting, interactive 
diagramming and sketch mapping. While the knowledge base will undergo 
continuous enhancement throughout its useful life, the process of knowledge 
base creation has a clear life cycle, different approaches to knowledge 
elicitation being appropriate at different stages. So, in the early stages of 
establishing the boundaries of a new knowledge base, extensive questionnaire 
survey across a community is appropriate. During the bulk of knowledge base 
creation, more intensive and interactive dialogue with targeted key informants 
is necessary. Prior to undertaking analysis of the content of the knowledge

--- Page 241 ---

244 
base in order to facilitate the planning of research or extension activities, ques-
tionnaire survey will be needed to establish how representative the knowledge 
obtained from key informants is of the knowledge in the broader community. 
The creation of a knowledge base representing the indigenous ecological 
knowledge of a community of interest can have a significant utility in better 
understanding that community and thereby, making research and extension 
activities more relevant to that community. Moreover, comparable knowl-
edge bases can be created representing scientific knowledge. The knowledge 
bases from different local and scientific communities can then be synthesised 
in an attempt to create a truly 'encyclopedic' knowledge base about focussed 
aspects of the ecology of particular agroforestry practices. While this process 
is exceedingly demanding, both practically and theoretically, it can be 
extremely revealing. In the accompanying paper [Thapa et aI., 1995 (this 
issue)], an example of the impact of this process on research and extension 
activities is described. 
While the AKT software and methodology has only begun to address issues 
relating to the analysis of indigenous knowledge and its synthesis with 
scientific knowledge, the experience such as that described in Thapa et aI. 
[1995 (this issue)] provides a sound basis for arguing that this type of approach 
should receive more attention within agroforestry development. 
Conclusions 
In summary, it has been argued in this paper that: 
• understanding indigenous knowledge is, in conjunction with understanding 
practice in the context of social, economic, cultural and environmental 
variables, an important means of understanding needs and constraints facing 
farmers; 
• understanding indigenous knowledge is an important activity in improving 
the communication between target communities and development profes-
sionals in the development process; 
• facilitating active partnerships between development professionals and local 
people in research can help to result in effective and well-targeted research; 
• indigenous knowledge provides a useful source of understanding of the 
functioning of agroforestry practices that may be complementary to 
scientific knowledge. 
In particular, the explicit representation of knowledge may have an impor-
tant role to play in enabling: 
• more thorough understanding of target farming communities through 
evaluation of the knowledge that they hold; 
• more effective identification and prioritisation of research objectives, 
leading to more appropriate research;

--- Page 242 ---

245 
• improved dissemination of knowledge to target farming communities; and 
• improved monitoring and evaluation of research activities.

Agrojorestry Systems 30: 249-261, 1995. 
© 1995 Kluwer Academic Publishers. 
Incorporation of indigenous knowledge and perspectives 
in agroforestry development 
Part 2: Case-study on the impact of explicit representation 
of farmers' knowledge 
B. THAPN, F. L. SINCLAIR2 and D. H. WALKER3 
1 Pakhribas Agricultural Centre, cia BTCO, P.O. Box 106, Kathmandu, Nepal; 2 School oj 
Agricultural and Forest Sciences, University ojWales, Bangor, Gwynedd LL57 2UW, UK; 
3 Division ojTropical Crops and Pastures, CS1RO, P.O. Aitkenyale, Queensland 4814, Australia 
Key words: Nepal, tree fodder, knowledge-based systems 
Abstract. An explicit representation of farmers' knowledge about on-farm tree fodder resources 
was developed using knowledge-based systems software and methodology at a front-line 
agricultural research centre in the eastern mid-hills of Nepal. Sophisticated knowledge of tree-
crop interactions and the feeding value of various tree fodders was revealed. Farmers' ecolog-
ical knowledge was more developed in some areas than others and was largely complementary 
to that of agricultural researchers in Nepal and the scientific literature more generally. Evaluation 
of the combined knowledge of farmers and researchers related to interdisciplinary land use 
problems by the creation of explicit, encyclopaedic knowledge bases, offers the possibility of 
targeting research more precisely to gaps in knowledge constraining the productivity and 
sustainability of the farming system. While similar insight into the depth of farmers knowledge 
may have been gained by researchers adopting a less formal approach to knowledge acquisi-
tion, without an explicit, durable and manipulable record, subsequent analyses and continued 
use of the knowledge in the research and development process might not be achieved. 
Introduction 
In an accompanying paper [Walker et aI., 1995b - this issue] it was argued 
that explicit representation of the ecological knowledge held by farmers could 
form an appropriate basis for planning, monitoring and evaluating agroforestry 
research and extension; and an overview was given of the AKT knowledge-
based systems software and methodology developed to achieve this. The 
present paper documents the impact of the trial application of a knowledge-
based systems approach to interdisciplinary research on tree fodder at a 
front-line agricultural research centre in Nepal. 
Institutional context 
Pakhribas Agricultural Centre (PAC) is located 25 km north-west of Dhankuta 
(87 0 30' E, 27 0 19' N), in the eastern hills of Nepal. Since 1982 it has held 
responsibility for agricultural research and development activities servicing 
the four districts of the Koshi Hills Area Rural Development Programme

--- Page 247 ---

250 
(KHARDEP) now the Koshi Hills Development Programme (KHDP). After 
integration into the national agricultural development network of regional 
research farms and stations in 1988, PAC's command area was increased to 
a total of 11 districts in the Mechi, Koshi and Sagarmatha zones. The 
command area of PAC thus comprises areas characterised by strongly dis-
sected relief ranging from 300 m to over 4000 m, a climate that varies from 
sub-tropical to alpine, and a heterogeneous farming community in terms of 
ethnic and socio-economic groups and farming activities [Gibbon and Shultz, 
1989]. 
Problem context 
Livestock playa critical role in crop cultivation and sustaining soil fertility 
in the hill farming systems of Nepal and are fed during the dry season on 
tree fodder. Land fragmentation, with consequent decrease in the size of farm 
holdings combined with decreasing access to dwindling common property 
forest resources that have been over-exploited, is causing farmers to incor-
porate an increasing number of fodder trees on their farmland [Carter and 
Gilmour, 1989]. The productivity of fodder trees is critical for maintaining 
livestock health and numbers and, therefore, the productivity and sustainability 
of people's livelihood system, but trees planted on crop terrace risers may also 
influence soil erosion through leaf drip effects and compete with staple food 
crops for light, water and nutrients. Farmers need, therefore, to achieve a 
balance between generating enough high quality fodder at appropriate times in 
the season and minimising the competitive effects of trees on staple food crops. 
Research context 
Research on tree fodder at PAC thus impinges on the work of four separately 
administered sections: the Forestry Section, concerned with the establish-
ment and management of fodder trees; the Livestock Section, concerned with 
the improvement of the fodder supply to animals (the amount, feeding value 
and timing of tree fodder given to animals); the Veterinary Investigation and 
Analytical Services Section, responsible for fodder evaluation and the 
Agronomy Section. While there was no combined tree fodder research strategy 
in operation at PAC when the present work was initiated, information exchange 
and research planning was facilitated by a fodder working group comprising 
the four sections. The trial application of using a knowledge-based systems 
approach to construct an explicit representation of farmers' knowledge was 
initiated because it was clear from analyses of information flows [Sinclair et 
aI., 1993] that there were opportunities for improving the effectiveness of 
fodder research at PAC by: 
• making it more relevant to the priorities and existing knowledge of client 
farmers and

--- Page 248 ---

251 
• making more effective use of previous research findings when new research 
was being planned. 
Methods 
The Agroforestry Knowledge Toolkits (AKTl and AKT2) and associated 
methodology [Walker et aI., 1994] support the creation and use of coherent 
and accessible, knowledge bases, synthesising information elicited from 
farmers and development professionals [Walker et aI., 1995a; Kendon et aI., 
1995]. This methodology as outlined in the accompanying paper [Walker et 
aI., 1995b - this issue] addresses the need to record information: 
• in suitably-sized chunks that represent useful knowledge without impairing 
the flexibility of combining the chunks variously to suit different purposes; 
and, 
• in a form which allows knowledge to be expressed naturally but sufficiently 
unambiguously to be interpretable by others. 
The AKT knowledge-based systems approach was applied at PAC to an inves-
tigation of farmers' knowledge about on-farm tree fodder resources. The study 
community was defined by the administrative boundary of Solma Village 
Development Committee comprising 3500 people in a collection of hamlets 
spread over an altitudinal range of 500 m to 2000 m. Sixty farmers selected 
randomly from the heads of households in the study community were inter-
viewed using a questionnaire. A detailed tree inventory was undertaken on 
30 of these farms. This was followed by the iterative creation and refinement 
of a knowledge base through semi-structured interviews with 40 purposively 
selected key informants, each interviewed an average of four times, for about 
one hour on each occasion. The purposive sample was stratified in terms of 
gender, altitude and ethnicity. Knowledge base creation took approximately 
two hours for every hour of interview material. It was undertaken on an 
iterative basis. Knowledge was entered into the knowledge base on the 
computer immediately after the first interview and the growing knowledge 
base was both modified and added to after each subsequent interview. 
Assessment of the content of the growing knowledge base itself provided the 
direction for further interviewing. A questionnaire survey was then used to 
the test the representativeness of the content of the knowledge base against 
the knowledge held by 50 randomly selected farmers from the community that 
had not previously been interviewed. 
There was nothing particularly novel about the methods of knowledge 
elicitation, which followed a multi-method approach (see, for example, Rusten 
and Gold [1991] with strong emphasis on semi-structured interviewing guided 
by participant observation. Their use was, however, honed towards producing 
a complete and coherent record of local ecological knowledge by the itera-

--- Page 249 ---

252 
tive evaluation on computer of what had been collected, as it was collected. 
This was facilitated by: 
• disaggregating knowledge into sets of unambiguous unitary statements 
using a restricted syntax (Table 1), 
• considering the context of these unitary statements in terms of their con-
ditionality and the local definition and classification of terms, and 
• evaluating related sets of statements by constructing hierarchically struc-
tured node-and-link diagrams (Fig. 1). 
Details of these methods of knowledge acquisition and how they are supported 
by use of the AKT software are published elsewhere [Walker et aI., 1994; 
Walker et aI., 1995a]. Statements in the knowledge base were tagged with 
their source so that people contributing knowledge could be credited with 
the knowledge they had contributed. 
Results 
The study demonstrated that farmers in the study area had knowledge of over 
90 different tree species that were deliberately managed on farmland on the 
basis of sophisticated knowledge of tree-crop interactions and the amount and 
feeding value of fodder required within the farming system. Details of the 
knowledge that was acquired are reported elsewhere [Thapa et aI., 1994; 
Thapa, 1994], the present focus is on the impact that the process of knowl-
edge acquisition has had on research on tree fodder resources at PAC. 
Systematic research on tree fodder is in its infancy in Nepal. This is partly 
because the basic information required to formulate research plans is scarce, 
so that a clear research strategy is lacking. The creation of a knowledge base, 
abstracting the knowledge available from a farming community, has made a 
significant contribution towards rectifying this situation. In the first place, it 
was evident that farmers were deliberately managing abundant natural regen-
eration of trees on crop terrace risers, obviating the need for the provision of 
nursery grown seedlings and schemes to motivate farmers to plant trees, which 
have dominated development activity in the forestry sector. 
Tree-crop interactions 
Investigation of farmers' knowledge about tree-crop interactions revealed that 
they understood that shade and splash erosion caused by leaf droplets (a 
process locally referred to as 'tapkan') reduced crop yield, and that tree attrib-
utes such as leaf size, leaf texture, crown density, crown size, tree height and 
leaf inclination angle influenced shade and/or leaf drip effects. Because 
farmers viewed these as primary issues for consideration in managing above-
ground interactions affecting crop yield, tree crown architecture was identi-
fied as an important area of consideration in planning tree fodder research.

--- Page 250 ---

253 
Table 1. Some illustrative examples of natural language statements and their formal equiva-
lents. The final knowledge base on tree fodder created through interaction with Solma farmers 
contained about 700 statements. The natural language statements are concise, unitary items 
of knowledge elicited from informants and the formal statements are the same knowledge 
represented using a restricted syntax of a formal grammar, fully specified in Walker et al. 
[1995a). The formal representation both forces an unambiguous interpretation of the meaning 
of the statement and enables automated reasoning with the statement on computer. The formal 
statements, which may be conditional, include four fundamental elements: i) objects, which are 
physical items e.g. trees; ii) processes (or events), that are changes or fluxes, e.g. germination 
or soil erosion; iii) attributes, which are properties of objects or processes e.g. tree height or 
the rate of soil erosion; and iv) values, which are the measurable state of an attribute e.g. 5 m 
or 5 t ha- I a -I. The elements may be combined in four ways that involve i) associating 
attributes and values to objects or processes; ii) representing causal linkages; iii) representing 
other, user-specified linkages; and iv) making comparisons. A part construct is available to 
identify component parts of an object, and causation may be two-way, in which case an increase 
in x causing an increase in y is automatically equivalent to a decrease in x causing a decrease 
y; or one-way, in which case this equivalence does not necessarily hold.
