Fig. 1. Some statements about the effects of tree shading on crops synthesised into a set of 
hierarchically linked node-and-Iink diagrams using AKT(I) software [Walker et aI., 1995a]. The 
boxes are nodes and the labelled arrows are the links, which also correspond to formal 
statements (Table 1). This is an illustrative example of the use of diagramming in developing 
a knowledge base. Diagrams, at anyone hierarchical level, used in constructing the tree fodder 
knowledge base in the present study typically contained from 20 to 30 nodes and from 30 to 
40 links [Thapa, 1994].

--- Page 252 ---

255 
In the absence of such information previously, researchers have tended to 
follow a more conventional approach, emphasising survival rate, above-ground 
growth rate, and total foliage production as the most important parameters 
for selection of germplasm. Such research may be of little relevance to the 
target communities because these are not necessarily the parameters of most 
concern to the farmers who can, in fact, specify associative tree ideotypes 
[Huxley, 1985] suitable for integration in their farming systems, in terms of 
tree attributes affecting crop yield, fodder quality and resilience to lopping. 
The results of this study have already benefited researchers at PAC, modified 
their understanding of above-ground interactions and significantly improved 
the design of agroforestry research by including consideration of this 
knowledge when new research has been planned (L. Joshi, pers. comm.I ). 
A detailed analysis of the ways in which farmers and researchers differ in 
their understanding of processes, attributes and terminology in describing and 
rationalising aspects of tree fodder production and management practices, 
has important implications for future tree fodder research and extension 
programmes. For example, one of the key processes considered by farmers 
in managing tree-crop interactions termed 'tapkan' concerns the erosive effect 
on soil and consequent reductions in crop yield caused by water droplets 
falling from tree leaves. This is an eclectic term with no exact scientific 
equivalent. The lack of understanding about tapkan by Nepali researchers 
indicated a gap in their knowledge and potential problems in interaction 
between the farmer and the researcher. Furthermore, the farmers' assertion 
that leaf size and texture affected the size of droplets falling from leaves and 
hence their erosive effect on soil, was in direct contradiction to the prevailing 
scientific literature that held, on the basis of controlled experimentation and 
knowledge of the surface tension properties of water, that droplet size was 
independent of canopy morphology [Brandt, 1989; Thomes, 1989]. Only 
recently have Hall and Calder [1993], using a new and expensive instrument 
(a disdrometer) capable of more accurate determination of droplet diameter, 
measured markedly different modification of rainfall drop size spectra under 
pine (Pinus caribaea), teak (Tectona grandis) and eucalypt (Eucalyptus camal-
dulensis) canopies. This new evidence suggests that the farmers' explanations 
may be valid scientifically and that their knowledge of the drop size modifi-
cation by a wide range of tree species may be pertinent to controlling soil 
erosion on slopes and complementary to what is known scientifically. The 
knowledge articulated by farmers relating to tapkan can now be utilised by 
researchers in focusing future research and in developing detailed research 
plans appropriate to farmers' needs. 
The study also revealed that the depth and spread of root systems was 
considered by farmers in managing below-ground interactions. Farmers clas-
sified many tree species as being either 'rukho' (23 species) or 'malilo' (17 
species). Rukho trees (for example, Ficus bengalensis) were thought to exhibit 
competitive and/or allelopathic effects which inhibited crop germination and 
growth rate and so reduced crop yield whereas malilo trees (for example,

--- Page 253 ---

256 
Alhizia ju/ihrissin) were thought to contribute to soil fertility through inputs 
from decomposing litter and have less competitive root systems, so enhancing 
crop yield. Since farmers viewed these as important issues for consideration 
in managing below-ground interactions, tree root architecture and leaf litter 
decomposition rates were identified as important areas of consideration in 
planning tree fodder research. Research on below-ground interactions in 
agroforestry has barely begun in Nepal, perhaps because of a combination 
of: forestry professionals not being aware of their character and so consid-
ering them of little or no immediate relevance; and, the inherent difficulty 
associated with studying roots. 
However, analysis of the farmers' knowledge system suggests that research 
on below-ground interactions may have greater leverage than research on 
above-ground processes because farmers consider them important but know 
little about them, presumably because they are difficult for the farmers them-
selves to observe. Research on root architecture including intra-species and 
inter-species variation in rooting pattern of common rukho and malilo fodder 
tree species; the effects of tree fodder management techniques, particularly 
frequency of fodder lopping, on rooting pattern; and, the variation of root 
development in relation to different propagation or planting materials and its 
implications for resource sharing (in terms of niche differentiation and root 
competition between tree and crop components for soil water and nutrients); 
are areas immediately identifiable from the agroforestry literature as having 
potential for research to alleviate constraints within the system [see for 
example, Van Noordwijk and Purnomosdhi, this issue; Schroth, this issue]. 
The knowledge articulated by farmers on crop cultivar responses to shade 
also had direct implications for planning future crop improvement pro-
grammes. The study clearly suggested that there was considerable potential 
for selecting crop cultivars for different shade levels and environments, as 
determined by altitude and aspect, found in the mid-hills of Nepal. Consider-
able information on existing shade tolerant cultivars was also forthcoming. 
Unfortunately, most improved crop cultivars from government agricultural 
research stations in Nepal have been selected under full sunlight. This may 
have partly contributed to the low rate of crop cultivar adoption by farmers. 
Fodder value 
Scientists both in Nepal [Subba and Tamang, 1990; Panday and Osti, 1993] 
and elsewhere (M. Gill and P. Thome, pers. comm.)2 have been working to 
ascertain fodder quality through chemical analyses. Much research on nutri-
tional value of tree fodder has been based on proximate analysis of fodder 
leaves, often emphasising crude protein and crude fibre content as the most 
important parameters for assessing fodder quality. More recently, a broader 
range of standard nutritive value assessment methods (which include mea-
surements of digestibility and the content of anti-nutritive factors) have been 
used. However, results from these studies are far from conclusive and often

--- Page 254 ---

257 
contradictory between different laboratories [Mahato and Subba, 1988; 
Paterson, 1993], in some cases vindicating farmers preferences and in others 
finding no chemical explanations for them [Subba et aI., 1992]. 
Analysis of the indigenous knowledge about tree fodder collected by Thapa 
[1994] was undertaken; it was found that farmers classified fodder trees, for 
various categories of livestock, and with various seasonal and age-dependent 
attributes, in terms of their nutritional value (posilo and kam posilo categories) 
and as either heating or cooling (chiso and obano categories; consistent with 
the earlier work elsewhere in Nepal of Rusten and Gold [1991]). Farmers 
assessed fodder quality in terms of its ability to satisfy animal appetite, its 
palatability, its effects on animal health and its effect on milk yield and the 
butterfat content of milk. Intra-species variability in fodder quality was 
consistently recognised for three previously undifferentiated species (Ficus 
nemoralis, Bauhinia purpurea and F. roxburghii) while some farmers recog-
nised sub-species variants in a further four species (F. lacor, F. semicordata, 
B. variegata and Saurauia nepaulensis). Knowledge both of how to identify 
the sub-species and their fodder value was apparent (Table 2). Farmers also 
possessed detailed knowledge of how the timing, extent and technique of 
fodder lopping affected the amount and quality of fodder production from 
different species. This record of farmers' knowledge of fodder value has 
subsequently provided the framework for innovative research using indige-
nous knowledge to make sense of the results of laboratory-based nutritive 
Table 2. Varietal characteristics of one of the species, Dudhilo (Ficus nemoralis) consistently

--- Page 255 ---

258 
value assessment [Thome et aI., 1994]. The important point to note here is 
that the analysis was conceived and implemented in conjunction with live-
stock scientists after the indigenous knowledge about tree fodder had been 
recorded in a knowledge base by a researcher from the Forestry Section at 
PAC. Significantly, the knowledge base provided a sufficiently flexible, 
explicit and accessible record of the local knowledge system for an analysis 
to be undertaken that had not been conceived when the knowledge acquisi-
tion was planned and conducted. 
Interesting correspondences were apparent between some terms for attrib-
utes used by farmers and different terms used similarly by researchers, for 
example 'bitterness' of leaves corresponded closely with 'tannin content' of 
leaves, indicating complementarity in the knowledge from the two sets of 
sources. In general the researchers' knowledge had a greater explanatory 
power with respect to what chemical constituents caused various effects on 
livestock but farmers knew the values of the attributes they recognised for 
many more species than did researchers for the attributes that they recognised. 
Distribution of knowledge and its relationship to agricultural practice 
Conceptual understanding of interactive processes such as shading and leaf 
droplet erosion were consistently held by farmers across gender, ethnic and 
altitudinal divides but detailed knowledge about species-specific tree attrib-
utes, which influenced the application of general concepts in practical 
management, varied amongst farmers, apparently associated with which 
particular species they had experience of cultivating [Thapa, 1994]. Access 
to a central repository of indigenous knowledge by less knowledgeable 
farmers, either directly or via the forestry extension service may, therefore, 
assist them in improving their practice. Comparison of indigenous ecological 
knowledge with farmer practice, by examining which types of tree were 
growing on which types of land, indicated that underlying ecological knowl-
edge held by farmers did, at least to some extent, determine their practice. 
Practices known by farmers to have negative ecological consequences did, 
however, occur; for example, planting trees that were known to have a 
predisposition to cause soil erosion on crop terrace risers because of their high 
fodder value at critical times of the year. The ubiquitous persistence of such 
practices implies that farmers are forced to make trade-offs between short term 
productivity and longer term sustainability and indicates substantial potential 
for improvement of farming practice through research and extension targeted 
on these areas. 
Conclusions 
The case study has revealed the depth and consistency of indigenous eco-
logical knowledge about agroforestry on one hillside in Nepal and its

--- Page 256 ---

259 
complementarity with existing scientific knowledge. Problems related to on-
farm tree fodder production in Nepal clearly require interdisciplinary research. 
Farmers already combine sophisticated knowledge of fodder production by 
trees, their impact on associated soil and crops, and the feeding value of fodder 
for livestock, in arriving at complex trade-offs between these elements. The 
creation of explicit, encyclopaedic knowledge bases, combining interdisci-
plinary scientific knowledge with that of farmers, has the potential to have a 
profound impact on the research and development cycle both in terms of: 
• targeting research at gaps in farmers' knowledge that constrain the pro-
ductivity and sustainability of their farming system and 
• marshalling the corporate knowledge of researchers related to a land use 
problem across traditional subject divisions. 
While the same level of insight with respect to the nature and extent of 
farmers' knowledge might have been achieved through the application of less 
formal approaches to knowledge acquisition (although this seems doubtful) 
the conclusions drawn could not have been as easily scrutinised retrospec-
tively. The research involved extented interaction with farming communities 
and, rather than simply developing an external ('etic' - Knight [1980]) descrip-
tion of landuse practice and constraints sought an 'emic' [Knight, 1980] 
explanation of system function, thereby revealing key issues in system man-
agement which had not been illuminated by previous Rapid Rural Appraisal 
type investigation at PAC [Chand and Gibbon, 1990]. Furthermore, the 
knowledge base remains a resource that can now be used in a variety of further 
research and planning activities.

Agroforestry Systems 30: 263-275, 1995. 
© 1995 Kluwer Academic Publishers. 
The use and value of multiple methods to capture the 
diversity of endogenous agroforestry knowledge: 
an example from Rwanda* 
C. DEN BIGGELAAR1 and M. A. GOLD2 
I clo ICRAF, United Nations Avenue, Gigiri, P.O. Box 30677, Nairobi, Kenya; 2 Department of 
Forestry, Michigan State University, East Lansing, MI48824-1222, USA 
Key words: indigenous knowledge, knowledge ranking, participatory research, socioeconomic 
surveys, tree inventory 
Abstract. To understand endogenous agroforestry knowledge and the processes behind the 
generation of such knowledge, multiple methods are needed. In this paper, the identification and 
characterization of farmer agroforestry experts will illustrate the necessity of multiple methods. 
Nonformal, participatory methods were employed to identify the research subjects (farmer 
agroforestry experts) and to gain insights into the emic conception and understanding of 
'knowledge' and 'agroforestry'. An adaptation of the wealth ranking game was used to deter-
mine (1) the research subjects using locally defined criteria of 'being knowledgeable about 
agroforestry', and (2) how this knowledge is distributed within a community. Additional 
information, approaching the subject from an etic perspective, was gathered using formal surveys 
to collect socioeconomic data and to make an inventory of all trees and tree species. 
Study results indicate that exclusive reliance on either emic or etic perspectives would not 
have yielded satisfactory results, as there is a differentiation in agroforestry knowledge and 
agroforestry systems between different groups of farmers. It is, therefore, recommended that 
future studies of endogenous knowledge systems combine qualitative and quantitative, partici-
patory and formal data collection methods as they provide complementary and supplementary 
perspectives on a complex reality. 
Introduction 
According to Tindimubona [1990], science is 'A cumulative tradition of 
knowledge, an evergrowing body of reflections and ideas, experiences and 
actions of a great stream of thinkers and workers'. It is the tool with which 
people learn about their environment, its resources and problems, and how to 
control and utilize them productively and sustainably. As environments vary 
across the globe, one can therefore assume that 'science' differs also in these 
different environments. This is confirmed by Feyerabend [1988], who states 
that there are many different kinds of science, first world science being only 
one science among many others. Thus, if there are different kinds of science, 
each science tradition (or better, the people that adhere to that tradition) may 
* An earlier version of this paper was published under the title 'Differentiating the Nature of 
Agroforestry Systems and Agroforestry Knowledge among Farmers in Southern Rwanda: 
Participatory and Formal Approaches as Complementary Methods' (Culture and Agriculture 
no. 50, Fall 1994 (in press).

--- Page 260 ---

264 
arrive at different conclusions about what constitutes a problem or need, and 
what acceptable solutions will be. Science and technology are above all 
socially constructed entities [Bijker et aI., 1987; Feyerabend, 1988; Knorr-
Cetina, 1981]. 'It is social groups that give meaning to (science and) tech-
nology and problems are defined within the context of the meaning assigned 
by a social group or a combination of social groups. Because social groups 
define problems of technological development, there is flexibility in the way 
things are designed, not one best way' [Pinch and Bijker, 1987]. 
A good example of a socially constructed entity is agriculture. People in 
different environments have devised diverse solutions to problems and con-
straints they encountered in the natural and social environment, resulting in 
a multitude of agricultural systems. Farmer conceived and designed agro-
forestry systems were used as the object of this study. Farmers in the secteurs1 
of Maraba and Simbi (Maraba Commune, Butare) and Kibingo (Karama 
Commune, Gikongoro) in southern Rwanda were studied to ascertain differ-
ences in knowledge of agroforestry and the development of this knowledge 
over time through farmers' own efforts. An understanding of the dynamics 
behind these systems and their underlying logic and knowledge is essential 
for improving existing or developing new systems acceptable to the farmers 
[Rakotomanana, 1988]. The first part of this paper will report on how agro-
forestry and knowledge is understood from an emic (local) perspective. This 
information is then used to determine how knowledge of agroforestry is 
distributed within the community, and to identify local tree experts (i.e., the 
persons most knowledgeable about and most active in knowledge production 
of agroforestry). A variation of the wealth ranking game [Grandin, 1988] was 
used to identify these local tree experts. In the second part of the paper, the 
ranking game results will be compared with the results of a socioeconomic 
survey and inventory of tree species to obtain an objective, more scientific 
view of agroforestry (the etic perspective). These methods provide contra-
dictory results due to the fact that the epistemological definition of (agro-
forestry) knowledge (developed in a time when people primarily relied on 
the gathering of tree products to meet their needs) no longer matches the 
present situation of trees purposively planted and managed on farms. Neither 
the reliance on tree experts as defined and identified according to local criteria 
as the best information sources, nor scientifically designed surveys are suffi-
cient methods for the study of endogenous agroforestry knowledge by them-
selves. As will be shown in this paper, answering one of the questions posed 
by Scoones and Thompson [1994], multiple methods are necessary as they 
provide contrasting but complementary information on the nature of agro-
forestry systems and agroforestry knowledge. The findings also support the 
results of previous studies of indigenous knowledge [Rusten and Gold, 1991] 
detailing the necessity of multiple methods.

--- Page 261 ---

265 
Methodology 
The basic assumption guiding the research was that not every farmer would 
have the same knowledge of trees, largely as a result of differing socioeco-
nomic and political positions within the community and differing biophys-
ical conditions on her or his farm. In addition, farmers would also differ in 
their propensity and aptitude towards experimentation and innovation. As the 
goal of this study was to understand the processes of knowledge production, 
it was necessary to interact with the farmers most knowledgeable about and 
involved in generating and developing agroforestry knowledge and tech-
nologies, as defined from an emic perspective. This made a random selection 
of farmers unacceptable since it could have resulted in a sample of too few 
farmers experimenting with trees on their farms. An adaptation of the 'wealth-
ranking game' [Grandin, 1988] provided a more suitable technique to deter-
mine the study's subjects. 
The knowledge ranking game 
An adaptation of the wealth ranking game was chosen for two reasons. First, 
taking opinions of a larger group of people into account was a more objec-
tive means of identifying local tree experts than relying solely on key infor-
mants. Second, it enabled researchers to learn the 'ways in which people 
construe the world of their experience, and describe it in categories inherent 
to those structurings' [Knorr-Cetina, 1981]. In this research, the game was not 
used to rank and group people according to their wealth but according to 
their knowledge of agroforestry. In addition to determining the subjects 
(farmers) for further in-depth study, the method provided an emic perspec-
tive on 'knowledge of trees', its distribution within a community, and a pre-
liminary understanding of the logic of endogenous agroforestry systems in 
southern Rwanda. The method also provided some quantitative information 
on the characteristics used by the informants to describe the groups of farmers 
they identified. 
The game was used with two modifications in this study. First, the ranking 
was performed with the goal of identifying the individuals most knowledge-
able about agroforestry.2 Second, the cards that were used did not have the 
names of heads of household, but of individual male and female farmers. In 
many societies it is the female farmers who collect and cultivate most tree 
crops. If only heads of household were identified on the cards, then the 
majority of these female farmers would be excluded since only a small per-
centage of rural households are headed by women in Rwanda (around 20%). 
Operationalizing the ranking criterion. Operationalizing the ranking crite-
rion 'knowledgeable about trees and tree cultivation' posed a challenge for 
two reasons. Since there is no equivalent term for 'knowledgeable' in 
Kinyarwanda, a descriptive equivalent had to be found. According to Kagame

--- Page 262 ---

266 
[1958], Kinyarwanda uses the same word for knowledge and experience 
(ubumenyi), indicating that knowledge is gained through experience. Having 
ubumenyi implies having knowledge of (experience with) many different 
things or notions. Thus, in the area of agroforestry knowledge, having many 
different tree species on the farm meant that one has experience with tree 
cultivation and, by consequence, more knowledge of trees. Therefore, species 
richness on the farm was used as a proxy criterion for differentiating highly 
knowledgeable from less knowledgeable farmers. The Kinyarwanda transla-
tion of the ranking criterion 'being knowledgeable about trees' used was 
'Abahinzi-borozi bafite ubumenyi bw'ibiti bwinshi'.3 
Choice of informants and sample size for the knowledge ranking procedure. 
After initial meetings in each secteur with farmers and authorities, the con-
seWers (aldermen representing the secteur in the commune council) were 
asked to prepare a list of all farmers (both male and female) in their respec-
tive secteurs, subdivided by cellule and colline. In total, there were 2159 
households in the three secteurs (704 in Maraba, 746 in Simbi and 709 in 
Kibingo) spread over 63 collines. According to Grandin [1988], if the level 
of agreement within a community on the ranking criterion is high, a sample 
size of 3-5 informants per locale is sufficient. Given that 3-5 informants were 
needed for each colline, the sample size was determined to be 241 informants, 
or about 11 % of all households. A systematic sample of every ninth name was 
taken from the lists prepared by the conseillers, alternating men and women 
so that the final sample contained 121 men and 120 women. These 241 infor-
mants ranked all male and female farmers of these 2159 households (i.e., heads 
of households and their spouses if applicable) in the three communities, with 
males ranking males and females ranking females. 
Formal socioeconomic survey and inventory of trees 
To obtain statistically analyzable data to confirm the perceived differences 
between tree experts and the general farm population, a formal socioeconomic 
survey and inventory of tree species was done on two samples of farmers 
drawn from the ranking game population. These two surveys provided an 
etic perspective to the understanding of local agroforestry systems. Upon 
completion of the ranking game, a series of 13 community interviews were 
held at the cellule level, during which participants were asked to chose 44 tree 
experts from among the group deemed most knowledgeable about agroforestry 
as determined with the ranking game. Thus, these tree experts were identi-
fied as experts by the community according to locally defined criteria of 
'expertise', using a two-stage process of ranking game and community inter-
views. Sixteen percent of the tree experts thus chosen were female. A group 
of 70 farmers (of whom 29% was female) identified as less knowledgeable 
about agroforestry were randomly selected from the remaining individuals to 
serve as a comparison group. The socio-economic survey and tree inventory

--- Page 263 ---

267 
consisted of interviewer-administered questionnaires developed by the author 
in collaboration with researchers of the Institut des Sciences Agronomiques 
du Rwanda, and pretested with farmers in the research area not included in 
the two samples. 
Results 
Farmers' views of agroforestry 
Lundgren [1982] defined agroforestry as a holistic approach to land use, based 
on the combination of trees and shrubs with crops, pastures or animals on 
the same land unit, either in sequence or all at the same time. In reality, as 
Rocheleau et al. [1989] wrote, most farmers cannot easily separate the holistic 
approach to managing a land unit from the integration of woody plants into 
agricultural and pastoral landscapes. Whereas formal agroforestry science is 
based on the systematic placement of trees relative to crops and pastures, rural 
people are often more concerned about the fit of the whole agroforestry 
practice, and trees in . general, into the larger landscape. This landscape 
approach to resource management could also be observed in Rwanda. 
Many outside observers (i.e., persons trained in and adhering to the Western 
scientific knowledge tradition) have noted a steady decrease in the amount 
of forest resources in Rwanda since the beginning of this century [Gatera, 
1980; Nwafor, 1979; Runyinya, 1980]. However, research by, amongst others, 
Balasubramanian and Egli [1986], Hummel [1988], and Pinner [1988] has 
shown that the amount of tree resources in the landscape actually increased 
during this period as farmers were well aware of the importance and the 
numerous benefits trees provide. Indeed, the disappearance of forests was 
often cited by farmers interviewed during this research as a primary motiva-
tion for introducing certain species on the farm. Thus, while forests, natural 
woodlots and forest galleries have all but disappeared under the influence of 
increasing population and pressure on the land, farmers have transformed a 
formerly near treeless landscape [Loupias, 1903; Johanssen, 1915] into a 
landscape dotted with many trees. However, these trees are more dispersed 
over the landscape, integrated in farming and pastoral systems. 
As a corollary to the increase in the number of trees on farms, farmers have 
generated extensive knowledge about the introduction and management of 
indigenous 'tree species on their land. In addition, they have acquired and 
adapted a wealth of knowledge of exotic species and silvicultural practices 
introduced since the 1930s, first by the Belgian colonial rulers and, since 
independence in 1962, by various development programs. These combined 
interventions in the landscape by both farmers and outsiders have led to very 
complex and multifaceted agroforestry systems. This is compounded by the 
fact that what constitutes a tree (igiti) in Rwandan epistemology is different 
from what is understood by the term in the Western world view. For Rwandan

--- Page 264 ---

268 
farmers, trees are all plants that are no~ grasses [Kagame, 1958]. The resulting 
agroforestry systems are more difficult to appreciate for researchers trained 
in the Western scientific tradition, as the inclusion of annuals and perennials, 
in addition to trees and shrubs, makes the systems more complex. Agroforestry 
in this research was defined as all ibiti found (either naturally seeded or pur-
posively planted) and managed on the farm, regardless of their integration 
within space and/or time, or their economic or ecological interactions. 
Indigenous conceptions of the nature of 'knowledge' 
In Kinyarwanda, knowledge, experience and science are all encompassed 
within the word ubumenyi. Science (not to be confounded by science as 
understood in Western epistemology) is to be understood as the possession 
of knowledge as contrasted with ignorance or misunderstanding. According 
to Kagame [1958], ubumenyi implies having knowledge of a plurality of 
notions/objects because a simple knowledge limited to one object or a limited 
number of notions cannot be constituted under the term ubumenyi. 
Consultants perceived ubumenyi as having four aspects, namely intelli-
gence, experience, communication and putting information into practice. 
Ubumenyi manifests itself through the intelligence one has in one's heart.4 
Ubumenyi can be 'natural' (i.e., a gift of God), can result from experience, 
or can be obtained by being well-informed and having gone to school 
(although consultants considered knowledge learned in school 'artificial 
knowledge'). Whatever the source of knowledge, one needs to put the infor-
mation into practice for it to be recognized as ubumenyi. 
All four themes (intelligence, experience, communication and practice) 
have to be present for a person to be recognized as being knowledgeable about 
something by one's fellow farmers. Being knowledgeable about something 
means knowing its utility, holding its secrets and showing a sustained interest 
in it as revealed through one's actions. It depends not only on what one knows 
but equally on how one can explain that knowledge to another person and 
provide sufficient and reliable information about it so that the other person 
can attain the same benefits. In short, besides knowing facts, real knowledge 
is first a willingness to do something with these facts, second to put them 
into practice, and third to communicate your knowledge about these facts to 
others. These findings are both startling and interesting, as they are analogous 
to what Western societies ascribe to their scientists: knowing basic facts, 
putting these into practice in experiments, communicating the results and the 
knowledge gained from of these experiments in journals and in scientific 
meetings, and replicability (Le., other scientists should be able to obtain the 
same results, all else being equal). However, the level at which Rwandan tree 
experts (as one category of indigenous scientists) operate and the methods 
they use are quite different from the one at which researchers trained in the 
Western knowledge tradition operate.

--- Page 265 ---

Knowledge ranking: a local perspective on the definition and 
identification of tree experts

through V - least knowledge) according to their perceived expertise of trees 
and tree cultivation (Table 1). The farmers identified as the most knowl-
edgeable about agroforestry (Group I) all had many ibiti 5 on their farms. 
However, the explanatory variables farm size (mentioned 105 times) and

--- Page 266 ---

270 
presence or absence of cattle (mentioned 109 times) were more often cited to 
characterize these farmers than species richness (mentioned 96 times). People 
with large farms have more land to plant trees, and can, therefore, have more 
trees in total and a greater species diversity. Similarly, cattle provide manure 
with which soil fertility can be maintained, and farmers in this group were 
identified as those having a sufficient (mentioned 18 times) to abundant 
(mentioned 42 times) manure supply. The ability to maintain soil fertility on 
at least part of the farm means that a harvest sufficient to feed the family 
(and perhaps some surplus) can be obtained from less land, thus freeing some 
land for the cultivation of tree crops. The most knowledgeable farmers were 
also identified as being richer than others, having woodlots, and continuing 
to plant more trees on their farms every year. Although few respondents 
mentioned where their fellow farmers had obtained their knowledge, for 
farmers in Group I 'self' was mentioned most frequently, meaning that these 
farmers were more likely to generate knowledge through their own trials and 
observation. This is also reflected in the statement that these farmers were 
actively engaged in searching for and obtaining planting materials. 
For Groups II and III, species richness was the principal distinguishing 
variable, more so than in the differentiation of Groups I and II. In Group I, 
farmers had between five and 14 tree species, in Group II farmers had between 
one and nine species, while the majority of farmers in Group III fell in the 
category 'less than four species'. Simultaneous with decreasing species 
diversity was a decrease in farm size in these three groups, pointing to a rela-
tionship between farm size and tree diversity. Animal ownership was about 
equal in each of these groups, even though the number of statements refer-
ring to absence of animals increased from seven in Group I to 17 in Group 
III. About one-quarter of the statements on species diversity for the farmers 
in Group III mentioned that these farmers had no trees other than those on 
the ruga (i.e., the home compound). This was an indication that farmers in 
this group were increasingly being squeezed for land for crops and eliminating 
trees -as a consequence. This was also supported by the observation made by 
the informants that these farmers need all their land for crops and therefore 
cannot cultivate trees. 
The least knowledgeable farmers (Groups IV and V) were characterized 
as having only trees on the rugo (particularly those in Group V), or, in addition 
to trees on the rugo, having one or two species in their fields. They have small 
farms, with none or only a few small animals and little to no manure. Lack 
of land was an often heard reason for not having trees or only having few of 
them, in spite of the recognition of the importance of trees. The ruga is 
therefore an important location for agroforestry, since this is the only place 
left for cultivating trees for the majority of farmers (for example, according 
to the 1989 Communal Development Plan, 54% of households in Maraba 
Commune have farms of less than 0.25 ha).

--- Page 267 ---

271 
Formal surveys: defining expertise from a scientific perspective 
The results of the socio-economic and tree surveys showed that the main 
ranking criteria used by the informants during the game (land, animals, species 
diversity) were significantly different between tree experts and comparison 
farmers. Because land is the major production factor in Rwandan agriculture 
(albeit one that is becoming increasingly limited for many farmers) and 
because having experience with a diversity of species was considered the most 
important aspect of 'being knowledgeable about agroforestry', only land and 
species diversity will be considered in the analysis presented here. For a 
complete review of factors important to differentiate tree experts and com-
parison farmers, see Den Biggelaar [1994]. 
Species diversity. A total of 152 ibiti were found, 31 of which were exotics. 
This diversity of species is remarkable as most farmers said that there were 
no or only few trees on the land when they started cultivating it. Indeed, when 
older people were asked to compare the current vegetation with that of their 
youth, all stated that there are many more trees now in and around the farm 
and in the general landscape than when they were young. The only trees they 
remember being present on the farm were those planted on the ruga and 
between the bananas immediately surrounding the ruga. All species presently 
found on the farm, therefore, were purposively planted and managed by the 
farmers in the last two to three decades. The reason for planting and main-
taining so many different tree species is to meet the diverse needs for tree 
products (fuelwood, poles, lumber, food, fruit, stakes, medicines). While wood 
needs could be satisfied with few species, for medical problems it is neces-
sary to plant many different species to treat the many diseases and parasites 
from which people and animals suffer. 
Diversity differences between experts and comparison farmers. The species 
inventory confirmed that tree experts have a significantly greater diversity 
(p < 0.01) of tree species on their farms (average 34 species) than compar-
ison farmers (average 12 species) (Table 2). In part, this can be attributed to 
the way tree experts were identified. During the ranking game, emphasis was 
given to tree diversity as a proxy for 'being knowledgeable about trees and 
tree cultivation'. While not known during the ranking game, later discussions 
in community and focus group meetings confirmed that knowledge is gained 
through experience, and that experience with many different tree species is 
more important in gaining expertise and knowledge than just having many 
trees of a few species. It also corresponds to the definition of knowledge by 
Kagame [1958] (having experience with many different objects or notions). 
While tree experts maintain a greater diversity of trees, the mean number 
of trees of each species on farms of tree experts and comparison farmers is 
about equal. High standard deviations in the number of trees per species are 
an indication of the variation in agroforestry systems between farms, and the

Farm size and tree density. The results of the socio-economic survey indi-
cated that tree experts (n = 44) have an average farm size of 1.27 ha, which 
is significantly different (at p < 0.01) from the size of comparison group farms 
(n = 70) of 0.18 ha (Table 2). While tree experts had more land, a greater 
variety of tree species and a greater total number of trees, comparison farmers 
had an average tree density more than twice that found on tree expert farms: 
1689 trees ha-1 (ranging from 1329 to 2564) compared to 731 trees ha-1 
(ranging from 636 to 1408). Thus, there is an inverse correlation between size 
of land holding and tree density, indicating different strategies of resource use 
and different pathways to intensification. As high tree densities require farmers 
to have higher levels of management skills and greater knowledge of the 
various components and their interactions, the nature and content of the agro-
forestry knowledge between tree experts and comparison farmers may be 
divergent. 
Conclusions 
Tree experts identified in this study all had much experience derived from 
cultivating a diversity of species, which translated into their being the most 
knowledgeable about trees and tree cultivation in the opinion of informants. 
However, being the most knowledgeable about a diversity of tree species may 
not necessarily mean that they are also the persons having the most knowl-
edge of managing these trees in highly integrated crop-tree systems. This study 
observed a differentiation in both agroforestry systems and agroforestry 
knowledge in the groups of tree experts and comparison farmers. 
Comparison farmers understood the need for and importance of trees. But 
as they had little land available, they needed a greater awareness of compo-
nent interactions, making their choice of species and species locations more 
critical. The smaller the farm, the more farmers in this group concentrated 
their efforts on fewer, less-competitive species, planted at a higher number 
of individuals of each species. This strategy resulted in very complex systems 
of low diversity, but with a high degree of integration of trees and crops within 
a small space. As agroforestry research concerns the study of the integration 
of and interaction between trees, crops and/or animals, future research on 
endogenous agroforestry systems should focus on the much more complex 
and integrated systems of the comparison farmer group to learn about farmers' 
knowledge about component interactions and the logic behind system designs. 
The results of this research indicate that endogenous agroforestry knowl-
edge is not homogenous but varies between individuals and groups of indi-
viduals, but with overlapping elements. These results confirm the observation 
by Scoones and Thompson [1994] that knowledge is manifold, discontinuous

--- Page 270 ---

274 
and dispersed, not singular, cohesive and systematized. Exclusive reliance 
on local definitions of 'knowledge' and 'being knowledgeable about agro-
forestry' for the identification of tree experts may, therefore, not provide 
satisfactory results. As a result of 'development' and other political, socio-
economic and demographic processes of change, the epistemological defini-
tion of knowledge defined by a society once largely dependent on gathering 
its tree products no longer appears applicable to the situation today in which 
people actively plant and manage trees on their farms. A more extensive kind 
of knowledge of trees is developing which includes knowledge about the 
planting and management of trees, their integration and interaction with crops 
and/or animals, as well as knowledge of their utility. The different strategies 
of agroforestry development farmers in this study pursued have resulted in 
multiple ways of knowing about trees and tree cultivation. To capture this 
diversity of agroforestry knowledge and agroforestry systems, it is recom-
mended that future studies of endogenous knowledge combine qualitative and 
quantitative, participatory and formal data collection methods seeking infor-
mation from both emic and etic perspectives, as they provide complementary 
and supplementary perspectives on a complex reality.

Historical development of agroforestry in China 
W. HSIUNG (XIONG), S. YANG and Q. TAO 
Department of Forestry, Nanjing Forestry University, Nanjing 210037, P.R. China 
Key words: agriculture, history, sericulture, slash-and-burn, small-farming economy 
Abstract. In China both agriculture and agroforestry originated in forests and developed side 
by side from their very beginnings. As revealed by archaeological evidence from ancient times, 
ancestral Chinese inhabited forests where they sheltered themselves from external hazards and 
lived on the edible parts of plants and animals through hunting and gathering activities. 
As early as the New Stone Age (7000-8000 years B.C.), fire was commonly used to burn 
the forests for slash-and-burn cultivation, which is a primitive form of agroforestry. Along with 
the rapid growth of population, the annexing of tribes, the collapse of clan society and the 
development of the slavery system, the nomadic mode of slash-and-burn farming evolved into 
settlement farming in the Xia Dynasty (2000-1600 B.C.). Peasants then engaged in settled 
cultivation. 
During the Shang and West Zhou Dynasty (1600-800 B.C.), perpetual settlement farming 
encouraged the development of private land-ownership. Peasants planted trees in or around the 
crop fields and grew fruit plants, vegetables and farmed domestic animals in their home yards 
for self-sufficiency. Since then, various forms of agroforestry have gradually developed and laid 
the fundamental framework of the Chinese small-farming economy for more than 3000 years. 
There has been a rapid growth of population in China over since the 1950s. At the same 
time, the area of arable land has decreased drastically and the environment has degraded rapidly 
as industrial development has taken place. The traditional labour-consuming and ineffective 
agroforestry management practices have not adapted to the current situation. In view of economic, 
ecological and social benefits, conversion of mono-biological production into a trinity system 
of agriculture, processing and marketing is suggested and planned experimentally. Such a 
management system, known as modern agroforestry, could be very beneficial to the develop-
ment of modern China's rural economy and environmental conservation. 
Introduction 
The term 'agroforestry' appeared in the 1970's and is now in wide use. 
However agriculture in China was initiated in forests and its original devel-
opment and practice has been closely related to and historically paralleled 
with forestry. Agroforestry may date back to the middle and late periods of 
the Old Stone Age. It has undergone embryonic and primitive states, through 
a traditional mode of evolution and then developed to the modern model of 
management by the mid 20th century [Agricultural Heritage Institute of China, 
1984; Tang, 1985; Yan and Yi, 1991]. The whole period of agroforestry 
development extends over several hundred thousand years. Based on the 
occurrence and development of agriculture in China, the present paper 
analyzes the composition, structure, function and efficiency of agroforestry in 
view of the historical background of China's social economy.

--- Page 273 ---

278 
Origin of agroforestry 
Forests are the largest terrestrial ecosystem on the earth with favourable 
environmental conditions and plentiful biological resources. They serve as a 
cradle of human civilization and a base for the birth of agriculture and forestry. 
Food and living conditions are essentials of human existence and they are also 
recognized as indicators of social and economic development. Stoneware, 
charcoal, ashes and animal bones dating from the Old Stone Age onwards 
have been found by archaeologists [Archaeological Institute, 1984; Du, 1982]. 
These show that Chinese ancestors had long in the past begun to live in forests 
in which they took shelter and protected themselves against possible attacks. 
They hunted birds and animals for meat and gathered the seeds and fruits of 
plants as crops. There was an extremely slow historical process of evolution 
from eating raw and living prey to using fire for heating and cooking that took 
a hundred thousand years to complete [Agricultural Heritage Institute of 
China, 1984; Du, 1982]. 
The Old Stone Age is the first turning point in the human history of 
evolution. The activities of the ancestral Chinese people at this time progressed 
gradually from simple instinct for survival to intelligent thinking as demon-
strated in the making of wooden and stone tools and the improvement of 
hunting and gathering. They gradually came to notice that some captured wild 
animals could be domesticated and that seeds falling into ashes from fires 
could germinate, grow, bloom and bear fruits and seeds. With the accumula-
tion of such experiences, the idea of raising animals and cultivating plant crops 
was conceived. Consequently, a primitive mode of agriculture took shape from 
these early practices [Agricultural Heritage Institute of China, 1984; Du, 1982; 
Tang, 1985; Zhang, 1992]. Therefore, it can be said that agriculture originated 
on forested land and was closely related to forestry activities at its very 
beginning. The earliest primitive farming society was thus formed in China. 
Primitive agroforestry 
Around 10,000 years ago, ancient China entered a new historical era - the 
New Stone Age. Ancestral Chinese practised primitive farming activities 
[Archaeological Institute, 1984; Zhu, 1988]. In ancient China people were 
always united by blood and kinsmanship and engaged collectively in all 
activities. Some wise men who made contributions to the lives of their people 
were supported as chiefs or head of families or tribes. In some history books 
there are legends about Sui Ren who drilled wood to make fire; Fu Xi who 
wove nets for catching fish and animals; You Chao who constructed shelters 
from trees; Shen Nong who taught his people to farm; Xuan Yuan who built 
wheels and carts and Lei Zu who raised silkworms [Agricultural Heritage 
Institute of China, 1984; Du, 1982]. All of them made efforts to bring the 
society forward at that time and were deservingly deified and respected as

--- Page 274 ---

279 
representatives of wisdom for ancestral Chinese. In particular, Shen Nong 
advocated the use of fire to bum down forests for hunting animals and to open 
up clearings for cultivation. He also made efforts to improve wooden and stone 
farming tools and to grow cereal crops in the ash-enriched soil of burnt patches 
in forested land. Such practices marked the beginning of slash-and-burn 
cultivation. Unburnt forested areas, on the other hand, became places for 
gathering of wild fruits, hunting and breeding of animals. This is the earliest 
form of agriculture, so to speak, and a primitive form of agroforestry. At the 
beginning of the New Stone Age, this form of agroforestry was merely 
supplementary to hunting and gathering as a means of livelihood [Tang, 1985; 
Yan and Yi, 1991; Zhu, 1988]. 
Slash-and-bumcultivation was a big step forward in human economic life. 
It indicated that Chinese ancestors were no longer entirely dependent on the 
provisions offered by nature. They added some supplies of natural products 
through their wisdom and labour. However, they knew nothing about farming 
techniques such as fertilization, ploughing, weeding and irrigation. The growth 
and yield of crops mainly depended on the fertility of forested land. The people 
burned down forests and used the ashes as fertilizers [Agricultural Heritage 
Institute of China, 1984; Deng, 1986; Yan and Yi, 1991; Zhang, 1992]. They 
sowed extensively in the hope of obtaining a bumper harvest. As a result of 
continued slash-and-burn farming, soil erosion became severe, aggressive 
weeds grew luxuriously, and yield decreased rapidly. They were forced to 
move to other forested areas to repeat the slash-and-burn process, deserting 
the existing previously-cultivated land. This is known as shifting cultivation 
[Sharma, 1976]. After an interval of years, as fertility recovered through 
vegetational succession, they came back and started the clearing and cultiva-
tion all over again. 
Slash-and-burn farming became popular in the New Stone Age. Its devel-
opment, continuation, and rotation varied from one area to another, mainly 
depending on population, cultural development and the amount of forested 
land. In those isolated areas with less population but more forest resources, 
slash-and-burn farming lasted for a much longer time in various forms. Even 
today this farming system remains quite popular in the very underdeveloped 
remote areas occupied by minority nationalities in southwestern China. Tribes 
using it include the Du Long, Nu, Xi Wa and Yao. They practise different 
forms of slash-and-burn and sow extensively in the hope of obtaining large 
harvests. After an interval of a certain number of years (3-4 or 7-8 years, 
depending on the fertility of the land) they move in groups from one place to 
another. That is why they are also known as nomadic tribes. Such a farming 
system involves extensive destruction of forest and land resources, and the 
yield obtained from crops is low. These people still live in severe poverty 
[Tang, 1985; Yan and Yi, 1991; Yu and Shen, 1983]. 
Slash-and-burn used in silvicultural management has had a history of more 
than one thousand years. It has been widely employed in site preparation for 
reforestation and afforestation of Chinese fir (Cunninghamia lanceolata) in

--- Page 275 ---

280 
southern China. Farmers slash down all ground cover including small trees 
and shrubs in summer, bum them in autumn, level up the ground to mix the 
ashes and soil in winter and then either plant seedlings or cuttings of fir trees 
with crops in spring or grow crops only for the first one or two years before 
tree planting. In newly established fir stands, intercropping is also practised 
until crown closure. Such a short phase of food crop production used as a 
silvicultural measure to ensure the establishment of timber stands is similar 
to the 'taungya' system developed for management of teak (Tectona grandis) 
stands in Burma in the mid 1800s [King, 1987]. After 25-30 years when the 
stand approaches maturity and the trees reach timber size, harvesting is con-
ducted. The stand is regenerated by sprouting from the stumps left behind. 
After another 25-30 years, the trees are again harvested and the stand is again 
vegetatively renewed. After the third rotation, the stand becomes much dete-
riorated and is no longer fit for vegetative regeneration. The harvested land 
is left deserted to the natural course of vegetational succession. After 40-50 
years when the fertility is naturally restored, the process of slashing, burning, 
site preparation, tree-planting and intercropping starts all over again. Today 
slash-and-burn is still a major part of the traditional silvicultural manage-
ment commonly practised in southern China [Sheng, 1983; Yu and Shen, 1983]. 
Traditional agroforestry 
In the latter part of the New Stone Age (2500-2100 B.C.), the population 
increased, tribes annexed, the clan society collapsed and the slavery system 
appeared. This enhanced drastically the reformation and development of 
farming practices. 
According to historical records, the first emperor of the Xia Dynasty (2000 
B.C.) divided his territory into nine regions, dredged rivers across the country, 
distinguished soil types and documented produce, so laying the foundations 
for agriculture [Agricultural Heritage Institute of China, 1984; Du, 1982]. In 
some areas, the practice of exploiting virgin land evolved into farming on 
cultivated but deserted land. Slash-and-burn was no longer the main farming 
system except in less populated, but abundantly forested and underdeveloped 
areas, while in hilly areas and plains with larger populations, forests were 
burned down on a massive scale to be converted into arable land for growing 
crops. Instead of nomadic farming, the slave owners allotted portions of their 
land to each slave they controlled. The slaves no longer moved to forested 
land for cultivation but settled down in one place where they engaged in 
farming activities permanently. The forests on poor hilly land were preserved 
as pasture land or as hunting sites. Sometimes luxuriant woodlots on culti-
vated land were also preserved as 'community woods' for social gatherings 
and ritual sacrifices, in addition to the supply and allocation of timber and 
firewood as described in the Analects of Confucius rYan and Yi, 1991; Zhang, 
1992].

--- Page 276 ---

281 
Settlement farming together with an increase in population in the Xia and 
Shan Dynasties (2000-1100 B.C.) became a motive force pushing agricultural 
development forward. By that time, people began to make and improve bronze 
and wooden agricultural implements, to use animal labour in farming, to grow 
different crops (such as rice, wheat, millet, sorghum, beans), to raise 
different domesticated animals (such as horse, cattle, sheep, chicken, pig, dog) 
and to cultivate fruit, vegetables, mulberry trees (Morus alba) and linen plants. 
There appeared the embryonic form of multiple management, a combination 
of farming', silviculture, animal husbandry and sericulture that laid the 
economic foundation of a small farming system for self-sufficiency. Gathering 
wild fruits and hunting wild animals became much less important activities 
and descended to a subordinate position [Agricultural Heritage Institute of 
China, 1984; Du, 1982; Tang, 1985]. 
During the West Zhou Dynasty (1100-700 B.C.), the slavery system 
gradually collapsed. A land-tenancy system, the 'nine squares', was widely 
adopted across the country particularly on the North China Plain as described 
by Mencius (400 B.C.) [Yan and Yi, 1991; Zhang, 1992]. A large square area 
of land was equally divided into nine small areas, of which the eight on the 
edges were allotted to serfs, while the central one belonged to the landlord 
and was"managed by the eight serfs for him. The private ownership of land 
became gradually established and resulted in further development of small 
self sufficiency systems. With one serf family as a production unit, animal 
husbandry, sericulture and domestic handicrafts (such as brewing, silk reeling 
and weaving) were managed and developed in conjunction with food pro-
duction for self-sufficiency. This was a major form of rural economy known 
as 'man-farming and woman-weaving' in old China [Tang, 1985; Wang, 1964]. 
Besides this, trees for timber and fruit were planted around farm houses as 
depicted in the Book of Songs (800 B.C.): 'trespass not on my lane, break 
not my willow. trees; leap not over my fence, pick not my mulberry leaves; 
intrude not my garden, cut not my sandalwood trees'. Growing melons and 
vegetables, as well as raising domesticated animals, were also included in this 
comprehensive homestead management [Zhang, 1992]. 
During the period of Spring-Autumn (770-481 B.C.) and the War-States 
(490-220 B.C.), a comprehensive management system consisting of farming, 
silviculture, animal husbandry and fishery activity had further developed. 
'To grow different cereals, food supply is assured, to plant mulberry trees 
and hemp and to rear domesticated animals, then farmers are affluent' (in 
Guan Zi, 400 B.C.). As an agricultural policy during these periods, compre-
hensive production for mUltiple purposes was advocated and encouraged as 
an effective way to prosperity. 'Those who can plant trees and raise silkworms 
are awarded with ounces of gold and hectolitres of grain' [Yan and Yi, 1991; 
Zhang, 1992]. Mencius (400 B.C.) highly praised the small farming system 
of multiple management and said 'if a family owns a certain area of land 
with mulberry trees planted around its house for breeding silkworms, 
domesticated animals raised in its yard for meat, and crop fields cultivated

--- Page 277 ---

282 
and managed properly for cereals, it will be prosperous and will not suffer 
from starvation' [Tang, 1985; Yan and Yi, 1991]. Meanwhile, forests and water 
resources were well protected and properly exploited. 'If the forests are timely 
felled, then an abundant supply of timber and fire wood is ensured, if the 
fishing net with relatively big holes is timely cast into the pond, then there 
is not shortage of fish and turtle for use' (Mencius) [Zhang, 1992]. This 
presents a very prosperous picture of the rural economy of multiple manage-
ment and ,a vivid sketch of pastoral peace. 
History next witnessed the unification and fall of the Qing Dynasty 
(221-207 B.C.) and the warring decade of the Chu and Han States (210-205 
B.C.). By the apex of the Han Dynasty (about 100 B.C.), social security was 
stable and the total population grew steadily up to 40 million. Experience of 
agroforestry accumulated from the past was collated, revised and widely 
extended to serve as a guideline for agricultural policy making. As stated in 
Huai Nan Zi (about 100 B.C.): 'Ever since ancient times, those who want to 
become masters of their people should make the best use of time and land 
plus human labour for production. Consequently, crops grow flourishingly, 
domesticated animals are all thriving, trees are timely planted, farming fields 
are regularly worked on. Mulberry trees and hemp are grown on fertile soil, 
while on hilly and steep mountain slopes unsuitable for growing crops, trees 
are planted for timber and firewood. Therefore, different sites are properly 
used for appropriate production' [Agricultural Heritage Institute of China, 
1984; Tang, 1985; Yan and Yi, 1991; Zhang, 1992]. Such timely and well 
arranged agroforestry activities resulted in enormous economic benefits. 
Si Maqian (150 B.C.), a great historian in the Westen Han Dynasty, gave 
a detailed description of the growing of fruit trees, timber trees and bamboos 
in his book, Historic Records. Tree selection and management became a 
constituent part of agricultural activities at that time [Zhang, 1992]. 
With the long history of China's order and disorder, the development of 
agriculture also experienced a course of rise and fall. Changes of governments 
as well as dynasties were always accompanied by wars and confusion. 
Production was ruined and people suffered from starvation and homeless-
ness. Afterwards when the political situation become steady, agriculture 
developed again and prospered. After the Han Dynasty (206 B.C.-220 A.D.), 
political security lasted for relatively long periods in such dynasties as the 
Jin (265-420), Tang (618-907), Song (960-1279), Yuan (1234-1368), Ming 
(1368-1661) and Qing (1661-1911). In each of these periods some advances 
were made in agricultural management and techniques. Double and triple 
interplanting and combination of crops and other products varied greatly, 
particularly in the Ming and Qiun Dynasties [Agricultural Heritage Institute 
of China, 1984; Du, 1982; Tang, 1985]. According to the analysis of Wang 
Xue Hu [1964], there exist 497 old agriculture books in China, of which 283 
appeared in the Ming and Qing Dynasties, accounting for 57% of the total. 
There are 162 books related the management of forests, animal husbandry and

--- Page 278 ---

283 
fisheries which includes mulberry, tea, fruit, vegetables, flowers, silkworm 
and bees. This number makes up 32.6% of the total [Wang, 1964]. 
The development of sericulture merits special mention. Cocoons, tough silk 
pieces and spindles unearthed in 1921 from the relics of the Yang Shao and 
Long Shan Cultures (2300-2000 B.C.) and Chinese characters such as 
mulberry, silkworm and silk recognized on the inscriptions uncovered from 
the relics of the late Shang Dynasty (1200-1100 B.C.) show clearly that China 
has a very long history of mulberry planting, silkworm rearing and silk reeling 
and weaving. First started in the Yellow River areas and later spread south-
ward to the Yangtze River regions, sericulture has developed steadily. Its 
economic significance is second only to the growth of cereal crops and is more 
important than animal husbandry. The laying of emphasis both on farming 
and sericulture has become an important guideline for making agricultural 
policies in different dynasties. In fact, sericulture is the earliest and most 
typical example of agroforestry in China. The Book of Songs (800 B.C.) 
mentions the techniques of pruning mulberry trees and collecting mulberry 
leaves for feeding silkworms [Zhang, 1992]. In the Fan Book of Agriculture 
(about 100 B.C.), there is an account of interplanting systems of mulberry 
with millet and hemp, using the excrement of silkworms as fertilizer [Du, 
1982; Tang, 1985; Zhang, 1992]. Through continuous efforts and improve-
ment by farmers of different times, sericulture together with animal husbandry 
or fisheries has become the major form of 'side-line'l production in the self-
sufficient rural economy. The mulberry plantation is intergrown with crops 
and other plants. The hatching and rearing of silkworms is repeated several 
times a year. At the same time handicraft industries such as silk reeling, 
spinning and weaving have also been rapidly developed, marking the way to 
the production of an economic commodity. Early in 200 B.C. silk from China 
made its way to mid and west Asia and Europe along a route that became 
well-known as the 'Silk Road' and in China as 'Seres' [Agricultural Heritage 
Institute of China, 1984; Du, 1982; Yan and Yi, 1991; Zhang, 1992]. In the 
tenth century, crop fields dotted with mulberry patches and fishing ponds 
surrounded by mulberry fields became very popular in the Yangtze River 
region and the delta of the Pearl River where crops, sericulture, fisheries and 
animal husbandry were comprehensively managed [Mei, 1983]. Since silk is 
the major raw material for the textile industry, sericulture remains the major 
form of 'side-line' production in China. 
The Ming and Qing Dynasties (14-19th centuries) witnessed the marked 
development of traditional agroforestry in China. The proliferation of various 
matching and combination modes of farming, silviculture, animal husbandry, 
fisheries and 'side-line' production was unprecedented. In the early half of 
the 20th century, however, both agriculture and agroforestry remained stagnant 
due to social disorder and upheaval. After the founding of New China in 1949 
development resumed. 
On a rough estimation, there exist in China more than 50 main types

--- Page 279 ---

284 
of traditional agroforestry. They can conveniently be grouped into five 
categories: 
1. Agroforestry with crops dominant. This model is popular in the vast 
agricultural plains where trees are planted as individuals, or in clusters, 
patches, strips and belts in crop fields. In provinces such as Shangdon, 
Anhui and Henan, there are crop-Paulownia Jortunei, crop-date (Ziziphus 
jujuba) and crop-persimmon (Diospyros kaki) intergrowing forms, while 
in the north, northwest and east of China, plantations such as '3-N'2 shelter 
belts and forest networks have been built in the cultivated fields or along 
the sea coasts. 
2. Agroforestry with forest dominant. In naturally or artificially forested areas 
such as timber stands, orchards, special tree plantations, watershed forests, 
scenic forests (such as forest parks, historical relics, temples) and con-
servation forests, various crops (cereals, vegetables, cash crops, medic-
inal plants and forages) are intergrown so as to enable multiple use of the 
stands without disturbance of their initial condition. They include ginseng 
(Panax ginseng) under forest in the northeast, tung-oil trees (Vernicia 
Jordii), mountain-spice trees (Litsea cubeba), and gold threads (Coptis 
chinensis) in Chinese fir plantations, mushrooms in bamboo stands, tea 
under pine forests, coffee and pepper in rubber plantations, medicinal 
Morinda (Morinda officina/is), and obscured Homalomena (Hamalomena 
occulta) and villous Amomum (Amomum villosum) under tropical forests. 
Moreover, the undergrowth of forests is also utilised to raise poultry and 
other domesticated animals as well as wildlife, such as deer in the north-
west forests, musk deer in the enclosed forest stands in Sichuan, sheep or 
goats in fir stands and chickens in bamboo plantations in Jiangsu. 
3. Agroforestry with pasture-husbandry dominant. Trees and shrubs are 
planted in clusters or belts on grazing land to provide protection for herds 
and to ensure herbage production, as commonly seen in Inner Mongolia 
and the sparse forest areas in the south. 
4. Agroforestry with fisheries dominant. Trees are planted along rivers, lakes, 
reservoirs and fish ponds not only for protecting dams against waves and 
preventing soil erosion, but also for helping the fisheries business. In the 
delta areas of the Yangtze and Pearl Rivers, fish ponds are usually sur-
rounded by mulberry trees, fruit trees, tea plants, sugar cane and other 
plants. Fish are even raised in rice fields in some areas. Such practices 
have a long history and continue to develop. 
5. Homestead and 'four-side'3 management. Timber trees, fruit trees, veg-
etables and sometimes aquatic crops are grown and poultry, animals and 
fish are raised on a small scale around farm houses, near villages, as well 
as on road and stream sides so as to make the best use of land and water 
resources. Besides self-sufficiency, some products can be marketed as a 
part of the major income for families. Though their combination varies

--- Page 280 ---

285 
greatly from place to place, such practices are very common in rural areas 
all over the country. 
During the past several thousand years, agroforestry systems with crop 
production dominant in a combination of silvo-pastoral-fishery and 'side-line' 
activities was gradually formed and then strongly developed in the closed rural 
society of China where it has played an important role in the development of 
the self-sufficient economy. Such a system, though meeting self-sufficiency 
requirements, is small in production scale and low in output for the amount 
of labour required for its management in a market economy. The rapid growth 
of population, decrease in arable land, development of industry and deterio-
ration of environment, particularly under the pressure of economic reform and 
open-door policies, have all conspired to poise this small farming system on 
the verge of collapse. 
Modern agroforestry 
In recent years Chinese .scientists have become more concerned with the 
present status of agricultural systems in relation to the Chinese rural economy 
and social background and have put forward concepts such as 'large-scale 
agriculture', 'stereo (vertical) farming', 'comprehensive farming', 'complex 
farming' and 'ecological agriculture' as well as appropriate technical measures 
in the hope of reforming the production structure for better use of water and 
land resources, increasing the income of farmers and improving the rural 
economy [Deng, 1986; Hu, 1988; Yang, 1987]. Their endeavours have resulted 
in some advances in the development of modern agroforestry. 
As a comprehensive management system agroforestry should include 
farming, silviculture, animal husbandry, fisheries, 'side-line' production, pro-
cessing and marketing which are organized and operated in accordance with 
the modern market economy, theories, methods and techniques of system 
ecology and ecosystem engineering. Only in this way can natural resources 
(land, climate, and water) and social resources (labour, science and tech-
nology) be fully utilized and maintained so as to achieve large and sustained 
economic, ecological and social benefits. Therefore, modern agroforestry is 
based on biological production from which economic growth can be achieved 
through the processing industry and the marketing system, as shown below 
[Hsiung, 1991]. 
biological production I 
\ 
crops and trees 
livestock and 
aquatic products 
~ 
processing ---.. marketing 
~

--- Page 281 ---

286 
In this trinity system, biological production is achieved through material 
and energy transformation by crops, trees, animals and micro-organisms. The 
higher the rate of transformation, then the greater is the economic efficiency. 
Therefore, the proportion of the production of silviculture, animal husbandry, 
fisheries and 'side-lines' should be increased. As regards the growth of crops, 
the traditional duality of cereal-economic crops should be gradually changed 
into the trinity of cereal-forage economic crops so as to facilitate the devel-
opment of animal husbandry. 
A village or rural area is an agroecosystem which can be considered as a 
unit for the planning and design of modem agroforestry. Its natural condi-
tions, socio-economic background, material supply, species diversity and 
technical level are important and should be carefully investigated and evalu-
ated prior to design. In addition, attention should be paid to the proper 
protection and exploitation of natural resources. 
Based on the conceptual framework mentioned above, model agroforestry 
systems can be transferred into tangible production systems with reference 
of the principles and methodology of ecosystem engineering [Hsiung and Zou, 
1985; Hsiung, 1991; Qian, 1988; Zhu, 1988]. Trees, crops, domesticated 
animals, aquatic species and their intermediate processes should be correctly 
selected in view of their economic value and biological characteristics. These 
related components should be purposely integrated into a production system 
in an appropriate time sequence, spatial arrangement and numerical propor-
tion so that they can fully perform their biological production role and create 
favourable conditions for successive components. According to the category 
and quantity of biological products (yields), processing systems and marketing 
systems can be correspondingly established. Thus, a whole rural economic 
system which combines agriculture, industry and trade as a trinity is formed. 
This is what is meant by modem agroforestry in China [Hsiung, 1991]. 
Experiments and practical trials have been conducted in recent years on 
variously labelled large-scale agriculture, comprehensive farming or ecolog-
ical agriculture. Planning and design concerning watercourses, fields, forests, 
roads and village locations have been carried out with reference to local 
conditions. The production structure has been modified and organised to 
specialise in certain items such as cereal crops, cotton, chickens, ducks, pigs, 
cattle and aquatic products including their primary processing and marketing. 
Farmers operate them on a relatively large scale either as families or as respon-
sible collectives and achieve remarkable economic profits. These integrated 
production systems belong to the category of modem agroforestry which, 
through persistent development and replenishment, are predicted to become 
an entirely new management system of the rural economy in China.

--- Page 282 ---

Notes 
1. 'Side-line' refers to secondary productive activities such as silk-worm breeding, bee-keeping 
or poultry husbandry which complement the primary activity of crop production. 
2. The '3-N' shelterbelt is a vast shelterbelt system which has gradually been established since 
the late 1970's in the North-East, North and North-West of China. 
3. 'Four-side' management refers to the productive use of road-sides, river-sides, village-sides 
and crop field sides.
